Can One Soul Manage Two Bodies? A Rational Exploration
Argument for the possibility of one soul managing many bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for the possibility of a single soul managing multiple bodies, including the analogical and teleological reasoning.

Core Idea
One soul can manage many bodies because its attachment is purely managerial, not intrinsic.

In this passage, the author tackles a fascinating speculative question: could a single soul simultaneously manage two bodies? He presents an argument based on the soul's role as a manager and ruler, and then strengthens it with a teleological consideration about the soul's desire for physical pleasures. Let's explore his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without breaking it down. Notice the double question and the author's remark that these investigations are obscure.
Pay attention to the phrase 'اعلم : أن هذه المباحث غامضة' – it sets the tone of cautious inquiry.
2
Identify the core argument
The author argues that one soul attaching to many bodies is not impossible. Find the reason: the soul's attachment to the body is only by way of management (تصرف) and administration (تدبير), just as one ruler can govern two kingdoms.
This is an analogy: ruler→kingdoms :: soul→bodies.
3
Examine the teleological reinforcement
The author adds that the soul loves the body because it uses bodily instruments to obtain physical pleasures. Using two bodies would serve this purpose more strongly, so attaching to many bodies might even be obligatory, or at least possible as a rarity.
This shifts from possibility to desirability, but the conclusion remains possibility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage does not settle the matter definitively but argues that one soul for many bodies is not impossible and at least possible in a rare manner. Consider how this argument fits into broader theological debates about the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني عشر في أنه هل يعقل وجود نفس واحدة تكون متصرفة في بحنين موجود نفسين يكونان متصرفتين في بحن واحد؟ اعلم : أن هذه المباحث غامضة. ولقائل أن يقول : أما تعلق النفس الواحدة بالأبدان الكثيرة ، يجب أن لا يكون ممتنعاً . وذلك لأن تعلق النفس بالبدن ، ليس إلا على سبيل التصرف والتدبير ، وكون الشيء الواحد متصرفاً في مملكتين وفي بيتين غير ممتنع ، فوجب أن لا يمتنع ذلك . والذي يزيده تقريراً : أن السبب لعشق النفس على التعلق بالبدن : هو أن تستعمل هذه الآلات البدنية في تحصيل اللذات الجسمانية ، والراحات البدنية . ومعلوم أن استعمال بدنين أكثر وأقوى في إفادة هذا المعنى ، فوجب أن يكون تعلق النفس بالأبدان الكثيرة واجباً . فإن لم يجب ذلك ، فلا أقل من الإمكان على سبيل الندرة
The twelfth chapter on whether it is conceivable for a single soul to exist that manages two bodies, for two souls to exist that manage a single body? Know: that these investigations are obscure. Someone may say: As for the attachment of one soul to many bodies, it must not be impossible. That is because the attachment of the soul to the body is only by way of management and administration, and it is not impossible for a single thing to be a manager in two kingdoms and two houses, so it must not be impossible. And what increases it in clarity: that the cause for the soul's love of attachment to the body is to use these bodily instruments to obtain physical pleasures and bodily comforts. And it is known that using two bodies is greater and stronger in providing this meaning, so it must be that the soul's attachment to many bodies is obligatory. If that is not obligatory, then at least it is possible in a rare manner.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage opens the twelfth chapter with a double question: can one soul manage two bodies, or can two souls manage one body? The author states that these investigations are obscure. Then he presents an argument for the first possibility: one soul attached to many bodies is not impossible. The rationale is that the soul's relation to the body is purely one of management and administration, analogous to a single ruler governing two kingdoms or two houses. Since that is not impossible, neither is one soul managing multiple bodies. He strengthens this with a teleological argument: the soul's attachment to the body aims at using bodily instruments for physical pleasures and comforts. Using two bodies would serve this purpose more effectively, so it might even be obligatory; if not obligatory, then at least possible as a rare occurrence.

Key Terms
النفس
البدن
التصرف
التدبير
اللذات الجسمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is inherently and inseparably tied to a single body.
Correction: The author argues that the soul's attachment is only managerial, not intrinsic, so one soul managing multiple bodies is not impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the author gives for the possibility of one soul managing multiple bodies?
A) The soul is immortal and can be in many places at once.
B) The soul's attachment to the body is only managerial, like a ruler over multiple domains.
C) The soul desires physical pleasures which are better achieved with more bodies.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The soul's attachment to the body is managerial, so one soul managing multiple bodies is at least possible, if not obligatory for pleasure-seeking.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul's attachment to your body is merely for management and administration, what does that mean for how you treat your body? Are you a good manager, or do you let your desires rule you?
?
The source says the soul loves the body because it seeks physical pleasures. What does this reveal about your own spiritual state? Which drives you more: the pursuit of bodily pleasures or the longing for something beyond this world?
?
If one soul can manage two bodies, then your identity is not tied to your physical form. Who are you truly? How would your life change if you lived with the certainty that you are first and foremost a soul, not a body?

The First Argument for Multiple Souls in One Body: Similarity of Essences
Sanusi presents the first logical proof for multiple souls sharing one body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if many souls are identical in essence and similar in attributes, then they must all be attached to one body.

Core Idea
Identical souls cannot be prioritized—all must attach to one body.

This lesson examines the first of several possible arguments for the possibility of multiple souls inhabiting a single body. The argument hinges on the idea that if souls are perfectly alike in their essence and qualities, then there is no reason for only some to be attached—they all must be. Let's read Sanusi's concise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the argument without analysis.
Notice that the author presents this as one of several 'وجوه' (aspects).
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Find the conditional premise (if souls are identical in essence and similar in qualities) and the conclusion (all souls must attach to the body).
The conclusion is stated with 'فوجب' (so it is necessary).
3
Reflect on the logical necessity
Consider why perfect similarity forces all souls to attach. What would happen if souls were not identical?
This shows the hypothetical nature of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلق النفوس الكثيرة بالبدن الواحد فهذا [ أيضا (۱) ] محتمل من وجوه : الأول : هو أن النفوس الكثيرة إذا كانت متماثلة في تمام الماهية ، وكانت متشابهة في الأخلاق والصفات ، فحينئذ تكون متشاركة في جميع الأمور التي لأجلها تعلقت بذلك البدن ، فلم يكن تعلق بعضها ،به ، أولى من تعلق الباقي به ، فوجب تعلقها بأسرها بذلك البدن
As for the attachment of many souls to one body, this is also possible in several respects. The first: that many souls, if they are identical in the entirety of their essence and similar in moral characters and qualities, then they share in all matters on account of which they are attached to that body, so the attachment of some of them to it is not more fitting than the attachment of the remainder to it, so their attachment altogether to that body is necessary.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the first of several possible ways (وجوه) for multiple souls to be attached to a single body. The argument is based on the premise that souls are identical in essence and similar in attributes. If they are alike in all respects, then there is no reason for one soul rather than another to be attached to a particular body. Since they are equal in the grounds for attachment, all of them must be attached to that same body. This is a logical necessity given the premise of perfect similarity.

Key Terms
النفوس الكثيرة
البدن الواحد
الماهية
الأخلاق
الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is taken as a factual claim that souls are identical in essence.
Correction: The argument is a hypothetical exploration: it asks what would follow if souls were perfectly identical, not that they actually are.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what condition would make multiple souls necessarily attach to one body?
A) They are created at the same time.
B) They are identical in essence and similar in qualities.
C) They all desire the same pleasures.
D) They are from the same spiritual realm.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for multiple souls in one body relies on the premise of perfect similarity: if souls are identical, they all must attach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul were identical to another in essence, would your deeds still be uniquely yours? How does the uniqueness of your soul press on your personal accountability before Allah?
?
What would it mean for you if your soul could be replaced by another identical soul? Would your relationship with Allah change?
?
In a world that often tries to make people feel generic and replaceable, how does this reflection on the uniqueness of your soul compel you to live with purpose and gratitude to the One who created you distinct?

Second Argument: Detached Souls Attaching to New Bodies
Second argument for multiple souls: detached souls attach to similar new bodies as helpers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how detached souls can attach to a new body as helpers, based on similarity and longing.

Core Idea
Detached souls can become helpers in a new body if it resembles their former one.

After establishing the first argument, the author presents a second, more detailed scenario. This argument does not require multiple souls to be inherent to the body from the start; instead, it suggests that souls from past lives can attach themselves to a new body that closely resembles their previous one. Their attachment serves a supportive role, influencing the person's moral conduct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and the literal translation provided. Let the author's own reasoning come through before you analyse it.
2
Identify the conditions for attachment
The argument lists three conditions: equality of souls in substance and attributes, great similarity of the present body to the bodies of the detached souls, and the longing of those souls for their perished bodies. Find these in the text.
3
Understand the outcome and classification
When the present body comes into existence, the detached souls attach and become helpers. Their nature determines whether they become angels of earth (good) or devils (evil). Reflect on how this supports the idea of multiple souls in one body.
This demonstrates a cooperative rather than conflictual multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنه لا يمتنع أن يقال : إن طائفة من النفوس المفارقة لأبدانها ، تكون مساوية لهذه النفوس الحاضرة ، مساواة في الجوهر وفي الصفات ، وكان هذا البدن الحاضر عظيم المشابهة بأبدان تلك النفوس المفارقة ، وكانت تلك النفوس المفارقة مشتاقة إلى تلك الأبدان المنقوصة (۱) المنقضية ، فإذا دخل هذا البدن في الوجود ، تعلقت تلك النفوس المفارقة بهذا البدن ، ضرباً من التعلق ، وصارت معينة لهذه النفوس القائمة في الحال ، على أحوالها وصفاتها ومهماتها . فإن كانت خيرة كانت تلك النفوس أعواناً للخير ، وهو المراد بملائكة الأرض ، وإن كانت شريرة كانت تلك النفوس أعواناً للشر ، وهو المراد بالشياطين ، الذين هم الأرواح الخبيثة الأرضية
The second: that it is not impossible to say that a group of souls that have separated from their bodies are equal to these present souls, equal in substance and attributes, and that this present body is greatly similar to the bodies of those separated souls, and those separated souls were longing for those imperfect, perished bodies. So when this body comes into existence, those separated souls attach to this body in some kind of attachment, and they become helpers for these present souls in their states, qualities, and tasks. If they are good, those souls become helpers of good, which is what is meant by the angels of the earth; and if they are evil, those souls become helpers of evil, which is what is meant by the devils, who are the evil earthly spirits.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for multiple souls in one body. It posits that souls that have left their bodies (النفوس المفارقة) and that are equal in substance and attributes to the current soul, and whose former bodies closely resemble the present body, may become attached to this new body due to their longing for their own perished bodies. This attachment is of a helping nature: good souls become 'angels of the earth' assisting in good, evil souls become 'devils' assisting in evil. Thus, multiple souls can be involved with one body in a supportive role.

Key Terms
النفوس المفارقة
النفوس الحاضرة
ملائكة الأرض
الشياطين
الأرواح الخبيثة الأرضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument claims that detached souls attach to all new bodies universally.
Correction: The argument only applies when the new body is greatly similar to the bodies of the detached souls and they are equal in substance. It is a theoretical scenario, not a universal claim.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the detached souls that become 'angels of the earth' from those that become 'devils'?
A) The type of body they attach to.
B) Their own nature being good or evil.
C) The time they attach.
D) The place where the body exists.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that detached souls can attach to a new body as helpers if they are equal in substance and the body resembles their former one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument describes detached souls longing for their former bodies and attaching to yours as helpers. What kinds of spiritual helpers are you attracting through your current character and actions? Are they from the angels of the earth or from the devils?
?
Consider the longing that drives these detached souls. Is there any attachment in your own heart to something from your past that might be drawing harmful spiritual influences into your life? What will you consciously detach from today?
?
If your own soul were to become detached, what kind of helper would it become based on your present nature? What one specific change can you make this week to ensure it would be a helper of good?

The Soul's Nature and Its Instrumental Relation to the Body
Sanusi lays out the necessary premise for the soul's managerial relation to the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the necessary premise for discussions about multiple souls in one body or one soul in multiple bodies: the soul is a simple substance that only manages the body as a tool for obtaining pleasures.

Core Idea
The soul uses the body like a tool for pleasure, not as an essential part of itself.

The author begins by briefly recalling the third division of souls, which involves two souls in one body or one soul in many bodies. However, he immediately points out a crucial premise that such investigations rely on: the soul must be understood as a non-corporeal substance with only a managerial relationship to the body. He then elaborates on this premise, explaining that the soul's motivation for managing the body is to use its organs to attain physical pleasures and desires. Finally, he illustrates this relationship with a powerful analogy: the soul's attachment to the body is like a craftsman's attachment to a tool – valuable only for its utility, and discardable if a better tool appears.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the third division
The passage opens with a mention of the third division (two souls in one body, one soul in many bodies). Observe that the author says this was already discussed in earlier chapters, but he uses it to introduce the necessary premise.
This sets the context for the rest of the lesson.
2
Identify the necessary premise
The author states that these investigations only apply to those who define the soul as a simple, self-subsistent substance with no attachment to the body except through management and administration. This is the key premise.
3
Understand the soul's motivation
The passage explains that the soul's only motivation for managing the body is to use its organs to obtain physical pleasures, bodily comforts, and psychological desires. This is a crucial point about the soul's purpose.
4
Grasp the analogy of the instrument
The author compares the soul's relationship to the body to that of a craftsman who loves a tool only for the work it enables. If a better tool appears, he abandons the old one. This shows the soul is not essentially tied to a specific body.
This analogy supports the idea that the soul is distinct from the body and can potentially be associated with different bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن يحصل نفسان في بدن واحد . وتحصل نفس في أبدان كثيرة . فقد ذكرنا كيفية تقريره في الفصول السالفة . واعلم : أن هذه المباحث إنما تتوجه على من يقول : النفس جوهر مجرد قائم بذاته (۲) لا تعلق لها بذلك البدن ، إلا على سبيل التصرف والتدبير . ومن المباحث اللائقة بهذا الباب : أن يقال : إذا قلنا : إن النفس جوهر ليس بجسم ولا بجسماني ، ولا تعلق لها بهذا البدن ، إلا تعلق التصرف والتدبير . مثل تصرف الملك في البلدة، والمالك في البيت . وثبت أيضاً : أنه لا داعي للنفس على التصرف في هذا البدن ، إلا لأجل أن تستعمل هذه الأعضاء وهذه الآلات ، في تحصيل اللذات الجسمانية ، والراحات البدنية ، والشهوات النفسانية . فحينئذ جرى تعلق هذه النفس بهذا البدن ، مجرى من أراد أن يعمل عملاً ، ولا يمكنه الإتيان بذلك العمل إلا بواسطة آلة مخصوصة . فهو يجب تلك الآلة ، ليتوصل بها إلى تحصيل ذلك العمل ، إلا أنه لا تكون محبته مقصورة على تلك الآلة بعينها ، بل إذا وجدت هذه الآلة صحيحة صالحة لذلك المقصود ، استعملها [ فإن رأى آلة أصلح من الأولى ، ترك الأولى واستعمل
As for the third division: that there occur two souls in one body, and one soul occurs in many bodies. We have already mentioned how to establish it in the previous chapters. And know: that these investigations only apply to those who say: the soul is a simple substance, subsisting by itself, having no attachment to that body except by way of management and administration. And among the investigations befitting this chapter is that it be said: If we say that the soul is a substance that is neither a body nor corporeal, and that it has no attachment to this body except the attachment of management and administration—like the management of the king in the city and the owner in the house—and it is also established that there is no motivation for the soul to manage this body except in order to use these organs and instruments to obtain physical pleasures, bodily comforts, and psychological desires. Then the attachment of this soul to this body becomes like that of someone who wants to perform an action and cannot do that action except by means of a specific instrument. He loves that instrument in order to achieve that action, but his love is not confined to that particular instrument; rather, if he finds this instrument sound and suitable for that purpose, he uses it; [if he sees an instrument better than the first, he leaves the first and uses]
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage first briefly mentions the third division (two souls in one body and one soul in many bodies) and notes it was covered earlier. Then it addresses the necessary premise for all these discussions: they only hold for those who define the soul as a simple self-subsistent substance with only a managerial relation to the body. The author elaborates by describing the soul's nature: it is not a body or corporeal, it manages the body like a king governs a city. The soul's motivation for managing the body is to use its organs to obtain physical pleasures and psychological desires. Therefore, the soul's attachment to the body is like a craftsman's attachment to a tool: he loves the tool for the work it does, but if a better tool appears, he will switch. This analogy supports the idea that the soul is not inherently tied to one specific body.

Key Terms
النفس جوهر مجرد
التصرف والتدبير
الآلات البدنية
اللذات الجسمانية
الشهوات النفسانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is essentially and inseparably connected to a particular body.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the soul has only a managerial relationship with the body, like a craftsman and his tool, and is not essentially tied to it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the soul's motivation for managing the body?
A) To gain knowledge and wisdom.
B) To use bodily organs to obtain physical pleasures and desires.
C) To fulfill a divine command without personal gain.
D) To maintain the body for eventual resurrection.

Key Takeaway
The soul relates to the body as a craftsman to his tool, loving it only for its utility in obtaining pleasures, implying the soul is not essentially tied to a specific body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you using your body as a mere tool to chase temporary pleasures, or have you forgotten that you are the manager, not the machine? When was the last time you deliberately denied a physical desire to strengthen your soul's authority over your body?
?
If you were to find a 'better instrument' tomorrow—a healthier, more capable body—would you be willing to abandon your current habits and attachments? What does your answer reveal about whether you truly love your body as a tool or have made it into an idol?
?
The soul uses the body like a craftsman uses a tool, yet you will one day leave this tool behind. What are you doing today to ensure that your soul's work—not your physical comforts—will endure when the tool is discarded?

First General Argument: Sense Specialization Refutes Soul's Perception of Particulars
First general argument against soul perceiving particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the innate specialization of the senses serves as a general argument against the soul directly perceiving particulars, and how the objection from instrumental use of organs is answered.

Core Idea
Innate sense specializations prove the soul does not directly perceive particulars.

This lesson examines the first of four general arguments that the soul does not perceive particulars. The argument appeals to our innate knowledge that each sense has a specific function. If the soul were the direct perceiver, these specializations would be meaningless. We will see how Avicenna's view is challenged and how an objection involving the soul's attention to organs is refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the passage starts by stating the soul's capacity and then presents Avicenna's view that the soul only perceives universals.
Look for the phrase 'النفس الناطقة لا تقوى إلا على إدراك الكليات والمجردات'.
2
Understand the appeal to innate specialization
The first general argument uses 'بديهة' (innate knowledge) that vision is in the eye, hearing in the ear, etc. If the soul were the perceiver, these specializations would be invalid.
The argument is: we know directly that the tongue tastes and the eye sees. If the soul did it, why have different organs?
3
Analyze the objection and the answer
The objection: the soul uses organs as instruments by turning attention to them. The answer: if the soul turns to the tongue, does the tongue taste? If yes, then the soul is not the perceiver; if no, then the tongue is like any other organ, invalidating specialization.
This refutation shows that the objection cannot explain the observed specialization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث سكر في المباشرة. بيان أن النفس الداخلة [؟] معركة الكليات والجينيات معا. وأتحاميل [؟] لجميع الإتصال بنفسها، وإن كانت بالكرة موقوفة على استعمال الكليات. ظاهر كلام الشيخ الرئيس مشعر بأن النفس الناطقة لا تقوى إلا على إدراك الكليات والمجردات، وأما الإدراكات الجزئية فإنها موزعة على الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة. واحتجوا على أن النفس لا تدرك الجزئيات بوجوه عامة ووجوه خاصة. أما الوجوه العامة فأربعة: الأول: إن العقلاء ببدائة عقولهم يعلمون أن رؤية المرئيات حاصلة في البصر لا غيره، وسماع الأصوات حاصل في الأذن لا في غيرها، بل نقول: كما أن البديهة حاكمة بأن اللسان غير مبصر، والعين غير ذائق، فهي أيضاً حاكمة بأن اللسان ذائق، والعين مبصرة. ولو قلنا: إن الموصوف بإدراك هذه المحسوسات هو النفس، لزم بطلان هذه الاختصاصات المعلومة. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: القوة المدركة، وإن كانت غير موجودة في هذه الأعضاء، لكنها آلات لها. فإذا وقع للنفس التفات إلى العين أبصرت أو إلى الأذن سمعت؟ قلنا: النفس إذا التفتت إلى اللسان، فهذا اللسان هل يدرك الطعم أم لا؟ فإن أدرك فقد حصل المطلوب، وإن لم يدرك فحينئذ نسبة اللسان إلى إدراك المذوقات، كنسبة سائر الأعضاء إليه. وذلك يبطل هذا الاختصاص.
Chapter Three: On Direct Perception [?]. Explanation that the soul involved [?] perceives universals and particulars together. And [?] for all perception by itself, although with the sphere is dependent on the use of universals. The apparent meaning of the Chief Master (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) suggests that the rational soul is only capable of perceiving universals and abstract entities. As for particular perceptions, they are distributed among the external and internal senses. They argued that the soul does not perceive particulars by general and specific arguments. As for the general arguments, they are four: First: The rational people know innately that vision of visible things occurs in the eye, not elsewhere, and hearing of sounds occurs in the ear, not elsewhere. Rather we say: just as intuition judges that the tongue is not sighted and the eye is not tasting, it also judges that the tongue is tasting and the eye is sighted. If we said that the subject of these sense perceptions is the soul, then these known specializations would be invalidated. They might say: Why is it not permissible to say: the perceiving power, even though it does not exist in these organs, yet they are instruments for it. So when the soul turns its attention to the eye it sees, or to the ear it hears? We say: If the soul turns to the tongue, does this tongue perceive taste or not? If it perceives, then the desired (conclusion) is obtained; if it does not perceive, then the relation of the tongue to the perception of tastes is like the relation of the other organs to it. And that invalidates this specialization.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage opens with a discussion on direct perception. It states that the soul is involved in perceiving both universals and particulars, but then quotes Avicenna's (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) view that the rational soul only perceives universals and abstractions, while particular perceptions are distributed among the external and internal senses. The author then declares that there are general and specific arguments for the claim that the soul does not perceive particulars. The first general argument invokes innate knowledge: everyone knows that sight occurs in the eye, hearing in the ear, etc. Intuition tells us that the tongue is for tasting and the eye for seeing. If the soul were the perceiver of these sensations, these specializations would be nullified. An objection is raised: the soul uses the organs as instruments by turning its attention to them. The answer is that if the soul turns to the tongue, does the tongue taste? If it does, then the soul is not the direct perceiver (the tongue does it), and if it does not, then the tongue is no different from other organs, thus invalidating the specialization. This keeps the first argument intact.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
الكليات والمجردات
الإدراكات الجزئية
الحواس الظاهرة والباطنة
بديهة
اختصاصات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the soul perceives particulars directly through the senses as instruments by turning its attention to them.
Correction: The argument shows that if the soul turns to the tongue, either the tongue itself perceives (so soul is not the direct perceiver) or it does not (so the specialization fails). Thus the soul cannot perceive particulars through the senses as instruments.

Checkpoint
According to the first general argument, if the soul were the direct perceiver of sensible qualities, what would be the consequence?
A) The specializations of the senses would be invalidated.
B) The soul would become material.
C) The senses would cease to function.
D) The tongue would not taste.

Key Takeaway
The innate specialization of the senses disproves that the soul directly perceives particulars; the objection from instrumental use fails because it cannot account for the specific function of each organ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use your eyes to read these words, do you truly believe that your soul is seeing directly, or is your eye the instrument of perception? How does this lesson challenge your intuitive assumption about where perception happens? Will you from now on consciously acknowledge the role of your senses as distinct receivers, or continue blurring the lines?
?
The argument uses innate knowledge of sense specializations. Is there an area of your faith where you rely on 'innate knowledge' that you have not critically examined? What is one assumption about your own soul's faculties that you have never questioned, and will you question it today?
?
The refutation of the instrumental objection shows that either the organ perceives directly or specialization is meaningless. Consider your own actions: Are you using your physical senses as a means to perceive spiritual truths? What specialization has Allah given your senses, and how can you honor that specialization rather than misusing it?

Third General Argument: Animal Perception
Presents the third general argument: animal perception implies non-physical souls for animals, which is far-fetched.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third general argument uses animal perception to argue that the subject of particular perceptions cannot be a non-physical soul.

Core Idea
Animals perceive; if perception required a non-physical soul, animals would have souls—an improbable conclusion.

The third argument draws upon the fact that animals, like humans, experience particular perceptions such as sight and hearing. If the soul were the perceiver and were non-physical, then animals too must possess non-physical souls. This is taken as unlikely, thus undercutting the claim that particular perceptions prove a non-physical soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the logical structure: premise, conditional implication, dismissal.
2
Identify the argument's form
The argument is a conditional (if-then) followed by a rejection of the consequent. Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the conclusion.
This is a modus tollens: if p then q, not q, so not p.
3
Consider the underlying assumption
Why is it far-fetched that animals have non-physical souls? Think about what is commonly attributed to animals: they have perception but not rationality. The argument exploits the intuition that animals are material.
The argument does not prove animals are material; it relies on prior belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: إن هذه الإدراكات الجزئية حاصلة لسائر الحيوانات. فلو كان الموصوف بهذه الإدراكات نفساً مجردة، لزم أن تكون نفوس الحيوانات نفوساً مجردة. وهو بعيد.
Third: These particular perceptions exist in all animals. If the subject of these perceptions were a non-physical soul, then it would be necessary that the souls of animals be non-physical souls. And that is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the third general argument. It points out that all animals, not just humans, have particular perceptions (e.g., seeing, hearing). If the subject of these perceptions were a non-physical soul (as the opposing view might hold for humans), then animals would also have to have non-physical souls. That conclusion is deemed unlikely (بعيد), presumably because animals lack rational faculties and are generally considered material. Therefore, particular perceptions are not the function of a non-physical soul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only humans have souls, but animals have perceptions; if perception is a function of the soul, then animals must have souls of the same non-physical kind.
Correction: The argument accepts that animals perceive, but it argues that if perception required a non-physical soul, the conclusion would be that animals have non-physical souls—which is implausible. So the argument does not prove animals have non-physical souls; rather it uses the implausibility to reject the idea that perception requires a non-physical soul.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The third argument uses the implausibility of animals having non-physical souls to reject the idea that particular perceptions come from a non-physical soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to follow the argument to its conclusion, even if it challenges your intuitive sympathy?
?
The author uses the implausibility of animal souls to reject the notion that perception requires a non-physical subject. Is your own sense of self—your 'I' that perceives—really just a physical process? If you are honest, do you not experience something irreducible when you see, hear, or feel? And if that experience is not physical, how do you avoid the very conclusion the author calls far-fetched?
?
This argument forces you to choose: either accept that animals have non-physical souls (which seems absurd), or deny that perception requires a non-physical soul at all. Which of these two positions do you actually live by? When you look at a dog that seems to recognize you, do you attribute to it a 'soul'? If not, can you consistently claim that your own perception is rooted in something immaterial? Sit with the discomfort of that choice.

Fourth General Argument: Imagination of a Sphere
Sanusi presents the fourth general argument: the impossibility of a sphere's image in a non-physical soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth general argument against the soul's immateriality, which claims that mental images of objects like spheres have spatial properties that cannot exist in a non-physical soul.

Core Idea
An imagined sphere has position and magnitude, revealing the soul cannot be immaterial.

This lesson presents a powerful argument from the nature of imagination. It claims that when we imagine a sphere, the mental image itself has spatial features like position and magnitude. Since an immaterial soul lacks these, the image cannot exist in it. This challenges the view that the soul is purely non-physical.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise of perception
Read the first part of the source: when we imagine a sphere, an image of the sphere must be imprinted in the perceiver because perception is a representation equal to the perceived.
This is a foundational principle about how perception works.
2
Identify the properties of the sphere's image
Notice that the image of a sphere has position and magnitude (وضع ومقدار). These are spatial properties that any physical object would have.
3
Consider the nature of the soul
The argument assumes the soul is non-physical, thus has no position or magnitude. Ask yourself: can an image with spatial properties exist in something without spatial properties?
The conclusion is that it is impossible.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since the image cannot be imprinted in the soul, the soul cannot be the subject of particular perceptions like imagination. This supports the view that the soul is material or that imagination requires a physical substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إنا إذا تخيلنا الكرة وجب أن يرتسم في هذا المدرك، صورة الكرة بناء على أن الإدراك صورة مساوية للمدرك، تحصل في المدرك. لكن من المحال أن ترتسم صورة الكرة، فيما لا وضع له ولا مقدار.
Fourth: When we imagine a sphere, it is necessary that an image of the sphere be imprinted in that perceiver, based on the principle that perception is a representation equal to the perceived, occurring in the perceiver. But it is impossible for an image of a sphere to be imprinted in something that has no position and no magnitude.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth general argument. It starts with the principle: when we imagine a sphere, an image (صورة) of the sphere must be imprinted in the perceiver (المدرك), because perception is a representation (صورة مساوية) of the perceived that occurs in the perceiver. However, a sphere's image has geometric properties like position and magnitude (وضع ومقدار). The soul is claimed to be non-physical, having no position or magnitude. Therefore, it is impossible for such an image to be imprinted in the soul. Hence, the soul cannot be the subject of particular perceptions like imagination.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Mental images are purely immaterial and have no spatial properties.
Correction: This argument asserts that the image of a sphere has position and magnitude, which are spatial properties, and therefore cannot exist in an immaterial soul.

Checkpoint

B) Because the image of a sphere has position and magnitude, which the soul lacks.
C) Because the soul cannot imagine.
D) Because perception is not representational.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument shows that mental images have spatial properties incompatible with an immaterial soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Special Arguments: Proximity and Perception
Specific arguments for corporeality of perception via spatial relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific argument that external perception must be physical because if the soul directly perceived, it would not differentiate between near and far, and why an objection based on sense organs is raised.

Core Idea
If the soul were the direct perceiver, distance would not matter—contradicting experience.

In this passage, the author presents a specific argument for the corporeality of perception, focusing on the implications of the soul being a non-physical substance. It emphasizes that if the soul were the direct perceiver of external objects, it would not be dependent on the object's presence. Consequently, perception of near and far would be identical, which is contrary to our actual experience. An objection and the beginning of its answer are also provided, though the answer is incomplete in our text.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional argument
The argument begins with a conditional: If the soul were the perceiver of external sensible objects, then its sensation would not depend on their presence. This is because the soul is non-physical and not spatially located.
The phrase 'لوجب أن لا يتوقف إحساس النفس بها، على حضورها' captures this.
2
Recognize the consequence for distance perception
The argument then deduces that if the above were true, then perception of near and far, present and absent, would be one and the same. This contradicts our experience where distance matters.
This shows that the soul cannot be the direct perceiver.
3
Consider the objection and its incomplete answer
An objection is raised: the soul perceives through instruments (sense organs) which have proximity and distance. The author begins to refute by pointing out that if an eye lacks the power of sight, then... but the text cuts off. Consider how this objection might be fully answered.
The answer likely continues that even if the organ is present, without the power, it doesn't perceive.

Commentary
This passage presents the specific arguments for the corporeality of perception. The first argument: if the soul were the direct perceiver of external objects, then its sensation would not depend on the objects' presence (since the soul is non-physical and not spatially located). Consequently, it would perceive near and far, present and absent, equally—which is false. The second point: the soul is non-physical, so it cannot have spatial relations like proximity to bodies. An objection is raised: the soul perceives through the instruments (sense organs) which have proximity and distance. The answer begins: if the eye lacks the power of sight, then... but the text cuts off, leaving the answer incomplete.

?

?

How does this call you to humility in your claims about what you 'feel' to be true? Will you commit to testing your perceptions against reality and revelation?

Three Proofs that Imagination Requires a Bodily Faculty
Three arguments demonstrating that imagination must reside in a bodily substrate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three arguments demonstrating that the faculty of imagination must reside in a bodily substrate.

Core Idea
Imagination requires a body because it involves spatial distinctions, magnitudes, and co-location constraints.

The following passage presents three proofs from Islamic philosophical theology that the imaginative faculty cannot be purely abstract but must be connected to a physical organ. These arguments rely on the necessity of spatial distinction for mental images, the variation in size of imagined objects, and the impossibility of imagining certain qualities in a single phantasm. As you read, notice how each proof leads to the same conclusion: imagination needs a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the three proofs
Read the passage carefully. Identify each of the three proofs. Notice they are labeled 'first', 'second', 'third'.
The proofs are numbered in the Arabic text, making them easy to distinguish.
2
Analyze the logical structure
For each proof, trace the argument: what assumption does it refute? What conclusion does it draw? How does it support the main thesis?
Pay attention to how each proof eliminates alternatives to arrive at the necessity of a bodily locus.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider how these three independent proofs together establish that imagination is a bodily faculty. What does this imply about the nature of the human soul?
Reflect on why this conclusion matters for understanding the relationship between mind and body in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا على أن التخيل لا يحصل إلا بقوة جسمانية : بوجوه ثلاثة : الأول : إنا إذا تخيلنا مربعا مجنحا بمربعين متساويين فلا شك أنه يتميز أحد هذين الجناحين عن الثاني في الفهم والإدراك . والنفي المحض والعدم الصرف ، يمتنع وقوع الامتياز فيه . فهذان المربعان لا بد وأن يكونا موجودين ، وظاهر أنه قد يكون ذلك غير موجود في الخارج ، فهو في الذهن . فإما أن يكون محل أحد هذين المربعين متساويان في الماهية ، وفي جميع لوازم الماهية ، فلو حصلا في محل واحد ، لارتفع الامتياز بالصفات العرضية أيضا ، فوجب أن لا يبقى الامتياز أصلا . وذلك باطل . وأن العلم الضروري حاصل بأنا نميز أحدهما عن الآخر . ولما بطل هذان القسمان تعين الثاني ، وهو أن يكون محل أحد هذين المربعين مباينا عن محل المربع الثاني . وهذا لا يعقل إلا إذا كان محل هذا الخيال جسما حتى يحصل في أحد جزئي ذلك الجسم، إحدى الصورتين. وفي الجزء الثاني منه ، الصورة الثانية . الثاني : إن الصورة الخيالية متفاوتة في المقادير. فإن تخيل البحر أعظم من تخيل الذرة . وهذا التفاوت إما أن يكون بسبب المأخوذ عنه أو بسبب الأخذ والأول باطل . لأنا قد نتخيل ما لا وجود له في الخارج . فبقي الثاني ، وهو أن تكون الصورة ، تارة ترتسم في جزء كبير ، فتكون تلك الصورة أكبر ، وتارة في جزء صغير فتكون تلك الصورة أصغر . الثالث : إنه لا يمكننا أن نتخيل السواد والبياض في شبح خيالي واحد ، ويمكننا ذلك في شبحين . ولو كان الشبحان لا يتميزان في الوضع ، لوجب أن لا يبقى فرق بين الممكن والمتعذر .
And they argued that imagination does not occur except by a bodily faculty, in three ways: First: If we imagine a winged square with two equal squares, there is no doubt that one of these two wings is distinguished from the second in understanding and perception. And pure negation and absolute non-existence preclude the occurrence of distinction in them. So these two squares must necessarily be existent, and it is apparent that that may not exist externally, so it is in the mind. So either the locus of one of these two squares is equal in essence and in all the concomitants of essence; then if they occurred in one locus, distinction by accidental attributes would also be removed, so it would be necessary that no distinction remain at all. And that is false. And necessary knowledge is obtained that we distinguish one from the other. And when these two divisions are invalidated, the second is determined, namely that the locus of one of these two squares is separate from the locus of the second square. And this is not intelligible unless the locus of this imagination is a body, such that one of the two images occurs in one part of that body, and the second image in the second part. Second: Indeed, the imaginary image varies in magnitudes. For the imagination of the sea is greater than the imagination of the atom. And this variation is either due to the thing from which it is taken or due to the taking; and the first is invalid, for we may imagine what has no existence externally. So the second remains, namely that the image is sometimes impressed in a large part, so that image is larger, and sometimes in a small part, so that image is smaller. Third: Indeed, we cannot imagine blackness and whiteness in one imaginary phantasm, and we can do so in two phantasms. And if the two phantasms were not distinct in position, it would be necessary that no difference remain between the possible and the impossible.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs for the corporeality of the imaginative faculty. It begins by stating the claim: imagination does not occur except through a bodily faculty. The first proof uses the example of imagining a winged square composed of two equal squares. In the mind, we clearly distinguish one wing from the other. Since pure non-existence cannot admit distinction, the two squares must exist in the mind. They cannot exist in the same locus because if they shared the same essence and were in one locus, there would be no distinction at all—but we do distinguish them. Therefore, each square must have a separate locus, which implies a body with parts. The second proof appeals to the varying sizes of imagined objects (e.g., sea vs. atom). This variation cannot be due to the external object, since we can imagine nonexistent things. Hence, it must be due to the part of the body in which the image is impressed—a larger part yields a larger image. The third proof considers imagining black and white: we cannot do so in a single phantasm, but we can in two. If the two phantasms were not distinct in position (i.e., in a bodily substrate), there would be no difference between possible and impossible. Thus, each phantasm must occupy a distinct location, again requiring a body.

Key Terms
تخيل (takhayyul)
قوة جسمانية (bodily faculty)
محل (locus)
تميز (distinction)
شبح خيالي (imaginary phantasm)
ماهية (essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think imagination is purely abstract and non-spatial, having no connection to the physical body.
Correction: These proofs demonstrate that imagination necessarily involves spatial properties (distinct positions, varying magnitudes, co-location constraints) that can only be realized in a bodily substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must the two imagined squares in the winged square be in different loci?
A) Because they are distinct in the mind and cannot be in the same subject without losing distinction.
B) Because they are physical objects requiring separate locations.
C) Because they have different colors that cannot coexist.
D) Because the mind cannot hold two images simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Imagination, though mental, necessarily involves spatial properties that can only be realized in a bodily substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine two contradictory colors in the same mental space, you feel an impossibility. How does that limitation expose the physical nature of your own imagination? What does it mean for you to rely on something so constrained when seeking truth?
?
You have just learned that the size of your mental images corresponds to the size of the brain region they occupy. Does this change how you treat your own thoughts? Are your grand ideas somehow 'lodged' in a small part of your body? How will you respond to the humbling notion that your imagination is not free from physical boundaries?
?
The proof shows that to distinguish two imagined objects, they must occupy different 'locations' in your brain. Have you ever considered that your mental world has a geography? What accountability does this place on you for the 'images' you entertain, knowing they occupy real space in your being?

Proof that the Estimative Faculty is Bodily
One argument demonstrating that the estimative faculty must be bodily.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the estimative faculty must be bodily because the judge of friendship/enmity must perceive the individual person, and such perception is bodily.

Core Idea
The judge and the perceiver must be the same; physical perception implies a bodily faculty.

This short but dense argument from classical Islamic philosophy tackles the nature of the estimative faculty (al-quwwa al-wahmiyya). The claim is that this faculty, which judges things like 'friend' or 'enemy', cannot be purely spiritual because it must perceive concrete individuals. Read carefully to see how the proof unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the key terms: قوة وهمية, تصديق, تصور, مدرك, جسماني.
The argument is concise; each word carries weight.
2
Understand the premise
Identify the initial observation: when we judge a specific person as friend or enemy, the one who judges must be the same as the one who perceives that person and the abstract meanings.
The consequence of separation is impossible: judgment before conception.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Since the perceiver of a physical person is bodily (as established earlier), the judge must also be bodily. Therefore, the estimative faculty is bodily.
The argument hinges on the identity of the perceiver and the judge.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: it grounds a cognitive faculty in the physical brain/body, opposing views that make it purely spiritual.
This is a key move in the debate about the nature of human faculties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا على أن القوة الوهمية جسمانية : بأن قالوا : إنا إذا حكمنا على هذا الشخص ، بأنه صديق أو عدو .. فالحاكم بهذا الحكم يجب أن يكون هو بعينه مدركا لهذا الشخص، ولمعنى الصداقة والعداوة ، وإلا لكان ذلك التصديق حاصلا قبل التصور ، وهو محال . ولما ثبت أن المدرك الصورة هذا الشخص ، يجب أن يكون جسمانيا وجب أن يكون ذلك الحاكم أيضا جسمانيا .
And they argued that the estimative faculty is bodily, by saying: When we judge concerning this person that he is a friend or an enemy, the one who makes this judgment must be the very one who perceives this person and the meaning of friendship and enmity; otherwise, that affirmation would occur before conception, which is impossible. And since it is established that the perceiver of the form of this person must be bodily, it follows that that judge must also be bodily.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the corporeality of the estimative (or 'wahmiyya') faculty. It begins by noting that when we judge a person as a friend or enemy, the judge must be the same entity that perceives both the individual person and the abstract meaning (friend/enemy). If that were not the case, the judgment (affirmation) would occur before the conception (tasawwur) of the object, which is impossible. The argument then appeals to a prior established point: the perceiver of a physical form (the person) must be bodily. Since the judge is the same perceiver, it too must be bodily. Thus, the estimative faculty cannot be a non-bodily soul but rather resides in a bodily organ.

Key Terms

تصديق (affirmation/judgment)

مدرك (perceiver)
جسماني (bodily)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The faculty that judges abstract relations like friendship is purely intellectual and non-material.
Correction: The argument shows that such judgment requires perception of a concrete individual, which is bodily, so the faculty itself must be bodily.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why must the judge (of friend/enemy) be bodily?
A) Because judgment requires a physical brain.
B) Because it must perceive the person's form, which is bodily, and the judge is the same perceiver.
C) Because friendship and enmity are physical concepts.
D) Because non-bodily beings cannot make judgments.

Key Takeaway
The estimative faculty is bodily because the judgment it makes requires perception of a physical individual, and such perception is inherently bodily.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that judgment requires prior conception. Think of a time when you affirmed something about a person without truly conceiving them first. What does that reveal about your own intellectual habits, and how will you change them starting today?
?
If the estimative faculty is bodily, then your judgments are not purely spiritual but tied to your physical nature. Does this humble your pride in your own discernment? How can you use this insight to seek more objective knowledge?

Proof that the Motive Power is Bodily
Alusi presents a proof by contrapositive that the motive power resides in the body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam uses a counterfactual argument to prove that the motive power must be a bodily faculty, not the soul itself.

Core Idea
If the soul directly moved the body, we could move anything without tools—but we can't.

This lesson examines a carefully constructed argument that establishes a key principle about the nature of the soul and its relationship to the body. The author presents a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the soul is the direct mover leads to an impossible consequence, forcing us to accept that the motive power is bodily. Let's follow the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the conditional phrase 'لو كان' (if it were).
The argument is concise, so read carefully.
2
Map the logical structure
Identify the assumption, the intermediate steps, the absurd consequence, and the conclusion. Notice that the argument uses the impossibility of a non-body touching a body as a key premise.
This is a classic reductio argument.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: why does the argument show that the motive power must be bodily? What would have to be true if the soul were the mover?
Connect to the idea that the soul is a non-physical substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا على أن القوة المحركة جسمانية : بأن قالوا : لو كان المحرك لهذه الأجسام هو النفس ، لكان ذلك التحريك حاصلا من غير توسط آلة جسمانية ، ومن غير مماسة آلة . لأن ما لا يكون جسما ، يمتنع أن يكون مماسا للجسم . ولو كان الأمر كذلك ، لوجب أن يكون الإنسان قادرا على تحريك سائر الأجسام من غير حاجة إلى شيء من الآلات الجسمانية ، ولما كان ذلك باطلا ، ثبت أن محل القوة المحركة ، هو البدن ، لا جوهر النفس .
And they argued that the motive power is bodily, by saying: If the mover of these bodies were the soul, then that moving would occur without the mediation of a bodily instrument and without contact of an instrument. Because what is not a body cannot be in contact with a body. And if that were the case, then man would be able to move all other bodies without needing any bodily instruments. And since that is false, it is established that the locus of the motive power is the body, not the substance of the soul.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage offers a proof for the corporeality of the motive power (quwwa muḥarrika). It argues by contrapositive: if the mover of the body were the soul (a non-bodily substance), then movement would occur without any bodily instrument and without any contact (because a non-body cannot touch a body). If that were true, humans would be able to move any external body at will without physical means—which is false. Therefore, the mover cannot be the soul itself; rather, the motive power must reside in the body (i.e., be a bodily faculty). The conclusion emphasizes that the locus (maḥall) of the motive power is the body, not the substance of the soul (jawhar al-nafs).

Key Terms
قوة محركة (motive power)
نفس (soul)
آلة جسمانية (bodily instrument)
مماسة (contact)
محل (locus)
جوهر النفس (substance of the soul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul can directly move the body without any physical instrument.
Correction: If that were true, humans would be able to move any external body at will, which is false. Therefore, the motive power must be a bodily faculty, not the soul itself.

Checkpoint
What is the false consequence if the soul were the direct mover of the body?
A) Humans would be able to move all bodies without instruments.
B) Humans would be stuck in one place.
C) The soul would become material.
D) Movement would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The motive power cannot be the soul itself because that would allow movement without instruments, which experience denies; it must be a bodily power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that you cannot move a single object by sheer will without a bodily instrument. When was the last time you felt frustrated by your body's limitations—perhaps in illness or weakness? Did you resent your body? How does this proof help you see the body not as a prison but as a necessary tool granted by Allah for action? Commit today to thank Allah for your bodily faculties and to use them in obedience, not to despise them.
?
If your soul could directly move any body, you could lift mountains with a thought. But you cannot. What does that teach you about your dependence on created means? In your daily life, do you rely more on physical efforts or on supplication? Let this argument remind you that Allah has placed you in a world of causes and effects. Reflect: Are you neglecting the means He has provided while expecting miraculous interventions? This week, strive to use the bodily instruments Allah gave you with full trust in His decree.

Turning the Localization Argument Against the Opponents
Summary of arguments and first reply to sensation localization objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument from localization of sensation can be turned against those who deny a non-bodily soul.

Core Idea
If localization proves a faculty is bodily, then knowledge would be bodily — negating the soul.

After summarizing the best arguments on sensation, the author begins his reply to the first objection. The opponents argued that because sight and hearing are localized in the eye and ear, those faculties are bodily. The author counters that perception and knowledge are also localized (in heart or brain), so by the same logic they would be bodily — which would destroy the concept of an immaterial soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the summary statement and the beginning of the reply.
The reply starts with 'والجواب عما ذكروا أولاً'.
2
Identify the opponents' premise
The opponents claim that sight and hearing are specific to the eye and ear, implying their faculties are bodily. Find this in the passage.
The premise is implied by the objection that sensation is localized.
3
Spot the author's counter
The author points out that perception and knowledge are also localized (in heart or brain). Why does he mention this?
He is using the opponents' own reasoning against them.
4
Understand the conclusion
If localization proves a bodily faculty, then knowledge being localized would prove the soul is bodily. This contradicts the affirmation of a non-bodily soul.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Think about whether this refutation is sufficient. The passage only gives the first 'face' of the response.
There may be further responses in the original text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه حكاية الوجوه التي ذكروها في هذا الباب على أحسن الوجوه ، وأقربها إلى العقل ، وأبعدها عن الحشو والفضول . والجواب عما ذكروه أولاً : وهو قولهم : « إنا نجد الإبصار مختصاً بالعين ، والسماع مختصاً بالأذن ، من وجوه : الأول : إنا نقول : كما أنا نجد هذه الحالة ، فكذلك نجد الإدراك والعلم من ناحية القلب أو الدماغ، على اختلاف المذهبين فيه . فإن دل ما ذكرتم على أن محل القوة الباصرة هو العين، وعلى أن محل القوة السامعة هو الأذن ، وجب أن يدل ما ذكرناه على أن محل العلم والإدراك هو القلب . وحينئذ يبطل القول بإثبات النفس .
So this is an account of the aspects that they mentioned in this chapter in the best way, the closest to reason, and the furthest from redundancy and verbosity. And the answer to what they mentioned first—which is their statement: 'We find sight specific to the eye, and hearing specific to the ear'—in aspects: First: We say: Just as we find this state, so too we find perception and knowledge coming from the direction of the heart or the brain, according to the difference of opinions therein. For if what you mentioned indicates that the locus of the power of sight is the eye, and that the locus of the power of hearing is the ear, then what we mentioned must indicate that the locus of knowledge and perception is the heart. And that would invalidate the affirmation of the soul.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage first summarizes the arguments presented in the chapter as being the best, most rational, and most concise. Then it begins the response to the first objection: the opponents claim that sight and hearing are localized in specific organs. The response counters by pointing out that perception and knowledge are also localized (in heart or brain, depending on the school). If the opponents take localization as proof that the faculty is bodily, then by the same token, the localization of knowledge would prove that the faculty of knowledge is also bodily—which would negate the existence of a non-bodily soul (nafs). This is a reductio ad absurdum against the opponents' premise. The response is only the first face (aspect); the subsequent faces are not present in this window.

علم (knowledge)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Localization of a function always implies the function is bodily.
Correction: The response shows that if localization proved a bodily faculty, then knowledge being localized would prove the soul bodily, which the opponents deny. Thus localization does not necessarily imply a bodily faculty.

Checkpoint

C) The heart and brain are not bodily.
D) The soul is located in the heart.

Key Takeaway
The localization of perception and knowledge in the heart or brain would, by the opponents' own logic, prove that the soul is bodily, thus refuting their position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in a non-material soul, but do you live as if your thoughts and knowledge are merely outputs of your brain? How would this argument unsettle that comfortable materialism?
?
When you learn about Allah or ponder your own mortality, where does that knowledge feel like it comes from — your head, your heart, or somewhere beyond your body? What would it mean if that knowledge were just a bodily process?
?
If you were to apply the opponents' logic to your own sense of self, would you conclude that you are only a collection of organs? This lesson exposes the flaw in that reduction. How must you change the way you view your own mind and soul from now on?

The First Proof: Unity of Cognition Proves the Soul
First proof for the soul as direct agent via unity of cognition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof for the soul's agency over the body, which demonstrates that a single entity (the soul) must underlie multiple cognitive functions such as perception, imagination, judgment, composition, and universalization.

Core Idea
The unity of diverse mental acts proves a single soul.

This passage opens a new chapter presenting decisive arguments that the soul is the direct agent of the body. The first proof argues from the unity of cognitive functions: when we sense, imagine, judge, compose, and universalize, we recognize that these operations must belong to one and the same subject. This subject is the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a continuous argument with several sub-steps.
2
Identify the overall claim
The author states that the soul is the one described with all possibilities and the direct agent of all movements. This is the thesis he aims to prove.
3
Follow the sequence of cognitive acts
The proof moves through sensation, imagination, judgment, composition, and universalization. At each step, note how the argument insists on a single subject.
Each sub-argument is built on the fact that we compare two cognitive contents and judge them to be from one source.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why can't different faculties account for these acts? The answer is the judgment of identity or relation requires a single subject that holds both contents together.
This is a classic argument for the unity of the soul.

الفصل [؟] سالم
في

نقول : الذي يدل على صحة ما ذكرناه وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : ما ذكرناه في إثبات (۱) ( النفس ، ولا بأس بإعادة (٢) ذلك . فنقول : إنا إذا رأينا العسل حكمنا بأنه حلو ، والقاضي على الشيئين لا بد وأن يحضره المقضي عليهما . فههنا شيء واحد يدرك جميع المحسوسات . ثم إذا تخيلناها [ ثم أحسسنا بها . حكمنا بأن ذلك المتخيل هو هذا المحسوس . فلا بد من شيء واحد ، هو المدرك ، وهو المتخيل . ثم إذا أحسسنا(۳) ] تلك الصور ، أو تخيلنا تلك الخيالات حكمنا عليها تارة بالعداوة ، وأخرى بالصداقة . فلا بد من شيء واحد هو المدرك لتلك الصور ولتلك المعاني . ثم إن القوة الفكرية تقوى على تركيب تلك الصور، وتلك المعاني ، بعضها بالبعض ، فيكون ذلك الشيء قادراً على التركيب والتحليل ، وعلى إدراك الصور والمعاني . ثم إن الإنسان يمكنه أن يحكم على كل واحد من الأشخاص بإدخاله(4) تحت الماهية الكلية، فلا بد من شيء واحد يكون بعينه موصوفاً بالإدراكات الكلية والجزئية . ثم إن الفعل الاختياري عبارة عما إذا اعتقد في
The chapter [؟] Salim. On the decisive argument that the one described with all kinds of possibilities (imkānāt) and the direct agent of all movements and stimulations for this body is the soul. We say: That which indicates the correctness of what we have mentioned are aspects: The first proof: What we mentioned in establishing the soul, and there is no harm in repeating that. So we say: When we see honey, we judge that it is sweet. And the judge of two things must have the two judged things present to him. So here there is one thing that perceives all sensible objects. Then when we imagine them [then we sense them], we judge that that imagined thing is this perceived thing. So there must be one thing, which is the perceiver and the imaginer. Then when we sense those forms, or imagine those images, we judge them sometimes as enmity and sometimes as friendship. So there must be one thing that is the perceiver of those forms and those meanings. Then the intellectual faculty is able to combine those forms and those meanings, some with others, so that thing becomes capable of composition and analysis, and of perceiving forms and meanings. Then man can judge each individual by including it under the universal essence, so there must be one thing that is described by both universal and particular perceptions. Then voluntary action is defined as [something that] when one believes in
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new chapter presenting proofs for the soul (al-nafs) as the direct agent of the body. The author first states the claim: the soul is the subject of all possibilities (imkānāt) and the direct agent of all movements and stimulations. He then says there are aspects (wujūh) that prove this, beginning with the first proof. The first proof argues for the unity of the soul through several cognitive acts: (1) When we see honey and judge it sweet, there is a single thing that perceives the honey. (2) When we imagine the honey and then sense it, we judge that the imagined is the same as the sensed, so there must be a single thing that is both the perceiver and the imaginer. (3) When we sense forms or imagine images, we judge them with varying relations (enmity, friendship), requiring a single perceiver of both forms and meanings. (4) The intellectual faculty (al-quwwa al-fikriyya) can compose and analyze these forms and meanings, so that thing must be capable of composition and analysis. (5) Man can judge particular individuals as instances of a universal essence, requiring a single thing that has both universal and particular perceptions. The proof cuts off while beginning to discuss voluntary action. The argument consistently shows that multiple operations (sensation, imagination, judgment, composition, universalization) require a single subject, which is the soul.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul is a collection of separate faculties (e.g., separate powers for sensation, imagination, etc.).
Correction: The proof shows that a single subject must perform multiple acts because we judge across these acts (e.g., the imagined is the same as the sensed).

Checkpoint

B) Because we judge that the imagined honey is the same as the sensed honey, implying a unified perceiver.
C) Because honey is always perceived as sweet.
D) Because the soul is the only thing that can move the body.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for the soul's agency shows that the unity of perception, imagination, judgment, composition, and universalization requires a single immaterial subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your senses, imagination, and intellect pull you in different directions, do you consciously turn to the single soul that Allah has placed within you to unify your will? This week, before you act, pause and ask yourself: 'Is this motion coming from my soul directly, or am I being driven by a scattered faculty?'
?
The proof shows that your soul is the direct agent of every voluntary act. Will you start each day by reminding yourself that every thought, word, and motion originates from the soul that you will be held accountable for?
?
If the same soul that perceives honey also judges, imagines, and universalizes, then why do you often act as if your desires and your intellect are at war? What would change if you consistently entrusted the governance of your inner faculties to that single, God-given soul?

Three Views on the Nature of Human Souls
Enumerates and refutes two positions on the nature of human souls, introducing the third

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible positions on whether human souls are necessary, eternal, or contingent and originated, and why the first two are rejected.

Core Idea
Human souls are contingent and created in time, not self-existent or eternal.

In this passage, the author presents a classification of possible views about the ontological status of human souls. He outlines three positions, then refutes the first two by referencing established proofs, before affirming the third as the correct one. This sets the stage for a detailed argument that souls are temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the three positions enumerated: souls as necessary, souls as eternal but not necessary, and souls as contingent and originated.
The author uses 'One of them...', 'The second...', 'The third...' to structure the discussion.
2
Understand the refutation of the first position
The first position (souls are necessary) is refuted by referencing that only the One (God) is necessary of existence. Ask yourself: Why can't multiple things be necessary?
This aligns with tawhid — only Allah is wajib al-wujud.
3
Note the dismissal of the second position
The second position (souls are eternal) is said to have been addressed earlier. Recognize that this indicates prior argumentation has already refuted it.
The author doesn't repeat the argument here; he assumes prior knowledge.
4
Prepare for the third position
The third position (souls are contingent and temporally originated) is introduced as the one the author will defend. Notice the phrase 'we say' (فنقول) which signals the beginning of the positive argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence, so the full argument continues in subsequent lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم: أن هذه النفوس البشرية فيها ثلاث مراتب في الاحتمالات:
أحدها: أن نقول إنها ليست محدثة، وليست أيضاً ممكنة، بل هي أشياء واجبة الوجود لذواتها.
وثانيها: أن يقال إنها ليست واجبة الوجود لذواتها، إلا أنها قديمة أزلية، كما يقول الفلاسفة في العقول الفلكية والنفوس الفلكية.
وثالثها: أن يقال: إنها لا واجبة ولا أزلية، بل هي ممكنة لذواتها، محدثة حدوثاً زمانياً.
أما القول الأول: فالدليل على بطلانه: الدلائل المذكورة في أن واجب الوجود لذاته، ليس إلا الواحد.
وأما القول الثاني: وهي أنها قديمة، فالقول فيه قد سبق.
وأما القول الثالث: وهو أنها ممكنة لذواتها، محدثة زمانياً، فنقول: لما
Know that these human souls have three ranks in the possible positions: One of them: that we say they are not originated and also not possible, but rather they are things necessary of existence in themselves. The second: that it is said they are not necessary of existence in themselves, but they are eternal, beginningless, as the philosophers say regarding the celestial intellects and celestial souls. The third: that it is said they are neither necessary nor eternal, but rather they are contingent in themselves, originated in time. As for the first statement: the proof of its falsehood is the proofs mentioned that the Necessary of Existence in Himself is none but the One. As for the second statement, which is that they are eternal, the discourse on it has preceded. As for the third statement, which is that they are contingent in themselves, temporally originated, we say: when
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a classification of possible positions on the ontological nature of human souls. It enumerates three possibilities: 1) The souls are necessary of existence in themselves (wajib al-wujud li-dhatiha) – i.e., uncreated and not contingent. 2) They are not necessary but are eternal (qadima azaliyya), as philosophers claim for celestial intellects and souls. 3) They are neither necessary nor eternal but contingent (mumkin) and temporally originated. The author then refutes the first position by referencing proofs that only the One (God) is necessary of existence. The second position is said to have been addressed earlier. The third position is the one the author intends to defend, as indicated by 'we say' (fa-naqulu), though the text breaks off. The structure sets up the correct view: human souls are contingent and created in time.

Key Terms
النفوس الناطقة
واجب الوجود
ممكنة
محدثة
قديمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think human souls are self-existent or eternal like God.
Correction: The passage refutes both: the first (necessary existence) contradicts tawhid; the second (eternal) is refuted by earlier arguments. The correct view is that souls are contingent and created in time.

Checkpoint

A) The souls are eternal.
B) The souls are necessary of existence in themselves.
C) The souls are contingent and originated.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Human souls are contingent and temporally originated; they are neither self-existent nor eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is not self-existent but created by Allah, how does that shape your sense of dependence on Him in every moment? What changes in your daily life when you truly internalize that your very existence is a gift sustained by His will?
?
The passage rejects the idea that your soul is eternal or self-existent. Have you ever acted as if your soul is independent of Allah—for example, by feeling that your achievements are due to your own power? What specific step can you take today to acknowledge your contingency before Him?
?
The correct view is that your soul is contingent and temporally originated. This means your soul has a beginning and is not self-sufficient. How does this truth affect how you view your purpose in life and your accountability to your Creator? What is one habit you can change to live in light of this reality?
