A) Khajal
B) Taʿajjub
C) Ḥayā
D) Ḥuzn

Key Takeaway
The passage identifies four distinct psychological states in humans—wonder, laughter/weeping, shame, and modesty—each triggered by specific cognitive judgments and often accompanied by bodily reactions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you experienced taʿajjub (wonder) at something in Allah's creation? Did it lead you to ponder His power? Commit to noticing one thing today that reminds you of His greatness and pause in wonder.
?
Think of a time you laughed heartily or wept deeply. Did you recognize that these were not merely physical reactions but responses to a sense of agreeableness or harm from Allah? How can you be more grateful for the agreeable and patient with the harmful?
?
Do you feel khajal (shame) when you fear others' opinions about your sins, but do you feel ḥayā (modesty) that prevents you from sinning out of reverence for Allah? Ask yourself: which of these two states drives your moral choices more often? Make a sincere intention to strengthen ḥayā before your Creator.

Memory as a Human Property with Uncertainty
Sanusi presents memory as the fifth human property and notes uncertainty about its exclusivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that memory is presented as the fifth human property and that the author expresses uncertainty about whether it is unique to humans.

Core Idea
Memory is a human property, but certainty about its exclusivity is problematic.

In this brief passage, the author introduces memory as the fifth property distinguishing humans from other animals. He notes a common claim that animals lack this ability, but then expresses doubt: affirming or denying this claim with certainty is problematic. This lesson will explore that hesitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author lists memory as the fifth human property, then adds a note of uncertainty.
The passage is very short but packs a claim and a caveat.
2
Identify the claim and the caveat
The claim is that memory does not occur in other animals. The caveat is that certainty about this is problematic.
This shows the author's cautious approach.
3
Consider why certainty is problematic
Think about why the author might doubt both affirmation and negation. This connects to the limitations of human knowledge about animal minds.
The commentary will explain further.

The Soul's Desire: Particular vs Universal
An objection about the soul's universal desire for pleasure is answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the soul's desire for pleasure does not imply a universal belief that all pleasure is desirable, and how this relates to the limits of human knowledge about the unseen.

Core Idea
The soul desires particular pleasures, not a universal rule.

In this passage, an objection is raised: if the soul naturally desires pleasure and avoids pain, doesn't it hold that all pleasure is desirable? The answer clarifies that the soul's desire is for each particular pleasurable thing as it appears, not for a general judgment. This distinction highlights the difference between particular responses and universal beliefs, and reminds us that ultimate knowledge of unseen matters belongs to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice that the passage begins with an objection ('إنكاره') and then gives an answer ('وأجيب عنه').
The passage is a complete unit of objection and answer.
2
Identify the objection
The objection states that because the soul desires every pleasure and avoids every pain, it must believe that every pleasurable is desired and every painful is disliked.
This is a logical inference from the soul's behavior.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer distinguishes between desiring a particular pleasure and holding a universal belief. The soul only desires each pleasurable thing as it appears, not the universal proposition 'every pleasurable is desired.'
Desire is particular, not general.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends by noting that judgments about unseen matters (like the soul's universal beliefs) belong only to Allah. This reminds us of the limits of human knowledge.
Humility in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنكاره . وفيه موضع بحث . فإنها راغبة في كل ما يكون لذيذاً عندها ، نافرة عن كل ما يكون مؤلماً عندها . فوجب أن يتقرر عندها : أن كل لذيذ مطلوب ، وأن كل مؤلم مكروه . وأجيب عنه : بأن رغبتها إنما تكون في هذا اللذيذ . فكل لذيذ حضر عندها فإنها ترغب فيه ، من حيث إنه ذلك الشيء . فأما أن تعتقد أن كل لذيذ ، فهو مطلوب . فهذا ليس عندها . واعلم : أن الحكم في هذه الأشياء بالنفي والإثبات : حكم على الغيب والعلم بها ليس إلا الله العلي العليم (۱) سبحانه وتعالى وتقدس ( م ، ط ) .
Its denial. And in it is a place for investigation. For it (the soul) is desirous of everything that is pleasurable to it, averse to everything that is painful to it. So it must be established for it that every pleasurable is desired, and every painful is disliked. And it was answered about that: that its desire is only for this particular pleasurable. So every pleasurable thing that appears to it, it desires it, insofar as it is that thing. But as for its believing that every pleasurable is desired, that is not for it. And know: that the judgment in these things by negation and affirmation is a judgment about the unseen, and knowledge of them is only for Allah, the High, the All-Knowing (1) glorified and exalted and sanctified (M, T).
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the nature of the soul's desire for pleasure. The objection states that the soul is naturally inclined toward every pleasure and averse to every pain, which would necessitate that it holds a universal judgment that all pleasure is desirable and all pain is detestable. The answer refines this: the soul's desire is not for a universal concept, but for each particular pleasurable thing as it appears; it does not make a general ruling that every pleasure is desirable. The passage concludes by noting that judgments about such unseen matters belong only to Allah, reminding the reader of the limits of human knowledge.

Correction: The soul only desires particular pleasures as they appear, not the universal rule.

Key Takeaway
The soul's desire for pleasure is particular, not universal; knowledge of unseen matters belongs only to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong desire for something pleasurable, do you ever assume that this desire is a universal truth everyone should follow? What does this passage teach you about the particularity of your own desires?
?

?
This passage reminds us that knowledge of the soul's true nature belongs only to Allah. Are there areas of your own inner life—your motives, fears, or hidden desires—that you try to fully understand on your own, without turning to Allah in humility? What would it look like to surrender that knowledge to Him?

Why Sensory Pleasures Are Not Ultimate Happiness
The author rejects the common view and offers a proof by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a reductio ad absurdum argument shows that human happiness is not found in sensory pleasures like eating and sex.

Core Idea
If happiness were in sensory pleasures, animals would be happier than humans.

This passage from al-Matalib al-'Aliyah confronts a common belief: most people think the highest happiness lies in sensual pleasures. The author rejects this by pointing out that animals surpass humans in those very pleasures, which would absurdly make them more perfect. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Notice the author first states the common view, then declares it rejected, then presents the first proof.
The text contains some OCR errors; the intended word for 'rational pleasures' is اللذات العقلية.
2
Identify the common misconception
Find where the author describes what most people believe about happiness. Understand why the author says this view is rejected by the wise.
3
Analyze the first proof
Follow the conditional argument: if happiness were in sensory pleasures, then animals superior in those would be more noble, which is false, so the premise must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني والسكرين في بيان أن اللغات المقلية أشرف وأكمل من اللذات الصية اللذات ، اعلم أن الغالب على الطباع العامية : [ الحكم ] بأن أقوى وأكمل السعادات : لذة المطعم والمنكح . ولذلك فإن جمهور الناس لا يعبدون الله تعالى إلا ليجدوا المطاعم اللذيذة في الآخرة ، وإلا ليجدوا المناكح الشهية [ هناك ] . وهذا القول مرذول عند المحققين من أهل الحكمة ، وأرباب الرياضة . ويدل عليه وجوه : الحجة الأولى : لو كانت سعادة الإنسان متعلقة بقضاء الشهوة ، وإمضاء الغضب . لكان اليحوان الذي يكون هو أقوى في هذا الباب من الإنسان : أشرف منه . لكن الجمل أكثر أكلاً من الناس ، والذئب أقوى في الإيذاء من الإنسان . والعصفور أقوى على السفاد من الإنسان ، فوجب كون هذه الأشياء أشرف من الإنسان . لكن التالي معلوم البطلان بالضرورة ، فوجب الجزم بأن سعادة الإنسان غير متعلقة بهذه الأمور . الحجة الثانية : كل شيء يكون سبباً لحصول الكمال والسعادة . فكل ما

Know that the prevalent among common natures is the judgment that the strongest and most perfect happinesses are the pleasure of food and intercourse. Therefore most people do not worship Allah Almighty except to find delicious foods in the afterlife, or to find desirable sexual partners there. And this statement is rejected by the verifiers among the people of wisdom and the practitioners of spiritual discipline. And evidence for it are aspects: The first proof: if human happiness were related to fulfilling desire and executing anger, then the animal that is stronger in this regard than humans would be more noble than it [the human]. But the camel eats more than people, the wolf is stronger in harming than humans, and the bird is stronger in copulation than humans, so it would be necessary that these things be more noble than humans. But the consequent is known to be false necessarily, so it is necessary to be certain that human happiness is not related to these matters. The second proof: Everything that is a cause for attaining perfection and happiness... so everything that

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter (the second) which argues that rational pleasures are superior to sensory pleasures. It first describes the common view: most people think the ultimate happiness lies in sensual pleasures like eating and sex, and even worship God just to obtain these in the afterlife. The author declares this view as rejected by the wise and spiritual practitioners. Then he presents the first proof: if human happiness were tied to satisfying desires and anger, then animals that exceed humans in those areas (e.g., camel in eating, wolf in harming, bird in sex) would be more perfect than humans. But that is plainly false, so happiness must not depend on these. The second proof is cut off but begins by stating that anything that is a cause for perfection and happiness...

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Common Misconception and the First Proof
Introduction and first proof via reductio ad absurdum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi's first argument uses the superiority of animals in sensory pleasures to prove that human happiness cannot consist in such pleasures.

Core Idea
If happiness were in sensory pleasures, animals would be nobler than humans.

Al-Razi begins by identifying a common error: most people believe the strongest happiness lies in eating and sexual gratification. He then presents a devastating reductio ad absurdum: if that were true, then animals—who surpass us in these very activities—would be superior to humans. This first argument lays the foundation for his critique of materialism.

Guided Path
1
Read the introduction and the first proof.
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Notice how al-Razi sets up the common view before refuting it.
Pay attention to the logical structure: condition, observation, conclusion, falsification.
2
Identify the reductio argument.
The proof is a reductio ad absurdum. Trace the conditional premise, the factual premise, and the absurd consequence.
The key step: 'camel eats more, wolf is stronger, bird copulates more'.
3
Reflect on the conclusion.
Ask yourself why the conclusion that these animals would be nobler is obviously false. This shows the original premise is false.
Al-Razi uses this to establish that happiness is not based on sensory pleasures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني والعشرين
في بيان أن اللذات العقلية أشرف وأكمل من اللذات الحسية
اعلم أن الغالب على الطباع العامية: الحكم بأن أقوى وأكمل السعادات: لذة المطعم والمنكح. ولذلك فإن جمهور الناس لا يعبدون الله تعالى إلا ليجدوا المطاعم اللذيذة في الآخرة، وإلا ليجدوا المناكح الشهية هناك.
وهذا القول مرذول عند المحققين من أهل الحكمة، وأرباب الرياضة.
ويدل عليه وجوه:
الحجة الأولى: لو كانت سعادة الإنسان متعلقة بقضاء الشهوة، وإمضاء الغضب. لكان الحيوان الذي يكون هو أقوى في هذا الباب من الإنسان: أشرف منه. لكن الجمل أكثر أكلاً من الناس، والذئب أقوى في الإيذاء من الإنسان. والعصفور أقوى على السفاد من الإنسان، فوجب كون هذه الأشياء أشرف من الإنسان. لكن التالي معلوم البطلان بالضرورة، فوجب الجزم بأن سعادة الإنسان غير متعلقة بهذه الأمور.
الحجة الثانية: كل شيء يكون سبباً لحصول الكمال والسعادة. فكل ما كان ذلك الشيء أكثر حصولاً، كانت السعادة والكمال أكثر حصولاً. فلو كان قضاء شهوة البطن والفرج سبباً لكمال حال الإنسان ولسعادته، لكان الإنسان كلما كان أكثر اشتغالاً بقضاء شهوة البطن والفرج، وأكثر استغراقاً فيه، كان أعلى درجة وأكمل فضيلة. لكن التالي باطل. لأن الإنسان الذي جعل عمره وقفاً على الأكل والشرب والسفاد، يعد من البهائم، ويقضي عليه بالدناءة والخساسة. وكل ذلك يدل على أن الاشتغال بقضاء هاتين الشهوتين، ليس من باب السعادات والكمالات، بل من باب دفع الحاجات والآفات.
الحجة الثالثة: إن الإنسان يشاركه في لذة الأكل والشرب، جميع الحيوانات الخسيسة. فإنه كما أن الإنسان يلتذ بأكل السكر، فكذلك يلتذ الجعل بأكل السرجين. فلو كانت هذه اللذات البدنية، هي السعادة الكبرى للإنسان، لوجب أن لا يكون للإنسان فضيلة على هذه الحيوانات الخسيسة، بل نزيد فنقول: لو كانت سعادة الإنسان متعلقة بهذه اللذات الحسية، لوجب أن يكون الإنسان أخس الحيوانات. والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله.
وبيان وجه الملازمة: أن الحيوانات الخسيسة مشاركة للإنسان في هذه اللذات الحسية البدنية، إلا أن الإنسان تنتقص عليه هذه المطالب بسبب العقل. فإن العقل سمي عقلا، لكونه عقالاً له، وحبساً له عن أكثر ما يشتهيه، ويميل طبعه إليه. فإذا كان التقدير أن كمال السعادة ليس إلا في هذه اللذات الحسية. ثم بينا: أن هذه اللذات الحسية حاصلة على سبيل التمام والكمال للبهائم والسباع من غير أن يكون الإنسان أخس الحيوانات. ولما كان هذا معلوم الفساد بالبديهة، ثبت: أن هذه اللذات الحسية، ليست موجبة للبهجة والسعادة.
Chapter Twenty-Two
On explaining that rational pleasures are nobler and more complete than sensory pleasures
Know that the prevailing disposition among common natures is the judgment that the strongest and most complete happinesses are the pleasure of eating and intercourse. Therefore, the majority of people worship God Almighty only to find delicious foods in the Hereafter, and to find desirable sexual partners there.
This saying is rejected by the verifiers among the people of wisdom and the practitioners of spiritual discipline.
Evidence indicates it through several points:
The first proof: If human happiness were dependent on fulfilling appetite and executing anger, then the animal that is stronger in this regard than man would be nobler than him. But the camel eats more than people, the wolf is stronger in harming than man, and the bird is stronger in copulation than man. So it would be necessary that these things be nobler than man. But the consequent is known to be false necessarily, so it is necessary to be certain that human happiness is not dependent on these matters.
The second proof: Everything that is a cause for the attainment of perfection and happiness—the more that thing is obtained, the more happiness and perfection are obtained. So if fulfilling the appetite of the stomach and genitals were a cause for the perfection of man's state and his happiness, then the more a man engages in fulfilling the appetite of the stomach and genitals and is absorbed in it, the higher his rank and more complete his virtue. But the consequent is false. Because a man who makes his life a consecrated devotion to eating, drinking, and copulation is counted among the beasts and is judged to be base and low. All that indicates that engaging in fulfilling these two appetites is not among happinesses and perfections, but rather among repelling needs and harms.
The third proof: All base animals share with man in the pleasure of eating and drinking. For just as man takes pleasure in eating sugar, so too does the dung beetle take pleasure in eating dung. So if these bodily pleasures were the greatest happiness for man, it would be necessary that man have no virtue over these base animals. Rather, we add and say: If human happiness were dependent on these sensory pleasures, it would be necessary that man be the basest of animals. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
And the explanation of the entailment: base animals share with man in these sensory bodily pleasures, except that man is diminished in these pursuits because of intellect. For the intellect is called 'aql because it is an 'iqāl (restraint) for him, holding him back from most of what he desires and to which his nature inclines. So if the estimation is that the perfection of happiness is only in these sensory pleasures, and then we have shown that these sensory pleasures are obtained completely and perfectly by beasts and predators without man being the basest of animals—and since this is known to be false by intuition, it is established that these sensory pleasures do not cause delight and happiness.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument presenting three proofs that sensory pleasures are not the highest form of human happiness. The author begins by stating the common misconception: most people believe the most powerful happiness lies in eating and intercourse, even worshipping God only for these rewards in the afterlife. This view is rejected by the verifiers among philosophers and spiritual practitioners. The author then provides three arguments.

First proof: If human happiness were tied to satisfying appetite and anger, then animals stronger in these areas would be nobler than humans. But the camel eats more, the wolf is more harmful, and the bird copulates more—yet these animals are not nobler. Therefore, happiness is not in these things.

Second proof: If fulfilling these appetites causes perfection and happiness, then the more one indulges, the more perfect one should become. But the glutton and lecher are considered base and beastly, not virtuous. Therefore, these activities are not sources of happiness or perfection but merely necessities.

Third proof: Base animals share in sensory pleasures, so if these were the greatest happiness, man would have no superiority over them. In fact, man would be the basest because his intellect restrains him from full indulgence. Since this is absurd, sensory pleasures are not the cause of true delight and happiness.

Key Terms
لذة عقلية
لذة حسية
سعادة
شهوة
غضب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Razi rejects bodily pleasures entirely.
Correction: Al-Razi only argues that these pleasures are not the ultimate happiness; they may still have value.

Checkpoint
What is the structure of the first proof?
A) A direct deduction that animals are nobler.
B) A reductio ad absurdum that if happiness were sensory, animals would be nobler, which is false.
C) An analogy between humans and animals.
D) A proof by authority from philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that if human happiness were sensory, animals would be nobler, which is false; thus happiness is not sensory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you ever catch yourself thinking more about the reward (food, spouse in Paradise) than about the One you are standing before? What would it take to shift your heart from the gift to the Giver?
?
Consider the camel, wolf, and bird that exceed you in eating, harming, and mating. Do you feel any envy or sense of lack? If not, why? And if their superiority in these does not make them nobler, what does that imply about the things you chase for happiness?
?
If your faith were stripped of all promises of sensory delights in the Hereafter, would your love for God remain? Be honest: what would remain? And what does your answer reveal about the true object of your worship?

The Second Proof: Indulgence Does Not Bring Perfection
Second proof using the principle of proportionate causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's second argument: if sensory pleasures caused perfection, then greater indulgence would mean greater virtue, but the opposite is true.

Core Idea
If sensory pleasures caused happiness, then gluttons would be the most perfect people.

Al-Razi's second argument uses a causal principle: if something is a cause of perfection, then more of it should produce more perfection. Applying this to sensory pleasures leads to an absurd conclusion: the person who spends his life eating, drinking, and fornicating would be the highest in virtue. But we know such a person is considered base, proving these pleasures are not sources of happiness.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof.
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional: if A causes perfection, then more A means more perfection.
Al-Razi uses a general principle about causality.
2
Apply the principle to sensory pleasures.
If fulfilling appetite causes perfection, then the more one indulges, the more perfect one becomes. But in reality, the glutton is despised.
Contrast the logical implication with common sense.
3
Understand the conclusion.
Since the outcome is false, the premise is false: fulfilling these appetites is not a cause of happiness, but merely a way to repel harm.
This argument refutes the idea that indulgence leads to happiness.

On explaining that rational pleasures are nobler and more complete than sensory pleasures

Checkpoint
What principle does al-Razi use in the second proof?
A) If something causes perfection, more of it causes less perfection.
B) If something causes perfection, more of it causes more perfection.
C) Sensory pleasures are the only cause of happiness.
D) Animals are more perfect than humans.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that if sensory pleasures caused happiness, then indulgence would increase virtue, but it doesn't, so they are not true causes of happiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to honestly tally your time and energy spent on pursuing food and sexual pleasure versus pursuing knowledge or worship, which would you say you value more as your ultimate happiness? What does that reveal about your understanding of perfection? Commit to a specific change this week to rebalance your priorities.
?
Al-Razi states that a person who makes his life devoted to eating, drinking, and copulation is 'counted among the beasts.' Look at your own daily habits: are there ways you quietly excuse indulgence as needed 'harm avoidance' when it might actually be overindulgence? Name one area where you will impose discipline starting tomorrow.
?
The proof uses the principle 'more cause yields more effect.' Apply this to your own spiritual life: is there a practice you believe brings you closer to Allah, yet you find that doing more of it actually makes you spiritually dull? What does that tell you about its true nature as a means to perfection? How will you adjust?

The Third Proof: Shared Base Pleasures and the Role of Intellect
Third proof using shared pleasures and the restraining role of intellect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi's third argument uses the fact that base animals share sensory pleasures and that intellect restrains humans, to prove that sensory pleasures cannot be the ultimate happiness.

Core Idea
If sensory pleasures were the highest good, then man would be the basest animal because his intellect restrains him.

Al-Razi's third argument points out that base animals also enjoy sensory pleasures—even the dung beetle enjoys its food. If these pleasures constituted the highest happiness, then humans would have no advantage over these animals. Worse still, because the intellect restrains humans from indulging fully, humans would be inferior to animals. This absurdity shows that sensory pleasures are not the cause of true delight.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof.
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the comparison between humans and base animals like the dung beetle.
Al-Razi uses the example of a dung beetle enjoying dung to show relativity of pleasure.
2
Understand the entailment.
If sensory pleasures were ultimate happiness, humans would have no superiority over animals. In fact, because intellect restrains them, they would be worse off.
The word 'aql (intellect) is said to be an 'iqāl (restraint).
3
Recognize the conclusion.
Since it is absurd to think humans are the basest animals, the premise that sensory pleasures are the highest happiness must be false.
Thus, sensory pleasures do not cause true delight and happiness.

Evidence indicates it through several points:
The first proof: If human happiness were dependent on fulfilling appetite and executing anger, then the animal that is stronger in this regard than man would be nobler than him. But the camel eats more than people, the wolf is stronger in harming than man, and the bird is stronger in copulation than man. So it would be necessary that these things be nobler than man. But the consequent is known to be false necessarily, so it is necessary to be certain that human happiness is not dependent on these matters.
The second proof: Everything that is a cause for the attainment of perfection and happiness—the more that thing is obtained, the more happiness and perfection are obtained. So if fulfilling the appetite of the stomach and genitals were a cause for the perfection of man's state and his happiness, then the more a man engages in fulfilling the appetite of the stomach and genitals and is absorbed in it, the higher his rank and more complete his virtue. But the consequent is false. Because a man who makes his life a consecrated devotion to eating, drinking, and copulation is counted among the beasts and is judged to be base and low. All that indicates that engaging in fulfilling these two appetites is not among happinesses and perfections, but rather among repelling needs and harms.
The third proof: All base animals share with man in the pleasure of eating and drinking. For just as man takes pleasure in eating sugar, so too does the dung beetle take pleasure in eating dung. So if these bodily pleasures were the greatest happiness for man, it would be necessary that man have no virtue over these base animals. Rather, we add and say: If human happiness were dependent on these sensory pleasures, it would be necessary that man be the basest of animals. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise.
And the explanation of the entailment: base animals share with man in these sensory bodily pleasures, except that man is diminished in these pursuits because of intellect. For the intellect is called 'aql because it is an 'iqāl (restraint) for him, holding him back from most of what he desires and to which his nature inclines. So if the estimation is that the perfection of happiness is only in these sensory pleasures, and then we have shown that these sensory pleasures are obtained completely and perfectly by beasts and predators without man being the basest of animals—and since this is known to be false by intuition, it is established that these sensory pleasures do not cause delight and happiness.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument presenting three proofs that sensory pleasures are not the highest form of human happiness. The author begins by stating the common misconception: most people believe the most powerful happiness lies in eating and intercourse, even worshipping God only for these rewards in the afterlife. This view is rejected by the verifiers among philosophers and spiritual practitioners. The author then provides three arguments.

First proof: If human happiness were tied to satisfying appetite and anger, then animals stronger in these areas would be nobler than humans. But the camel eats more, the wolf is more harmful, and the bird copulates more—yet these animals are not nobler. Therefore, happiness is not in these things.

Second proof: If fulfilling these appetites causes perfection and happiness, then the more one indulges, the more perfect one should become. But the glutton and lecher are considered base and beastly, not virtuous. Therefore, these activities are not sources of happiness or perfection but merely necessities.

Third proof: Base animals share in sensory pleasures, so if these were the greatest happiness, man would have no superiority over them. In fact, man would be the basest because his intellect restrains him from full indulgence. Since this is absurd, sensory pleasures are not the cause of true delight and happiness.

Key Terms
لذة حسية
بهجة
عقل
عقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the intellect is a hindrance to happiness.
Correction: Al-Razi uses intellect's restraining role to argue that if sensory pleasures were ultimate, then intellect would be a disadvantage, but since that's false, sensory pleasures are not ultimate.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi say that man would be the basest animal if sensory pleasures were the highest happiness?
A) Because man's senses are weaker than animals.
B) Because intellect restrains man from indulging fully, unlike animals.
C) Because man does not enjoy dung.
D) Because animals worship God more.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that if sensory pleasures were the ultimate happiness, humans would be inferior to animals due to intellectual restraint; therefore, true happiness must be rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you prioritize sensory pleasure over what your intellect tells you is right, are you not reducing yourself to the level of a dung beetle? What specific pleasure have you indulged in lately that you knew was beneath you?
?
Your intellect restrains you from many desires—do you resent that restraint, or do you see it as Allah's mercy lifting you above the animals? How can you embrace the 'iqāl rather than fight it?
?
If true happiness is not in sensory pleasures, then what have you been chasing all your life? Pause and name one thing you pursue that, if you were honest, is just a sugar-coated dung. What will you replace it with today?

The Eighth Argument: Shame and Sensual Acts
Razi argues that people naturally feel shame about sensual acts, proving they are not noble.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's eighth argument against the ultimate goodness of sensual pleasures, based on natural shame and social norms.

Core Idea
Shame reveals that sensual acts cannot be true perfection.

Razi presents a compelling argument from common human experience: we hide certain acts and never boast about them, while true excellences are proudly displayed. This lesson explores that argument and its three supporting observations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very structured argument with clear evidence.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that anything truly perfect and happy is proudly displayed without shame. Identify this core principle.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Examine the three pieces of evidence
Razi provides three observations: no one boasts about sensual acts, they are done in private, and references to them are used as insults. List each one and see how they support the claim.
Each evidence reinforces the idea that these acts are base.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument matters for the broader discussion of ultimate happiness. How does shame disqualify these acts from being true perfection?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : كل شيء يكون في نفسه كمالاً وسعادة ، وجب أن لا يستحي من إظهاره . ويتبجح بفعله. ونحن لا نعلم بالضرورة أن أحداً من العقلاء لا يفتخر بكثرة الأكل ، ولا بكثرة المباشرة ، ولا بكونه مستغرق الوقت والزمان في هذه الأعمال ، [ وأيضاً : (۱) ] فالناس لا يقدمون على الوقاع إلا في الخلوة ، وأما عند حضور الناس فلا . فإن أحداً من العقلاء لا يجد من نفسه تجويز الإقدام عليه ، وذلك يدل على أنه تقرر في عقول الخلق أنه فعل خسيس ، وعمل قبيح ، فيجب إخفاؤه عن العيون . وأيضاً : فقد جرت عادة السفهاء بأنه لا يشتم بعضهم بعضاً إلا بذكر ألفاظ الوقاع ، وذلك يدل على أنه مرتبة خسيسة ، ودرجة قبيحة . وأيضاً : لو أن واحداً من السفهاء أخذ يحكي عند حضور الجمع العظيم : أن فلاناً كيف يواقع زوجته ؟ فإن ذلك الرجل يستحي من ذلك الكلام ، ويتأذى من ذلك القائل . وكل هذا يدل على أن ذلك الفعل ليس من الكمالات والسعادات ، بل هو عمل باطل وفعل قبيح
The eighth proof: Everything that is in itself perfection and happiness must not be ashamed of being displayed and must be boasted of in its performance. And we know by necessity that none of the rational people boast of much eating, nor of much sexual intercourse, nor of being absorbed in time and time in these acts. [Also: (1)] People do not engage in sexual intercourse except in private, not in the presence of others. For no rational person finds in himself permission to do it in public. This indicates that it is established in the minds of people that it is a base act and an ugly deed, so it must be hidden from eyes. Also, it is the habit of fools that they do not insult each other except by mentioning words of sexual intercourse, and that indicates it is a base rank and ugly degree. Also, if one of the fools were to narrate in the presence of a great gathering how so-and-so has intercourse with his wife, then that man would be ashamed of that speech and harmed by that speaker. All this indicates that this act is not among perfections and happinesses, but rather a vain deed and an ugly act.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This is the eighth argument, based on natural shame and social sanctions. Razi argues that anything truly excellent is boasted about openly. But nobody boasts about eating too much or having frequent sex; such things are done in private and even the mention of them is considered insulting. Therefore, these acts cannot be true happiness. The argument uses three pieces of evidence: (1) no one prides themselves on these acts, (2) people hide them, (3) using words for these acts is a common insult. All indicate their baseness.

Key Terms
إظهار
الخلوة
السفهاء
القبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument claims that all bodily pleasures are evil or forbidden.
Correction: The argument does not claim that bodily pleasures are evil, but that they cannot be the ultimate good because of the shame associated with them.

Checkpoint
What reason does Razi give for why sensual acts are not true perfection?
A) They are forbidden by religious law.
B) People feel shame and do not boast about them.
C) They are only found in animals.
D) They cause physical harm.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument from shame shows that sensual pleasures are not true perfections because they are hidden and not boasted about.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you hid an action or desire because you felt ashamed. What does that shame reveal about its worth in light of Razi's argument? Will you pursue it again, or seek what you would boast about openly?
?
If you were to boast about something you did today, would it be a physical pleasure or an act of character? What does your answer tell you about what you truly value? Ask yourself: am I investing in what I would be proud to display before others?
?
Razi says no rational person boasts about sensual indulgence. Are you willing to examine your own habits and see where you secretly boast or feel shame? Will you allow the shame to guide you toward what is truly noble, or will you ignore it?

The Ninth Argument: Value and Appetite in Animals and Humans
Razi argues that even animals are valued more when they show less inclination to sensual pleasures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the example of animals to argue that minimizing sensual pleasures is a sign of excellence, and how this applies to rational humans.

Core Idea
Even animals are valued more when they show less appetite and more trainability.

In this passage, Razi presents his ninth argument, an analogy drawn from the animal kingdom. He observes that among horses and donkeys, those with stronger appetites and less trainability are valued less, while those with minimal desires and quick acceptance of training are prized. From this, he draws a lesson for rational humans: just as the value of animals lies in controlling their base instincts, the perfection of the rational soul must also involve minimizing sensual pleasures.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice the contrast between the well-trained horse and the untrained one.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key observation
Look for the pattern: what quality in an animal leads to higher value? Compare the two descriptions of horses.
The key is less appetite and more trainability.
3
Examine the conclusion
Notice how Razi moves from the specific example to a general principle about all non-speaking animals, and then applies it to the rational animal (human).
The rhetorical question at the end is the punchline.
4
Consider the implication
Reflect on what this argument suggests about human perfection. Why would minimizing appetites be a sign of excellence?
This connects to broader themes in Islamic ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : كل فرس أو حمار ، كان ميله إلى الأكل والشرب والإيذاء أكثر ، وكان قبوله للرياضة أقل : كانت قيمته أقل . وكل حيوان كان أقل رغبة في الأكل والشرب ، وكان أسرع قبولاً للرياضة : كانت قيمته أكثر . إلا ترى أن الفرس الذي يقبل الرياضة والعدو الشديد ، فإنه يشترى بثمن رفيع . وكل فرس لا يقبل هذه الرياضة ، فإنه يوضع على ظهره الإكاف . ویسوى بينه وبين الحمار ، ولا يشترى إلا بالثمن القليل . فلما كانت الحيوانات التي هي غير ناطقة ، لا تظهر فضائلها بسبب الأكل والشرب والوقاع ، بل بسبب تقليلها منه ، ويسبب قبول الأدب وحسن الخدمة لمولاه ، فما ظنك بالحيوان الناطق العاقل
The ninth proof: Every horse or donkey, the more its inclination to eating, drinking, and harming, and the less its acceptance of training, the lower its value. And every animal that has less desire for eating and drinking and is quicker to accept training has higher value. Do you not see that the horse that accepts training and intense running is purchased at a high price? And every horse that does not accept this training is made to carry a pack-saddle, is equated with the donkey, and is only bought for a low price. Since these non-speaking animals do not show their virtues through eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse, but rather through minimizing them and through accepting discipline and good service to their master, what then do you think about the speaking, rational animal?
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This is the ninth argument, an analogy from animals. Razi points out that even among irrational animals, the value increases when they have less appetite and more trainability. A horse that is eager to eat and drink but resistant to training is worth little, while one that is disciplined and fast is valuable. The virtues of animals are shown by minimizing base appetites and accepting training. If this is true for irrational animals, then certainly it is more so for rational humans. Therefore, human perfection cannot lie in indulging appetites.

Key Terms
الرياضة
القيمة
الحيوان الناطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the intrinsic value of animals.
Correction: Actually, the argument uses animal value as an analogy to show that minimizing appetites is a sign of excellence, applicable to humans.

Checkpoint
What is the author's point in the ninth argument?
A) Horses are better than donkeys.
B) Animals with fewer appetites are more valuable.
C) Humans should eat less than animals.
D) Rational animals are those that eat little.

Key Takeaway
The value of a creature is not based on the intensity of its appetites but on its ability to control them and accept training, and this principle applies even more to rational humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your daily habits, how much time do you spend chasing food, entertainment, and comfort versus training your mind, character, and faith? If your value were measured by this ratio, would you be a high-value horse or a pack-horse? What concrete step will you take today to increase your 'trainability'?
?
Razi says that even animals show virtue by minimizing desires. Think of one desire you struggle to control—whether it be for food, sleep, entertainment, or status. If you mastered that desire, how would your relationship with Allah and others improve? What is the smallest act of self-discipline you can commit to right now?
?
The argument ends with a rhetorical question: 'What then do you think about the rational, speaking animal?' You are that rational animal. Are you using your reason to elevate yourself above your appetites, or are you letting your appetites dictate your life? In one area where you have a choice today, will you choose discipline or indulgence?

The Eighth Proof: Shame Reveals Bodily Pleasures Are Base
Author presents eighth proof that bodily pleasures are not perfections using evidence from universal shame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eighth proof uses the universal human sense of shame to argue that bodily pleasures are not true perfections.

Core Idea
Shame reveals that bodily pleasures are base, not noble.

This lesson presents the eighth proof from the author against the idea that bodily pleasures are perfections. The argument draws on universal human experience: we instinctively hide certain acts and feel shame when they are exposed. By examining this common reaction, the author shows that these acts cannot be true perfections or sources of happiness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the opening principle to the examples.
The author uses several concrete observations to support his claim.
2
Identify the main claim and the evidence
The main claim is that bodily pleasures are not perfections. The evidence consists of: no boasting, privacy of acts, insults using related terms, and embarrassment when such acts are recounted.
Each piece of evidence reinforces the same conclusion.
3
Reflect on the examples
Think about the examples given: excessive eating, frequent intercourse, insults, and recounting intimate behavior. Why does each point to baseness?
The universality of these reactions is key.
4
Understand why shame indicates baseness
Shame and concealment are responses to what is considered low or ugly. If an act were a perfection, it would be proudly displayed. The opposite reactions show these acts are not perfections.
This connects to the broader topic of what constitutes true perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : كل شيء يكون في نفسه كمالاً وسعادة ، وجب أن لا يستحي من إظهاره . ويتبجح بفعله. ونحن لا نعلم بالضرورة أن أحداً من العقلاء لا يفتخر بكثرة الأكل ، ولا بكثرة المباشرة ، ولا بكونه مستغرق الوقت والزمان في هذه الأعمال ، [ وأيضاً : (۱) ] فالناس لا يقدمون على الوقاع إلا في الخلوة ، وأما عند حضور الناس فلا . فإن أحداً من العقلاء لا يجد من نفسه تجويز الإقدام عليه ، وذلك يدل على أنه تقرر في عقول الخلق أنه فعل خسيس ، وعمل قبيح ، فيجب إخفاؤه عن العيون . وأيضاً : فقد جرت عادة السفهاء بأنه لا يشتم بعضهم بعضاً إلا بذكر ألفاظ الوقاع ، وذلك يدل على أنه مرتبة خسيسة ، ودرجة قبيحة . وأيضاً : لو أن واحداً من السفهاء أخذ يحكي عند حضور الجمع العظيم : أن فلاناً كيف يواقع زوجته ؟ فإن ذلك الرجل يستحي من ذلك الكلام ، ويتأذى من ذلك القائل . وكل هذا يدل على أن ذلك الفعل ليس من الكمالات والسعادات ، بل هو عمل باطل وفعل قبيح
The eighth proof: Everything that is in itself perfection and happiness must not be ashamed of its manifestation and should be boasted of in its doing. And we know by necessity that no rational person boasts of excessive eating, nor of excessive intercourse, nor of being engrossed in these acts. [Also:] People do not engage in intercourse except in seclusion, but in the presence of people they do not. For no rational person finds in himself the allowance to proceed with it, and that indicates that it is established in the minds of people that it is a base act and an ugly deed, so it must be hidden from sight. Also: It is the custom of foolish people that they insult each other only by mentioning words of intercourse, and that indicates that it is a base rank and an ugly degree. Also: If one of the foolish people were to recount in the presence of a great gathering how so-and-so has intercourse with his wife, that man would be ashamed of that speech and would be hurt by that speaker. All of this indicates that this act is not among perfections and happiness, but rather it is a vain deed and an ugly action.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof against the claim that bodily pleasures are perfections. The argument proceeds by noting that anything genuinely perfect is proudly displayed, not hidden. The author observes that no rational person boasts of eating or intercourse, and people only engage in such acts in private. Furthermore, insults often involve references to such acts, and describing another's intimate behavior causes shame. These observations collectively demonstrate that such acts are base, not perfections.

The Ninth Proof: Animal Discipline as Moral Evidence
The author presents the ninth proof using animal value as analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the value of domestic animals, when judged by their willingness to be trained, provides an analogy for human virtue.

Discipline raises value in animals; the same applies to rational humans.

This lesson examines the ninth proof from the author, which draws a striking analogy from everyday experience. We observe that horses and donkeys are valued more when they submit to training and restrain their appetites. This simple observation, the author argues, points to a universal principle that must hold even more strongly for rational beings.

The Tenth Proof: Perfection Through Knowledge and Character
The author presents the tenth proof based on regional differences in knowledge and character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tenth proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which argues that human perfection consists in true knowledge and virtuous character, as evidenced by regional differences in civilization.

Core Idea
Human perfection is revealed through knowledge and character, not location.

This lesson presents the tenth proof from the author's series. He uses an observational argument: people at the earth's edges lack knowledge and virtue, while those in central regions possess them and are considered superior. This leads to a key conclusion about the nature of human perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author contrasts two groups.
2
Identify the two groups
Find the description of the peripheral inhabitants (Zanj and Saqaliba) and the central inhabitants. What are their characteristics?
The author uses this contrast as evidence.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that human perfection is not based on location but on true knowledge and virtuous character. Ask yourself why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : إن سكان أطراف الأرض ، لما لم تكمل معارفهم وأخلاقهم وعقولهم ، لا جرم كانوا في نهاية الخسة والدناءة . ألا ترى أن سكان الإقليم الأول ، وهم الزنوج ، وسكان [ الإقليم السابع وهم الصقالبة ، لما قل نصيبهم من المعارف [ الإلهيه (۲) ] الحقيقية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة ، لا جرم تقرر في عقول العقلاء : خسة درجاتهم ، ودناءة مراتبهم ، وأما سكان وسط المعمورة ، لما فازوا بالمعارف الحقيقية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة ، لا جرم أقر كل واحد ، بأنهم أفضل طوائف البشر وأكملهم . وذلك يدل على أن فضيلة الإنسان وكماله ، لا تظهر إلا بالعلوم الحقيقية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة .
The tenth proof: The inhabitants of the ends of the earth, since their knowledge, character, and intellects are not perfected, are necessarily at the utmost baseness and degradation. Do you not see that the inhabitants of the first clime, who are the Zanj, and the inhabitants of the seventh clime, who are the Saqaliba, since their share of true divine knowledge and virtuous character is small, it is established in the minds of rational people the baseness of their ranks and the degradation of their stations. As for the inhabitants of the middle of the inhabited world, since they attain true knowledge and virtuous character, everyone acknowledges that they are the best and most perfect of human groups. That indicates that the virtue and perfection of a human appear only through true sciences and virtuous character.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof, an argument from observation of different regions. The author notes that people at the edges of the earth, like the Zanj and Saqaliba, lack perfected knowledge and character, and are considered base. In contrast, those in the middle of the inhabited world have true knowledge and virtuous character, and are deemed the best. This proves that human perfection is not found in bodily pleasures but in true sciences and virtuous character.

Key Terms
معارف
أخلاق
فضيلة
خسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human perfection depends on geographical location or race.
Correction: The author uses regional differences only as evidence; what matter are knowledge and character. Perfection is not inherent to any region or people.

Checkpoint
According to the tenth proof, what determines human perfection?
A) Geographical location.
B) Wealth and status.
C) True knowledge and virtuous character.
D) Physical strength.

Key Takeaway
The tenth proof shows human perfection does not come from bodily pleasures but from true sciences and virtuous ethics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you satisfied to remain in the 'periphery' of intellectual and moral mediocrity, making excuses for your lack of growth, or will you take concrete steps to acquire true knowledge and cultivate virtuous character, even if it costs you comfort?
?
In what area of your life have you blamed your environment—your upbringing, society, or circumstances—for your shortcomings, when this proof shows that perfection is achieved through personal effort in seeking knowledge and virtue? What is one specific habit you will change starting today?
?
If human perfection is only through true sciences and virtuous character, then what is the one book you will read or the one character trait you will actively work on this week to move from the 'edge' toward the 'center' of human excellence? How will you hold yourself accountable?

Human excellence stems from true knowledge and virtuous morals
Argument that inhabitants of the middle climate are most excellent due to true knowledge and morals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human excellence, according to the author, is not determined by race or climate but by the acquisition of true divine knowledge and virtuous morals.

Core Idea
True knowledge and virtuous morals are the real sources of human excellence.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a climatic argument to illustrate a deeper point about human perfection. The author contrasts the inhabitants of extreme climates with those of the middle, not to endorse environmental determinism, but to show that what truly elevates humans is their share of divine sciences and moral virtues. As you read, notice how the argument moves from observation to a universal conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a reference to morals and intellects, then moves into a comparison.
2
Identify the two groups compared
The author contrasts two sets of people: inhabitants of the first and seventh climates (Zanj and Slavs) and inhabitants of the middle climate. Note what each group is said to possess or lack.
The lack is of 'true divine sciences and virtuous morals' for the extreme climates.
3
Understand the causal claim
The author claims that the lack of knowledge and morals leads to baseness, while possession leads to being considered the best of humans. This is a cause-effect relationship.
The phrase 'لا جرم' indicates an inevitable consequence.
4
Extract the final conclusion
The last sentence states that human virtue and perfection do not appear except through true sciences and virtuous morals. This is the main takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخلاقهم وعقولهم ، لا جرم كانوا في نهاية الخسة والدناءة . ألا ترى أن سكان الإقليم الأول ، وهم الزنوج ، وسكان الإقليم السابع وهم الصقالبة ، لما قل نصيبهم من المعارف الإلهية الحقيقية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة ، لا جرم تقرر في عقول العقلاء : خسة درجاتهم ، ودناءة مراتبهم ، وأما سكان وسط المعمورة ، لما فازوا بالمعارف الحقيقية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة ، لا جرم أقر كل واحد ، بأنهم أفضل طوائف البشر وأكملهم . وذلك يدل على أن فضيلة الإنسان وكماله ، لا تظهر إلا بالعلوم الحقيقية ، والأخلاق الفاضلة .
And their morals and intellects, inevitably they were at the utmost baseness and lowness. Do you not see that the inhabitants of the first climate, who are the Zanj, and the inhabitants of the seventh climate, who are the Slavs, when their share of true divine sciences and virtuous morals was little, inevitably it was established in the minds of the intelligent: the lowness of their ranks and the baseness of their stations. And as for the inhabitants of the middle of the inhabited world, when they attained true sciences and virtuous morals, inevitably everyone acknowledged that they are the best of human groups and the most perfect. And that indicates that the virtue and perfection of man do not appear except through true sciences and virtuous morals.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument that human excellence is tied to true knowledge and virtuous morals. The author uses a geographical observation: inhabitants of the first and seventh climates (the Zanj and Slavs) have little share of divine sciences and virtuous morals, and thus are base. In contrast, inhabitants of the middle climates possess these, and are universally acknowledged as the best and most perfect humans. The conclusion is that human virtue and perfection only manifest through true sciences and virtuous morals. The argument assumes a correlation between climate and access to knowledge, but the logical point is that knowledge and morals are the real determinants of human excellence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human excellence is determined by race, climate, or geography.
Correction: The passage argues that climate is not the direct cause; rather, it is the acquisition of true divine sciences and virtuous morals that determines excellence. The climatic example serves only to illustrate this point.

A) They are innate and independent of external factors.
B) They depend solely on climate.
C) They appear only through true sciences and virtuous morals.
D) They are limited to inhabitants of the middle climate.

Key Takeaway
Human virtue and perfection are attained only through true sciences and virtuous morals.

?
This lesson says that your perfection depends on these two things. If you were to die tomorrow, would you be counted among those with true sciences and virtuous morals? What will you change starting today?

Refuting the Objection on Animal Souls
Refutation of an objection about equality of animal and human souls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equality in immateriality does not imply equality in quiddity, and how this refutes an objection against animal souls being immaterial.

Core Idea
Equality in a negative predicate does not guarantee equality in complete essence.

This passage opens a chapter on the souls of animals. The author first presents the philosophers' view that animal souls are immaterial, then addresses a potential objection: if animal souls are immaterial, they would be equal to human souls in quiddity. The author refutes this by pointing out that immateriality is a negative predicate, and equality in negative predicates does not necessitate equality in quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening and philosophers' claim
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to where the objection is raised. Note the author's description of the philosophers' view and their lack of proof.
The philosophers agreed that animal souls are immaterial but provided no proof.
2
Identify the objection and the response
Find the objection introduced by 'وليس لأحد أن يقول' and the response beginning with 'فإنا نقول'. Understand how the author refutes it.
The objection claims immateriality would make animal souls equal to human souls in quiddity.
3
Reflect on the concept of negative predicate
Consider why equality in a negative predicate (immateriality) does not imply equality in positive qualities like knowledge and morals.
A negative predicate only negates something; it does not define positive attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث والثلاثون في البحث عن نفوس سائر الحيوانات . أما الفلاسفة المتأخرون فقد اتفقوا على أنها قوى جسمانية ، وأنه يكون لها نفوس مجردة . ولم يذكروا في تقرير هذا حجة ولا شبهة . وليس لأحد أن يقول : لو كانت نفوسها نفوساً مجردة ، لوجب كونها مساوية للنفوس البشرية في تمام الماهية . ويلزم وقوع الاستواء في العلوم والأخلاق ، وذلك محال . فإنا نقول : الاستواء في التجرد استواء في قيد سلبي . وقد عرفت أن الاستواء في القيود السلبية لا يوجب الاستواء في تمام الماهية . وأما سائر الناس فقد اختلفوا في أنه : هل لها نفوس مجردة ؟ وهل لها شيء من القوى العقلية أم لا ؟ فزعمت طائفة من أهل النظر ومن أهل الأثر : أن ذلك ثابت . واحتجوا على صحته بالمعقول والمنقول . أما المعقول فهو أنهم قالوا : إنا نشاهد من هذه الحيوانات أفعالاً لا تصدر إلا من أفاضل العقلاء ، وذلك يدل على أن معها قدراً من العقل . وبينوا ذلك بوجوه : الأول : إن الفأرة تدخل ذنبها في قارورة الدهن ، ثم تلحسه . وهذا الفعل لا يصدر إلا لعلمها بمجموع مقدمات :
The thirty-third chapter on the investigation of the souls of all other animals. As for the later philosophers, they agreed that they are material forces, and that they have immaterial souls. And they did not mention in establishing this any proof or even a doubtful argument. And no one should say: If their souls were immaterial souls, they would necessarily be equal to human souls in complete quiddity, and equality in sciences and morals would necessarily occur, and that is impossible. For we say: Equality in immateriality is equality in a negative predicate. And you have known that equality in negative predicates does not necessitate equality in complete quiddity. As for other people, they differed concerning whether they have immaterial souls and whether they have any rational faculty or not. So a group of the people of theoretical inquiry and the people of transmitted tradition claimed that this is established. They argued for its validity by reason and transmitted evidence. As for the rational, they said: We observe from these animals actions that only issue from the most excellent of rational people, and that indicates that they possess a measure of intellect. And they explained this by several faces: The first: The mouse inserts its tail into the oil flask, then licks it. And this action does not issue except from its knowledge of a set of premises:
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is the opening of a chapter on animal souls. It first presents the view of later philosophers that animal souls are immaterial, though they provided no proof. Then it refutes a potential objection: if animal souls were immaterial, they would be equal to human souls in quiddity, requiring equality in knowledge and morals, which is absurd. The refutation points out that equality in a negative predicate (immateriality) does not imply equality in positive quiddity. The passage then turns to a group of scholars (theologians and traditionists) who affirm that animals have immaterial souls and rational powers, supporting their view with both reason and transmitted evidence. As a rational proof, they observe animals performing actions that only intelligent humans would do, indicating they have some intellect. The first example is a mouse inserting its tail into an oil flask and licking it, an action requiring knowledge of a set of premises. The argument assumes that such goal-directed behavior implies reasoning.

Key Terms
نفوس مجردة
قيد سلبي
تمام الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are both immaterial, they must have the same essence.
Correction: Immateriality is a negative predicate; being immaterial does not specify positive attributes. Two immaterial things can have different quiddities.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does equality in immateriality not imply equality in complete quiddity?
A) Because immaterial souls are not real.
B) Because immateriality is a negative predicate, and equality in negative predicates does not necessitate equality in quiddity.
C) Because human souls are not immaterial.
D) Because the philosophers provided no proof.

Key Takeaway
Immateriality is only a negation of materiality, so it does not determine the positive essence of a soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because two people share a common 'negative' trait—like not being a certain nationality or not holding a certain belief—they must be identical in their whole character? How does this lesson's distinction between negative predicates and complete essence challenge you to evaluate others more justly?
?
When you think about your own soul, do you sometimes reduce your identity to what you are not (sins you avoid, ideologies you reject) rather than what you actively are in your positive essence? How can you shift your focus from 'negative predicates' to building your complete quiddity in faith and morals?
?
This lesson warns against inferring equality in quiddity from equality in a negative predicate. Can you identify an area in your life where you have falsely equated shared denials (e.g., 'we both reject X') with full agreement or similarity? What accountability do you have to seek positive common ground rather than resting on shared negatives?

Rational Proof of Animal Intellect: The Mouse Example
Presentation of rational proof for animal intellect using the mouse example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that animals possess intellect, as exemplified by the mouse's action of using its tail to retrieve oil.

Core Idea
Goal-directed, multi-step actions in animals indicate rational reasoning.

After refuting an objection about animal souls, the author presents the view of a group of scholars who affirm that animals have immaterial souls and rational powers. They support this with both rational and transmitted evidence. The first rational evidence they provide is the remarkable behavior of a mouse inserting its tail into an oil flask to retrieve oil, an action that requires understanding of cause and effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the claim and the rational argument
Read the passage from 'وأما سائر الناس' to the end. Notice that the group of scholars affirm animal souls and rational faculties, and they argue from observation.
They claim that observed animal actions only come from the most excellent rational people.
2
Understand the mouse example
The mouse inserts its tail into an oil flask, then licks it. This action implies knowledge of a set of premises: that the tail can absorb oil, that licking the tail delivers oil, etc.
The passage does not list the premises; they are implied.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does the author consider this action evidence of intellect? What premises must the mouse have considered?
This action is multi-step and goal-oriented, suggesting reasoning.

Refuting the Equality Objection on Animal Souls
Refuting the claim that animal souls would equal human souls if immaterial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equality in a negative attribute like immateriality does not necessitate equality in complete essence.

Core Idea
Negative attributes do not confer positive essence; shared negation does not mean shared nature.

This passage presents a key argument in the debate over animal souls. The later philosophers held that animal souls are material, but an objection is raised: if they were immaterial, they would be equal to human souls. The response cleverly distinguishes between negative and positive attributes, showing that mere immateriality is a negation, not a positive quality that would force equality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the apparent contradiction in the philosophers' statement (material powers yet immaterial souls) – this is likely a scribal error.
The intended meaning is probably that they are material powers and do not have immaterial souls.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
Find the objection: if animal souls were immaterial, they would equal human souls in essence, leading to equality in knowledge and ethics. Understand why this seems plausible.
3
Analyze the rebuttal using negative attributes
The answer states that equality in immateriality is equality in a negative attribute (قيد سلبي). Negative attributes do not affect positive essence. Thus the objection fails.
This is a key logical distinction in Islamic philosophy.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this refutation is important: it preserves the difference between human and animal souls without denying the possibility of immaterial animal souls (though the philosophers deny that anyway).
The argument stands regardless of the textual issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة المتأخرون فقد اتفقوا على أنها قوى جسمانية ، وأنه يكون لها نفوس مجردة . ولم يذكروا في تقرير هذا حجة ولا شبهة . وليس لأحد أن يقول : لو كانت نفوسها نفوساً مجردة ، لوجب كونها مساوية للنفوس البشرية في تمام الماهية. ويلزم وقوع الاستواء في العلوم والأخلاق ، وذلك محال . فإنا نقول : الاستواء في التجرد استواء في قيد سلبي . وقد عرفت أن الاستواء في القيود السلبية لا يوجب الاستواء في تمام الماهية .
As for the later philosophers, they agreed that they are material powers, and that they have immaterial souls. And they did not mention in support of this any proof or specious argument. And no one may say: if their souls were immaterial souls, they would necessarily be equal to human souls in complete essence. And equality in sciences and morals would follow, and that is impossible. For we say: equality in immateriality is equality in a negative attribute. And you have known that equality in negative attributes does not necessitate equality in complete essence.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the view of later philosophers regarding animal souls and then refutes an objection. It begins by stating the philosophers' position, though the text appears contradictory: it says they agreed that animal souls are material powers and that they have immaterial souls. This is likely a scribal error; the intended meaning is probably that they agreed they are material powers and do not have immaterial souls, as the subsequent objection presupposes a claim of immateriality. The author then presents an objection: if animals had immaterial souls, they would be equal to humans in essence, leading to equality in knowledge and ethics, which is empirically false. The answer is that equality in the negative attribute of immateriality (which is a mere negation of being in a location) does not necessitate equality in the complete essence, because negative attributes do not confer positive content. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
نفوس مجردة
استواء
قيد سلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any shared attribute between two entities means they share the same essence.
Correction: Sharing a negative attribute (like immateriality) does not imply shared essence, because negative attributes do not determine positive content.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the response to the objection that if animals had immaterial souls they would be equal to humans?
A) That is true, so animals are equal to humans.
B) Equality in a negative attribute does not imply equality in essence.
C) Animals do not have immaterial souls.
D) The objection is invalid because animals are not rational.

Key Takeaway
Sharing a negative attribute like immateriality does not force two essences to be identical; negative attributes do not confer positive content.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you meet someone who shares a superficial trait with you (like a hobby or background), do you automatically assume you share a deeper connection? How can this lesson's distinction between negative and positive attributes help you avoid false assumptions about others?
?
You often define yourself by what you are not (e.g., 'I am not arrogant'). Are these negative attributes a true reflection of your essence, or do they obscure the positive qualities you should be cultivating? What one positive attribute can you focus on today?
?
Have you ever rejected a truth because it seemed to lead to an uncomfortable conclusion (like the objector rejecting animal immateriality)? Reflect on a time you did this. How can you train yourself to examine the reasoning rather than the conclusion?

The First Proof for Animal Intellect: The Mouse and the Oil Bottle
First rational proof for animal intellect using a mouse's action

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple animal action can be analyzed as a rational proof involving multiple premises, demonstrating that animals possess intellect.

Core Idea
A mouse's simple action of dipping its tail reveals a hidden syllogism.

This lesson introduces the first of three rational proofs for animal intellect. Theologians argue that even a seemingly instinctive behavior like a mouse licking oil from a bottle actually involves a chain of reasoning. By breaking down the action into premises, they show that animals engage in logical deduction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise logical structure.
The passage enumerates four premises explicitly.
2
Identify the four premises
List each premise: (1) need for oil, (2) head cannot enter, (3) tail can enter, (4) the method achieves the goal.
These premises lead to the conclusion that the mouse should act.
3
Understand the syllogistic reasoning
Combine the premises: a goal, constraints, available means, and effectiveness → rational action.
This mirrors human practical reasoning.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask: does this prove intellect beyond instinct? The author claims yes, because the mouse must 'know' these premises.
This is a key point in the debate on animal cognition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبينوا ذلك بوجوه : الأول : إن الفأرة تدخل ذنبها في قارورة الدهن ، ثم تلحسه . وهذا الفعل لا يصدر إلا لعلمها بمجموع مقدمات : فإحداها : أنها محتاجة إلى الدهن . وثانيها : أن رأسها لا يدخل في القارورة . وثالثها : أن ذنبها يدخل . ورابعها : أن المقصود حاصل بهذا الطريق ، فوجب الإقدام عليه .
And they explained that with several aspects: The first: The mouse inserts its tail into the oil jar, then licks it. And this action does not proceed except from its knowledge of a collection of premises: First: that it needs the oil. Second: that its head does not enter the jar. Third: that its tail enters. Fourth: that the purpose is achieved by this method, so it must undertake it.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three rational proofs for the existence of intellect in animals. The author explains that the action of a mouse dipping its tail into a jar of oil and then licking it demonstrates that the mouse possesses a set of premises that lead to a conclusion. The four premises are: (1) it needs oil, (2) its head cannot enter the jar, (3) its tail can enter, (4) the goal is achieved this way. Therefore, the mouse deduces that it should act accordingly. This reasoning shows that the mouse has something beyond instinct, namely a form of reasoning (qiyas) that rational beings perform.

Key Terms
فأرة
قارورة الدهن
مقدمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals act purely by instinct without any reasoning.
Correction: This argument shows that even a mouse's action involves deductive reasoning from premises, indicating intellect.

Checkpoint
How many premises does the mouse's action involve according to the text?
A) 2
B) 3
C) 4
D) 5

Key Takeaway
The mouse's tail-dipping and licking demonstrates that animals possess intellect by deducing from four premises that this action achieves its goal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The mouse used logic to overcome a physical limitation (its head couldn't enter). What spiritual limitation in your life are you trying to overcome with your own effort, when Allah has given you a different, more effective path? Will you take it?
?
If even a mouse is endowed with intellect to achieve its worldly needs, how much more accountable are you for using your intellect to secure your eternal need—the pleasure of Allah? What hinders you from applying logical reasoning to your faith?
?
The mouse knew its goal and the means. Do you know your ultimate goal in life? And are you using the means Allah provided (the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and your intellect) to achieve it, or are you distracted by lesser aims?
