The Geometric Proof: The Hexagon's Unique Benefits
Classification of tiling shapes and the hexagon's unique combination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hexagon is the only shape that simultaneously fills space and has wide angles, and how bees' choice of it demonstrates divine guidance.

Core Idea
The hexagon perfectly balances space-filling and wide angles, a feat no other shape achieves.

This lesson presents a geometric argument for divine design in nature. By examining the properties of shapes used for tiling, we see that the hexagon alone combines two crucial benefits. Bees, by building hexagonal cells, exhibit a geometric wisdom that points to a higher intelligence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on shape classification
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the argument. Then read the translation.
Notice the author breaks shapes into two categories.
2
Identify the two shape categories
Find the two groups: shapes that fill space but have narrow angles, and shapes that do not fill space despite wide angles.
3
Evaluate triangles and squares
Understand that triangles and squares fill space but their narrow angles waste corner space.
4
Evaluate heptagons and octagons
These have wide angles but leave gaps, wasting space.
5
Conclude with the hexagon
Only the hexagon fills space fully with wide angles, leading bees to choose it—a sign of divine inspiration.
This step connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن النحل يبني البيوت المسدسة . وهذا الشكل فيه منفعتان لا تحصلان إلا من المسدس . وتقريره : أن الأشكال على قسمين : منها أشكال متى ضم بعضها إلى بعض ، امتلأت القرصة منها، ومنها : أشكال ليست كذلك فالقسم الأول : كالمثلثات والمربعات فإنهما وإن امتلأت الفرصة منهما، إلا أن زواياهما ضيقة ، فتبقى معطلة . وأما المسبع والمثمن وغيرهما ، فزواياهم وإن كانت واسعة ، إلا أنه لا تملأ القرصة منهم ، بل يبقى بينهم فضاء معطل . وأما الشكل المستجمع لكلتي المنفعتين ، فليس إلا المسدس . وذلك لأن زواياه واسعة ، فلا يبقى شيء من الجوانب فيه معطلاً ، وإذا ضمت المسدسات بعضها إلى بعض ، لم يبق فيما بينها فرجة ضائعة . وإذا ثبت أن الشكل الموصوف بهاتين الصفتين هو المسدس ، لا جرم اختارت النحل بناء بيوتها على هذا الشكل ، ولولا أنه تعالى أعطاها من الإلهام والذكاء ، وإلا لما حصل هذا الأمر . وفيه أعجوبة ثانية . وهي : أن البشر لا يقدرون على بناء البيت المسدس إلا بالمسطر والفرجار . والنحل يبني تلك البيوت من غير حاجة إلى شيء من الآلات والأدوات .
And the second: The bee builds hexagonal houses. And this shape has two benefits that are not obtained except from the hexagon. Its explanation: shapes are of two types: those which, when some are joined to others, fill the space (al-qarsa), and those which are not. The first type: like triangles and squares; they fill the space, but their angles are narrow, so they remain wasted. As for the heptagon and octagon and others, their angles are wide, but they do not fill the space; rather there remains wasted space between them. And the shape that combines both benefits is only the hexagon. That is because its angles are wide, so no part of its sides is wasted, and when hexagons are joined together, no gap remains between them. And when it is established that the shape described by these two attributes is the hexagon, inevitably the bees chose to build their houses in this shape. And had God not given them inspiration and intelligence, this would not occur. And in it is a second wonder: that humans are not able to build a hexagonal house except with a ruler and compass. And the bees build those houses without needing any tools or instruments.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof, which is a geometric argument. The author classifies shapes used for tiling into two categories: those that fill the plane but have narrow angles (like triangles and squares, which waste corner space) and those that do not fill the plane (like heptagons and octagons, leaving gaps). The hexagon uniquely combines both benefits: it fills the plane completely (since 120° angles fit together) and has wide angles so no corner space is wasted. Bees, by building hexagonal cells, demonstrate an understanding of this property, which requires intelligence beyond mere instinct. The author adds a second wonder: humans need tools like rulers and compasses to construct hexagons, while bees do so effortlessly, proving they possess divinely inspired intellect.

Key Terms
بيت المسدس
منفعتان
زوايا
مسدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bees build hexagonal cells randomly or by pure instinct without any reasoning.
Correction: The geometric optimization shows that bees must be endowed with a form of intelligence or divine inspiration, as the hexagon's unique properties are not accidental.

Checkpoint
What shape combines both benefits of filling space and having wide angles?
A) Triangle
B) Square
C) Hexagon
D) Octagon

Key Takeaway
The hexagon is the only shape that both fills space and has wide angles, indicating a divine intelligence behind bees' construction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The bee's hexagon reveals a geometric perfection that no human engineer could improve. When was the last time you truly marveled at a sign of God's wisdom in creation, and did it change how you lived that day? What one thing in nature will you deliberately observe tomorrow to seek His design?
?
You now know that the hexagon uniquely combines two benefits. This means every detail in creation has purpose and precision. Which area of your life—your time, your relationships, your worship—have you assumed has no divine purpose? Will you now ask God to show you the wisdom He placed there?
?
The bees did not discover this on their own; God inspired them. Have you ever taken credit for an insight or skill without acknowledging that all intelligence comes from Him? How would your daily work and study change if you began each task with a conscious recognition that your ability to think is a gift from Allah?

The Second Wonder: Bees' Construction Without Tools
Contrasting human and bee construction abilities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional evidence of divine design: bees build perfect hexagons without tools, while humans require rulers and compasses.

Core Idea
Bees achieve what humans can only do with tools, proving their intelligence is divinely inspired.

After establishing the geometric necessity of the hexagon, the author presents a second wonder: bees accomplish this intricate construction without any tools or instruments. Humans, despite their intellect, cannot draw a perfect hexagon without a ruler and compass. This contrast further emphasizes the divine gift of inspiration to bees.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
This short passage contrasts human and bee abilities in constructing hexagons.
Focus on the comparison.
2
Identify the wonder
The wonder is that bees do not need tools like rulers and compasses, whereas humans do.
3
Reflect on the implication
This shows that bees possess a built-in knowledge or inspiration from God, surpassing human crafted methods.
Connect to the overall theme of divine design.

The Ant's Foresight as Proof of Animal Intellect
Citing ant behavior as evidence of animal intellect and foresight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ant's behavior of gathering provisions is used as evidence that animals possess knowledge and foresight.

Core Idea
Ants storing food shows they know the future—a sign of intellect.

This short passage presents the third rational proof for animal intelligence: the ant prepares provisions for itself in advance, which requires knowledge of future need. It then directs attention to the wonders of bee society, hinting at further evidence. Let's examine the ant argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then its translation. Notice the logical structure: the claim (ants gather provisions) and the reason (due to knowledge).
2
Identify the key term 'ذخيرة'
The word 'ذخيرة' means 'provision' or 'store'. Why does storing imply knowledge?
Consider that storing requires anticipation of future need.
3
Reflect on the bee reference
The author mentions bee wonders as additional evidence. Why might he do this?
It shows the argument from ants is not isolated but part of a broader pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن النملة تسعى في إعداد الذخيرة لنفسها ، وما ذاك إلا لعلمها . واعلم : أن عجائب أحوال النحل في رئاسته ، وفي تدبيره لأحوال الرعية ، وفي كيفية خدمة الرعية لذلك الرئيس : كثيرة ، مذكورة في كتاب الحيوان .
The third: The ant strives to prepare provisions for itself, and that is only due to its knowledge. And know: the wonders of the conditions of bees in their leadership, and in their management of the subjects, and in how the subjects serve that leader, are many, mentioned in the Book of Animals.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage presents the third rational proof: ants gather and store food for the future, which requires knowledge that they will need sustenance later. This foresight indicates some form of intellect. The author then concludes the series of proofs by directing attention to the wonders of bee society—their leadership, management, and service—which are detailed in another work, Kitab al-Hayawan (The Book of Animals), implying that these phenomena further support the presence of intelligence in animals.

The Bear's Clever Strategies
The author enumerates three clever behaviors of bears as examples of instinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three examples of instinctive intelligence in bears as described by the author.

Core Idea
Instinct guides animals to perform complex actions without learning.

This passage presents the sixth example of animal instincts, focusing on the bear. The author describes three distinct behaviors that demonstrate how the bear uses clever tactics to hunt, defend itself, and obtain food. Each behavior illustrates a level of intelligence that points to a guiding instinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own descriptions come through before interpreting.
Notice the three distinct strategies are separated by 'Also' (وأيضاً).
2
Identify each strategy
Find each of the three behaviors the bear performs. Note what the bear does in each case and what the outcome is.
3
Reflect on instinct
Consider why these behaviors are seen as instinctive rather than learned. What does each strategy require the bear to know?
Think about how the bear 'knows' to lie in the bull's path or to use a stick.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
This example is part of a larger argument that instinct points to a Creator. Ask yourself how these behaviors support that idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن الدب إذا أراد أن يفترس الثور ، علم أنه لا يمكنه أن يقصده ظاهراً . فيقال : إنه يستلقي في ممر ذلك الثور ، فإذا قرب ذلك الثور ، جعل قرنيه فيما بين ذراعيه ، ولا يزال ينهش ما بين ذراعيه حتى يثخنه وأيضاً : إنه يأخذ العصا ويضرب الإنسان ، حتى يتوهم أنه مات فيتركه . وأيضاً : يصعد الشجر أخف صعود ، ويأخذ الجوز بين كفيه ، ويضرب ما في أحد كفيه على ما في الكف الآخر ، ثم ينفخ فيه ، ويزيل القشور ويأكل اللب
The sixth: When the bear wants to prey upon the bull, it knows that it cannot approach it openly. So it is said: It lies down in the path of that bull, and when the bull approaches, it places its horns between its forelegs, and keeps biting between its forelegs until it incapacitates it. Also: It takes a stick and strikes a person, so that he thinks it is dead and leaves it. Also: It climbs the tree with the lightest climbing, and takes the walnut between its paws, and strikes what is in one paw against what is in the other paw, then blows into it, removes the shells, and eats the kernel.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth example of animal instincts, focusing on the bear. It describes three clever behaviors: first, the bear's method of ambushing a bull by lying in its path and attacking from underneath; second, the bear's deceptive use of a stick to feign death; third, the bear's technique for opening walnuts by striking them between its paws, blowing away the shells, and eating the kernel. Each demonstrates an intelligence that suggests the animal is guided by instinct implanted by the Creator.

The Fox's Clever Trick
Describes the fox's method to rid itself of fleas using a decoy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the step-by-step clever trick the fox uses to rid itself of fleas and mosquitoes.

Core Idea
The fox uses a decoy to transfer pests and then discards it.

This passage presents a fascinating example of animal cleverness. The fox's method to free itself from harmful insects shows an intricate understanding of cause and effect. Let's examine the steps in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the story's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the clear sequence of actions.
2
Identify the problem
The fox is infested with fleas and mosquitoes on its skin. Understand the initial condition.
3
Trace the fox's actions
Follow each step: taking dead skin, submerging gradually, insects moving upward, head submerging, transfer to skin, then throwing it away.
Note the gradual submerging is key.
4
Appreciate the cleverness
Reflect on why this is considered an amazing trick. The fox uses the dead skin as a decoy to collect all pests and then discards it.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : يقال : إن الثعلب إذا اجتمع البق الكثير ، والبعوض الكثير في جلده . أخذ بفيه قطعة من جلد حيوان ميت، ثم إنه يضع يديه ورجليه في الماء ، ولا يزال يغوص فيه قليلا قليلا ، [ فإذا أحس البق والبعوض بـالمـاء أخذت تصعد إلى المواضع الخارجة من الثعلب من الماء ، ثم إن الثعلب لا يزال يغوص قليلا قليلا (۱) ] وتلك الحيوانات ترتفع قليلا قليلا . فإذا غاص كل بدنه في الماء ويقي رأسه خارج الماء ، تصاعدت كل تلك الحيوانات إلى رأسه ، ثم إنه يغوص برأسه في الماء قليلا قليلا ، فتنتقل تلك الحيوانات إلى الجلدة الميتة ، وتجتمع فيها . فإذا أحس الثعلب بانتقالها إلى تلك الجلدة ، رماها في الماء ، وخرج من الماء سليماً فارغاً عن تلك الحيوانات المؤذية . ولا شك أنها حيلة عجيبة في دفع تلك المؤذيات
The seventh: It is said: When many fleas and many mosquitoes gather on the fox's skin, it takes in its mouth a piece of skin from a dead animal, then it puts its forelegs and hind legs in the water, and keeps submerging little by little. [When the fleas and mosquitoes feel the water, they begin to climb up to the parts of the fox that are out of the water, then the fox keeps submerging little by little (1)] And those animals rise little by little. When all its body is submerged and its head remains out of the water, all those animals climb up to its head, then it submerges its head little by little, so those animals transfer to the dead skin and gather in it. When the fox senses their transfer to that skin, it throws it into the water and comes out of the water safe and free from those harmful animals. Undoubtedly, this is an amazing trick to repel those harmful things.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh example, describing how a fox cleverly rids itself of fleas and mosquitoes by using a piece of dead skin as a decoy. The fox gradually submerges its body in water, causing the insects to move upward to avoid the water, eventually concentrating on the head. The fox then submerges its head, prompting the insects to transfer to the piece of skin it holds in its mouth. Finally, the fox throws the skin into the water, freeing itself. The author marvels at this ingenious behavior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The fox used a piece of dead skin as a decoy—something of no value to it. What worthless distractions or indulgences are you holding onto that actually host spiritual pests? Will you find the courage to throw them away today?
?
The fox patiently submerged itself step by step, not all at once. What persistent sin or harmful relationship are you trying to fix in a single plunge? Are you willing to endure gradual discomfort, trusting that each small submersion brings you closer to freedom?

Animal Instincts and Self-Medication
Lists multiple animal instincts and self-medication examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how various animal behaviors illustrate innate, divinely-guided instincts that go beyond learned experience.

Core Idea
Animals instinctively know what to eat for healing—evidence of a divine guide.

This passage presents a series of remarkable animal behaviors, from horses recognizing voices to bustards using wild lettuce as an antidote. Each example points to an innate knowledge that cannot be learned, urging us to reflect on the source of this wisdom. As you read, consider the pattern: animals consistently seek specific remedies when needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the variety of animals and their specific actions.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question at the end.
2
List the animal behaviors
Write down each animal and its distinctive behavior mentioned in the passage. See if you can identify a common theme.
There are at least seven different examples.
3
Analyze the pattern
Observe that many animals seek out specific plants or substances to treat ailments or protect themselves. What does this suggest about the source of their knowledge?
Think about whether these behaviors could be learned.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with 'So reflect: from where did these animals obtain this seeking and this treatment?' Consider possible answers from a theological perspective.
The author implies divine guidance.
5
Connect to broader theme
This example supports the idea that all creatures are guided by a higher power. How does this strengthen the argument for God's existence?
Relate to the overall purpose of the book.

And the cheetah, when given the drug known as 'choker of the cheetah', seeks human dung and eats it. When the crocodile intends to swallow that bird, it is hurt by that thorn, so it opens its mouth and the bird exits. And the turtle, after eating a snake, eats mountain thyme, then returns. That has been observed.
• Some trustworthy hunting enthusiasts recounted: He saw a bustard fighting a viper and retreating from it to a certain plant, eating from it, then returning. It kept doing that, and that person was sitting in a sunken hideout (as hunters do), and the plant was near that spot. When the bustard was occupied with the vipers, the man uprooted that plant. The bustard returned to its growing place and began circling around it in a continuous circle, then fell down and died. So that man knew that it was treating itself by eating that plant from the viper's venom, and that plant is wild lettuce.
• As for the weasel, it seeks aid in fighting the snake by eating rue, for the rue aroma is disliked by the snake. And dogs, when their stomachs are ill, eat the spike of wheat. And when storks wound one another, they treat those wounds with mountain thyme.
So reflect: from where did these animals obtain this seeking and this treatment?

The passage concludes with a rhetorical question urging reflection on the source of this knowledge, implying divine guidance.

The Ninth Example: Hedgehogs and Wind Prediction
Describes hedgehogs sensing wind and a story of a man in Constantinople.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hedgehog's instinctive behavior serves as an example of divine wisdom in creation, as illustrated by the story of a man in Constantinople.

Core Idea
Hedgehogs' wind-sensing instinct is a sign of divine design.

In this passage, the author presents the ninth example from a series, focusing on the curious behavior of hedgehogs that adjust their burrows in anticipation of wind direction. He then recounts an anecdote about a man who used this behavior to predict winds, amassing wealth and providing public benefit. This example highlights the intricate instincts placed in animals by the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the example and the story. Notice that the hedgehog changes its burrow entrance based on wind direction before the wind actually blows.
2
Reflect on the hedgehog's behavior
Consider how the hedgehog senses the north and south wind beforehand and modifies its burrow entrance. This instinct is beyond simple animal behavior.
The hedgehog does not need to learn this; it is innate.
3
Consider the man's application
The man in Constantinople observed the hedgehog and used its behavior to predict winds, benefiting others and gaining wealth. This shows how natural phenomena can be utilized.
4
Identify the lesson
The example points to a natural sign (instinct) as evidence of a wise Creator. The hedgehog's instinct is a marvel of creation that points to divine wisdom.
This connects to larger theological arguments about signs in nature.

The reason for that was a hedgehog in his house that did the aforementioned action.

Scriptural Evidence: Animals Know Their Prayer
The author cites two Quranic verses as proof that animals pray and glorify God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran explicitly states that birds know their own prayer and glorification, implying animal cognition.

Core Idea
The Quran says each bird knows its prayer – a clear sign of animal cognition.

How do we know animals are aware of God? The author begins with direct scriptural evidence from the Quran. These verses are not metaphorical; they claim that birds and mountains actively participate in glorification. Let's examine them closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the two Quranic verses
Read the Arabic of the fifth and sixth evidence citations. The author quotes Surah Saba' and Surah An-Nur.
The verses are short but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the key words
Focus on 'أوبي' (repeat/echo) and 'صافات' (in rows). These indicate deliberate action.
3
Reflect on the implication
If birds know their prayer, they must have knowledge of God. This is the author's point.
This verse is often used in discussions of animal cognition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : قوله تعالى : ( يا جبال أوبي معه والطير ) . السادسة : قوله تعالى : ( والطير صافات كل قد علم صلاته وتسبيحه ) [ قيل : معناه : كل من الطير قد علم صلاته وتسبيحه ] . قال بعضهم : كنت جالساً عند أبي جعفر الباقر ، فقال لي : أتدري ما تقول هذه العصافير عند طلوع الشمس ، وبعد طلوعها ؟ قلت : لا أدري قال : فإنها تقدس ربها ، وتسأله قوت يومها . وأقول : رأيت في بعض الكتب : أن في بعض الأوقات ، اشتد القحط، وعظم حر الصيف . والناس خرجوا للاستسقاء [ فلما فلجوا ] قال : خرجت إلى بعض الجبال، فرأيت ظبية جاءت إلى موضع ، كان في الماضي من الزمان مملوءاً من الماء. ولعل تلك الظبية كانت تشرب منه . فلما وصلت الظبية إليه ما وجدت فيه شيئاً من الماء ، وكان أثر العطش الشديد ظاهراً على تلك الظبية ، فوقفت وحركت رأسها إلى جانب السماء [ مراراً ] فأطبق الغيم وجاء الغيث الكثير .
The fifth: His statement, the Exalted: 'O mountains, repeat with him, and the birds.' The sixth: His statement, the Exalted: 'And the birds in rows, each indeed knows its prayer and its glorification.' [It is said: Its meaning: each of the birds knows its prayer and its glorification.] Some said: I was sitting with Abu Ja'far al-Baqir, and he said to me: Do you know what these sparrows say at sunrise and after sunrise? I said: I do not know. He said: Indeed they glorify their Lord and ask Him for their daily sustenance. And I say: I saw in some books that at some times, the drought intensified, and the heat of summer became great. And the people went out to pray for rain. [When they were disappointed?] He said: I went out to some mountains, and I saw a gazelle come to a place that in past time was full of water. Perhaps that gazelle used to drink from it. When the gazelle reached it, it did not find any water in it, and the effect of severe thirst was apparent on that gazelle. So it stopped and moved its head toward the sky repeatedly. Then the clouds closed in and abundant rain came.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage presents two types of evidence for the claim that animals possess knowledge of God. First, scriptural evidence: the author cites two Quranic verses. The fifth verse (Saba' 10) commands the mountains and birds to join David in glorification, implying they can obey. The sixth verse (An-Nur 41) explicitly states that every bird knows its prayer and glorification, with a parenthetical clarifying that 'each' refers to each bird individually. Second, empirical evidence: an anecdote from Imam al-Baqir that sparrows glorify God and ask for sustenance at sunrise, and a story from a book about a gazelle that, despite severe drought, turned its head to the sky and prayed for rain, which was immediately answered. These examples are meant to show that animals exhibit purposeful, intelligent behavior that suggests cognition and awareness of God.

Key Terms
أوبي
صافات
تسبيحه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These verses are metaphorical and do not indicate real animal cognition.
Correction: The author takes them literally, and the parenthetical explanation emphasizes 'each' bird knows its prayer.

Checkpoint
What does the sixth evidence (Quran 24:41) say about birds?
A) They sing beautifully.
B) Each bird knows its prayer and glorification.
C) They fly in rows for protection.
D) They praise only when commanded.

Key Takeaway
The Quran explicitly teaches that each bird knows its own prayer and glorification, proving animal cognition of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If each bird knows its own prayer, then your prayer is also known to Allah. Do you pray with the same consciousness and devotion as those birds?
?
The birds glorify Allah constantly. When was the last time you stopped to reflect on the worship happening all around you, and how does that change your own awareness of Allah?
?
You have been given intellect far beyond a bird's. Yet the bird knows its prayer. Do you truly know yours? What one change will you make today to learn your prayer more deeply?

Empirical Evidence: Stories of Animals Praying
The author relates two anecdotes as empirical proof that animals pray to God.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Sparrows ask for sustenance and a gazelle's prayer brings rain – animals truly call upon God.

Beyond scripture, the author provides two real-life accounts: one from Imam al-Baqir about sparrows, and another from an old book about a gazelle. These stories are meant to show that animals turn to God in need, confirming their knowledge of Him.

The Futility of Attributing Intellect to Animals
Author argues against animal intellect using empirical evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that animals cannot be rational beings, based on the absence of the effects of intellect.

Core Idea
Animals lack intellect because they don't avoid harm or seek benefit.

The author presents a straightforward argument: if animals possessed intellect, we would see its effects. Yet animals do not avoid harmful actions nor discern benefit from harm. Therefore, attributing intellect to them would be futile and unworthy of the Wise Creator. This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two key observations
The author mentions two specific observations about animals: they do not guard against ugly actions, and they do not distinguish between what benefits and harms them. Note these as evidence.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that it is necessary to definitively judge that animals are not rational. Consider why this is considered a 'necessary' judgment.
The word 'وجب القطع' implies a decisive conclusion based on clear evidence.
4
Reflect on the concept of عبث
Think about why attributing intellect to animals without its effects would be 'عبث' (futility) and why that is incompatible with the Wise Agent.
This connects divine wisdom to the consistency of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكنها الانتفاع البتة بذلك العقل : عبث . وهذا لا يليق بالفاعل الحكيم وأثار العقل غير ظاهرة فيها ، لأنها لا تحترز عن الأفعال القبيحة ، ولا تميز بين ما ينفعها وبين ما يضرها . فوجب القطع بأنها غير عاقلة
+
ولمجيب أن يجيب فيقول : إن درجات العلوم والمعارف كثيرة ، واختلاف النفوس في ماهياتها : محتمل . فلعل خصوصية نفس كل واحد منها ، لا تقتضي إلا لنوع معين من العقل ، وإلا لقسم مخصوص من المعرفة . فإن كان المراد بالعقل جميع العلوم الحاصلة للإنسان ، فحق أنها ليست عاقلة . وإن كان المراد بالعقل معرفة نوع من هذه الأنواع. فظاهر أنها موصوفة بهذه المعرفة . وبالجملة : فالحكم عليها بالثبوت والعدم : حكم على الغيب . ولا يعلم الغيب إلا الله تعالى .
وليكن ههنا آخر كلامنا في النفوس الحيوانية [ والله أعلم (١) ] .
That it can benefit at all from that intellect is futile. And this is not befitting of the Wise Agent. And the effects of intellect are not apparent in them, because they do not guard against ugly actions, nor do they distinguish between what benefits them and what harms them. So it is necessary to definitively judge that they are not rational.
+
And the respondent may answer by saying: The degrees of sciences and knowledges are many, and the difference of souls in their essences is possible. Perhaps the particularity of each one's soul does not require except a specific type of intellect, or a particular division of knowledge. For if by 'intellect' is meant all the sciences acquired by humans, then it is true that they are not rational. But if by 'intellect' is meant knowledge of a type of these types, then it is apparent that they are described by that knowledge. In summary: judging them as having or lacking [intellect] is a judgment about the unseen. And none knows the unseen except Allah, the Exalted.
And let this be the end of our discussion on animal souls [And Allah knows best (1)].
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and counter-argument regarding the intellect of animals. The author begins by stating that if animals were capable of using intellect but show no signs of it—they do not avoid harmful actions nor distinguish between benefit and harm—then granting them intellect would be futile (عبث) and unbefitting of the Wise Agent (الفاعل الحكيم). Therefore, the author asserts that it is necessary to definitively judge that animals are not rational (غير عاقلة).

Then the author introduces a possible objection from a respondent (المجيب). The objector argues that the degrees of knowledge and sciences are many, and souls may differ in their essences. It is possible that the particular nature of each animal's soul requires only a specific type of intellect or a particular division of knowledge. The objector clarifies: if by 'intellect' one means all the sciences that humans acquire, then indeed animals do not have that. But if by 'intellect' one means a particular type of knowledge, then it is apparent that animals may be described by that knowledge. In the end, the author summarizes that judging whether animals possess intellect or not is a judgment about the unseen (الغيب), and only Allah knows the unseen. The author then concludes this section on animal souls with 'And Allah knows best'.

Key Terms
العقل
الفاعل الحكيم
عبث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals have some form of rationality because they exhibit instinctive behaviors.
Correction: The author argues that true intellect would manifest in avoiding harmful actions and seeking benefit, which animals do not do.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the evidence that animals are not rational?
A) They cannot speak.
B) They do not avoid ugly actions or distinguish benefit from harm.
C) They lack emotions.
D) They cannot be trained.

Key Takeaway
The absence of intellect's observable effects in animals proves they lack it, and attributing futile faculties to them contradicts divine wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have been given intellect that animals lack. Are you using it to guard against ugly actions and seek what truly benefits you? Where in your life are you living as if you were without reason? What will you change today to honor the intellect Allah gave you?
?
When you face a moral choice, do you act like an animal—following instinct or desire—or do you engage your intellect to distinguish between what is truly beneficial and what is harmful? What is one area where you need to stop rationalizing and start reasoning with your God-given ‘aql?
?
The author says giving intellect without benefit is futile (عبث). Allah is not futile. So ask yourself: Are you benefiting from your intellect? Are you using it to avoid ugly actions and to seek real benefit? If not, you are treating your own intellect as if it were futile—and that is not befitting of the One who gave it to you.

The Objection and Limits of Knowledge of the Unseen
Objection and response concerning animal intellect, ending with a note on the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's argument that animals may possess a specific type of intellect suited to their souls, and the author's caution about judging the unseen.

Core Idea
Different souls may have different kinds of intellect; judging absence is a claim about the unseen.

After the author's definitive stance, an objector raises a nuanced point: degrees of knowledge are many, and souls differ. Perhaps animals have a specific type of intellect tailored to their nature. The author then reminds us that such judgments are about the unseen, which only Allah knows. This lesson explores the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage for the objection and the author's concluding note. Pay attention to the structure: the objector's speech and the author's summary.
2
Identify the objector's key points
Notice how the objector mentions the many degrees of knowledge and the possibility that souls differ in essence. This sets up the idea that animals might have a specific type of intellect.
The objector is not fully agreeing, but raising a logical possibility.
3
Understand the clarification about 'intellect'
The objector distinguishes between two meanings of 'intellect': all human sciences vs. a specific type. This shows the debate may be about definitions.
4
Reflect on the unseen
The author concludes that judging whether animals have intellect or not is a judgment about the unseen (ghayb). Only Allah knows. This is a humbling reminder of epistemic limits.

Two Theories on the Origin of Jinn: Celestial Emanations vs. Supreme Spirit
Enumerates two theories on jinn origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two contrasting theories on the origin of jinn and devils: one based on celestial spirits and the other on an independent supreme spirit.

Core Idea
Jinn are seen either as planetary emanations or as creations of a greatest spirit.

This passage presents the third research of a larger discussion, contrasting two views on the origin of jinn and devils. The first view traces jinn to celestial spirits, distinguishing evil from good planetary sources. The second view, held by religious groups, posits a supreme spirit independent of celestial bodies, with jinn as its offspring. Understanding these contrasts helps clarify different theological and philosophical frameworks.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is divided into two main parts.
2
Identify the classification in the first theory
The first theory classifies jinn into evil (from Saturn and Mars) and good (from benefic planets). Note how the author uses specific planetary names.
This classification is based on the nature of the celestial souls.
3
Recognize the contrast with the religious view
The second theory denies the connection to celestial spirits and instead posits a single greatest spirit from which jinn derive. Compare this to the first theory's multi-source approach.
The religious view emphasizes independence from celestial influences.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each theory affects the understanding of jinn's nature and their relationship to the cosmos. Which theory aligns with different worldviews?
This passage does not endorse either view; it presents them for comparison.

This is a classification of different theories on the origin of jinn.

The First Proof Against Jinn: The Dense Bodies Argument
Deniers' first proof: jinn must be dense or subtle - dense bodies impossibility shown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disjunctive argument used by deniers of jinn, and why the possibility of jinn being dense bodies is rejected on the grounds of leading to sophistry.

Core Idea
If jinn are dense bodies, they would be visible to all — and that leads to absurdity.

This passage presents the opening argument of those who deny the existence of jinn and devils. It sets up a logical disjunction: if jinn exist, they must be either dense or subtle bodies. The author then refutes the dense bodies case, arguing that it would lead to sophistry. The subtle bodies refutation is not given here, making this an incomplete proof. In this lesson, we will examine the structure of this argument and understand why the dense bodies possibility is deemed impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the logical structure of 'If... then...'.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is a disjunctive syllogism: jinn must be either dense or subtle bodies. The passage only refutes the dense option. Identify the 'either-or' and the reasoning for dense bodies.
3
Understand the sophistry objection
The author argues that if dense jinn could be present unseen, we could doubt all sense perception, leading to sophistry. Consider why this is a problem.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Recognize the incompleteness
Note that the subtle bodies part is missing. The proof is not finished. This is important because the argument as it stands only refutes one possibility.
The source needs continuation from later pages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بإنكار الجن والشياطين من وجوه : الحجة الأولى : إنها لو كانت موجودة ، لكانت إما أن تكون من الأجسام الكثيفة ؛ أو من الأجسام اللطيفة . والقسمان باطلان ، فبطل القول بوجودها . أما بيان أنه يمتنع كونها من الأجسام الكثيفة ، فلأنها لو كانت كذلك ، لوجب في كل من كان سليم الحس ، أن يبصرها. إذ لو جوزنا حضور مثل هذه الموجودات ، مع ) ] أنا لا نراها لم يمتنع أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة ، وشموس مشرقة ، ورعود قاصفة ، ، ورياح. عاصفة أنا لا نحس بشيء منها . وذلك يوجب القول بالسفسطة
And those who deny jinn and devils argued from aspects: The first proof: If they existed, they would have to be either from dense bodies or from subtle bodies. Both divisions are false, so the claim of their existence is false. As for the demonstration that it is impossible for them to be from dense bodies: Because if they were so, then everyone with sound sense would have to see them. For if we permitted the presence of such existents while we do not see them, it would not be impossible that there be in our presence towering mountains, shining suns, thunderclaps, and violent winds that we perceive nothing of. And that would necessitate sophistry.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of those who deny jinn and devils. It argues by disjunction: if jinn existed, they must be either dense or subtle bodies. Both are impossible. For dense bodies, they would be visible to everyone; if we allow unseen dense bodies, we could doubt all sense perception, leading to sophistry. The argument for subtle bodies is not given as the text cuts off. This proof aims to show that affirming jinn leads to absurd consequences.

Misconception: One might think this argument completely disproves the existence of jinn.
Correction: The argument only refutes the possibility of jinn being dense bodies; it does not address subtle bodies, so it is not a complete disproof.

Checkpoint

B) We might doubt all sense perception
C) Jinn would be subtle
D) It would prove their existence

Key Takeaway
The dense bodies possibility for jinn is rejected because it would lead to universal doubt about sense perception, but the argument against subtle bodies is missing here.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Three Possibilities for Internal Thoughts: Why They Must Be External Spiritual Beings
The author divides internal mental occurrences into three possibilities, refutes the first two, and concludes they are external beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that internal mental words and images must be caused by external spiritual beings (jinn and devils), and why the alternatives (pure non-existence or mere mental existence) are impossible.

Core Idea
Thoughts inside your mind are real entities caused by jinn and devils.

This passage presents a rigorous logical argument about the nature of the internal voices and images that occur in our minds. The author considers three possibilities: they could be nothing at all, they could be purely mental constructs, or they could be real external entities. Through a series of proofs, he eliminates the first two possibilities, concluding that these phenomena are caused by spiritual beings such as jinn and devils. This argument has profound implications for understanding the reality of the unseen and the source of our inner experiences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trilemma
Read the first sentence of the passage where the author lists the three possibilities for internal words and images.
The three options are: pure non-existence, existence only in the mind, or existence in actuality.
2
Examine refutation of the first possibility
Focus on the argument against pure non-existence. The author claims that if these thoughts have specific attributes, they cannot be nothing, otherwise sensory perceptions would also be nothing.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Examine refutation of the second possibility
The author provides two proofs against mental existence only. First, if mental existence were sufficient, it would lead to sophistry. Second, consider the locus of these images: either a material organ (too small) or an abstract substance (cannot have shape), both impossible.
Pay attention to the second proof's sub-arguments about size and abstract substance.
4
Understand the conclusion and the subtle point
After eliminating the first two options, the third remains: these words and images are external beings. The author then adds a subtle point: seclusion from sensory things strengthens these phenomena, leading to full disclosure of the spiritual beings.
This subtle point supports the external explanation by linking empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تكون أموراً موجودة في الذهن والخيال، وإما أن تكون أشياء موجودة في الأعيان كائنة في الجود. والقسمان الأولان باطلان، فيبقى الثالث. وذلك هو الجن والشياطين. تناجيه وتناديه في سره وفي قلبه وباطنه. وإنما قلنا: إن القول بأن تلك الكلمات الواردة في الخواطر، وأن تلك الصور المشاهدة في الباطن، يمتنع أن تكون عدماً محضاً ونفياً صرفاً. وذلك لأنا نسمع تلك الحروف من القلب والباطن، ونفهم معانيها، وتميز بينها وبين غيرها. فلو جاز القول بأنها مع اتصافها بهذه الصفات المعينة والخواص المعينة عدم محض، ونفي صرف. لم يمتنع أيضاً في كون هذه الصور المخصوصة بهذه الحواس كونها عدماً محضاً ونفياً صرفاً. وكل ذلك باطل محال. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو أنها موجودات في الأذهان، لا في الأعيان. فهذا باطل قطعاً. ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنا لو جوزنا أن تكون هذه الألفاظ المترتبة المتعاقبة، وأن تكون هذه الصور المخصوصة المتميزة عما يغايرها، بألوانها وأشكالها: موجودات في الأذهان لا في الأعيان، فلنجز في هذه الصور المحسوسة: كونها كذلك ويلزم منه السفسطة. والثاني: إن محل هذه الصور. إما أن يكون جسماً مخصوصاً كدماغ أو قلب، وإما أن يكون جوهراً مجرداً، والأول باطل. لأن الصور العظيمة يمتنع انطباعها في المحل الصغير. والثاني أيضاً باطل. لأن الجوهر المجرد لا يحصل فيه جوانب مختلفة، ولا أجزاء متباينة. وإذا كان كذلك، كانت الصورة المنطبعة فيه، يمتنع أن يحصل فيها الجوانب المختلفة، والأجزاء المتباينة بحسب الأحياز والجهات. وإذا كان كذلك، كانت الصورة الحالة في الجوهر المجرد يمتنع أن يكون لها شكل، وجوانب متميزة. بحسب الجهات. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكل ما كان موصوفاً بهذه الصفات، يمتنع حلوله في الجوهر المجرد فثبت بهذا البيان الذي لخصناه: أن هذه الألفاظ الذهنية، وهذه الصور المتخيلة، إما أن تكون عدماً محضاً، ونفياً صرفاً. وإما أن تكون موجودات إلا أنه لا وجود لها إلا في الأذهان. وإما أن تكون موجودات في الأعيان، وثبت بطلان القسمين الأولين. فبقي القسم الثالث. وهو أنها أشخاص موجودة في الأعيان، وأنها تتراءى للناس وتخاطبهم. ثم ههنا دقيقة أخرى: وهي أنه كلما كان اشتغال الإنسان بالمحسوسات أكثر، كانت تلك الخيالات أضعف. وكلما كان انقطاعه عن المحسوسات أكثر، كان بقاؤه في الفكر والخلوة أكثر، فكان ظهور هذه الأحوال أكثر. فهذا يقوى في العقل وفي الخواطر: أنه لا يزال يتزايد ذلك الظهور، ويقوى ذلك التجلي، حتى ينتهي إلى الانكشاف التام لتلك الأشخاص الروحانية. وهذا كلام قوي في هذا الباب
They are either things existing in the mind and imagination, or things existing in actuality as beings in existence. The first two divisions are false, so the third remains. That is the jinn and devils. They whisper to him and call out to him in his secret, in his heart and inner self. And we only say: The claim that those words occurring in thoughts and those images witnessed inwardly cannot possibly be pure non-existence and absolute nothingness. That is because we hear those letters from the heart and inner self, understand their meanings, and distinguish between them and others. For if it were permitted to say that they, despite being characterized by these specific attributes and specific properties, are pure non-existence and absolute nothingness, then it would also not be impossible for these specific images perceived by these senses to be pure non-existence and absolute nothingness. All that is false and impossible. As for the second division: that they are existents in minds, not in actuality. This is absolutely false. Two proofs indicate it: The first: If we allowed that these ordered successive words and these specific images distinguished from what is other than them, by their colors and forms, are existents in minds not in actuality, then we should allow these sensory images to be likewise, which would lead to sophistry. The second: The locus of these images is either a specific body like the brain or heart, or an abstract substance. The first is false because great images cannot be impressed in a small locus. The second is also false because an abstract substance does not contain various sides nor distinct parts. If so, then an image impressed in it cannot have various sides and parts differing in positions and directions. If so, then an image residing in an abstract substance cannot have a form and distinct sides according to directions. If the case is so, then everything characterized by these attributes cannot reside in an abstract substance. Thus it is established by this exposition we have summarized: that these mental words and these imagined images are either pure non-existence and absolute nothingness, or existents but with no existence except in minds, or existents in actuality. The first two divisions have been proven false, so the third remains: that they are individuals existing in actuality, and they appear to people and address them. Then there is another subtle point: that the more a person is occupied with sensory things, the weaker these imaginations are. And the more his detachment from sensory things is greater, and his remaining in thought and seclusion is more, then the manifestation of these states is greater. This strengthens in the intellect and in thoughts that this manifestation continually increases, and this unveiling strengthens until it reaches complete disclosure of those spiritual individuals. And this is a strong statement in this matter.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument for the existence of external spiritual beings (jinn and devils) responsible for internal thoughts and images. The author begins by establishing a trilemma: the words and images that occur in the mind are either (1) pure non-existence, (2) mental existents only, or (3) actual external beings. He states that the first two are false, so the third must be true, identifying them as jinn and devils that whisper to humans. First, against pure non-existence: if these thoughts have specific attributes and are distinguished, they cannot be nothing, because then sensory perceptions would also be nothing, which is absurd. Second, against mental existence only: if they were merely mental, then by the same logic sensory perceptions could also be merely mental, leading to sophistry (denial of external reality). Additionally, the mind's locus cannot hold them: if the mind is a material organ, it is too small for large images; if it is an abstract substance, it cannot have parts or sides, so images with shape and direction cannot reside in it. Having refuted the first two possibilities, the only remaining option is that these words and images are external individuals that appear to people and speak to them. Finally, a subtle point: engagement with sensory things weakens these phenomena, while seclusion and detachment strengthen them, culminating in complete disclosure of these spiritual beings. This supports the external explanation.

Key Terms

الوجود في الأذهان
الوجود في الأعيان
الجن
الشياطين
الخواطر
الصور
الجوهر المجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Internal thoughts and images are purely subjective mental phenomena with no external reality.
Correction: The author argues that if they were purely mental, it would lead to absurdities, and they must be caused by real external spiritual beings (jinn/devils).

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what are the three possibilities for internal words and images?
A) Non-existence, mental existence, external existence
B) Jinn, devils, angels
C) Heart, mind, soul
D) Sound, image, meaning

Key Takeaway
The logical impossibility of internal thoughts being non-existent or merely mental forces the conclusion that they are real external spiritual beings that interact with humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a sudden thought or image in your mind, do you ever pause to consider that it might be a whisper from a jinn or devil, rather than merely your own reflection? How would your response change if you took that possibility seriously?
?
The author notes that seclusion and detachment from sensory distractions increase the manifestation of these spiritual beings. Are you actively creating space in your life for silence and solitude, or do you fill every moment with noise to avoid confronting what emerges from within?
?
If you truly believed that your inner thoughts are not entirely your own, what practical steps would you take to guard your heart and mind against evil whispers? Write down one concrete change you will make today.

The Second Argument: Whispers Must Come from an External Agent
The author argues that the agent of internal whispers cannot be the human himself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument proving that internal whispers originate from an external agent (jinn) and not from the human himself.

Core Idea
If you hate a thought and cannot repel it, you are not its author.

Sanusi continues his arguments for the existence of jinn by presenting a logical proof based on the experience of aversion. He argues that the very fact we often struggle to dismiss unwanted thoughts shows that they originate outside us. This passage introduces that argument and defines what is meant by 'jinn'.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: a claim, a division of possibilities, elimination of one, and conclusion.
This argument is independent; it does not rely on the previous one.
2
Understand the key premise
The author starts with the principle that every event (including these whispers) must have an agent (fāʿil). Identify this premise.
3
Analyze the elimination of the human
The author rules out the human as agent by pointing to the extreme aversion and inability to repel the thoughts. See how he uses this as proof.
Think of a time you had a thought you hated but could not get rid of.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since the human cannot be the agent, the agent must be something else. That something else is called 'jinn' in this context—a being that can inject speech into the inner self.
Note the definition: jinn is simply the external agent of these whispers.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this argument strengthens the case for the existence of an unseen world and the impact of jinn on human thoughts.
This is a rational argument, not based on scripture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: إن هذه الوساوس التي تخطر بالبال والكلمات التي تقع في القلوب، لا بد لها من فاعل وفاعلها، إما ذلك الإنسان، وإما غيره والأول باطل. لأن ذلك الإنسان قد يكون في غاية النفرة والكراهية لتلك الخواطر حتى إنه قد يحتال بالحيل الكثيرة في أن يدفعها عن نفسه فلا يقدر عليها البتة. وإذا كان كذلك، امتنع أن يكون فاعلها هو هذا الإنسان فوجب أن يكون المتكلم بتلك الخواطر، والناطق بها: فاعل آخر غير الإنسان، وأن ذلك الفاعل يقدر على إيصال نطقه إلى باطن الإنسان. ولا نعني بالجن إلا هذا الفاعل
The second argument: These whispers that occur to the mind and the words that occur in the hearts must have an agent. Their agent is either that human or another. The first is false, because that human may be in extreme aversion and hatred toward those thoughts, such that he may use many devices to repel them from himself but is utterly unable to do so. If this is the case, it is impossible that their agent is this human. So it is necessary that the speaker of those thoughts and the utterer of them is another agent besides the human, and that that agent is able to convey his speech to the inner self of the human. And we mean by 'jinn' nothing but this agent.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents a second, independent argument to prove that internal whispers originate from an external source, namely jinn. The argument is based on the principle that every event requires an agent. The words and whispers that arise in the heart must have an agent, who is either the person himself or something else. The author rules out the person himself by observing that the person often feels extreme aversion and hatred toward these thoughts, and even tries hard to repel them through various means, yet fails completely. If he were the agent, he would be able to stop them at will. Since he cannot, the agent must be external. This external agent is what is meant by 'jinn' in this context: a being that can convey its speech into the inner self of a human.

الفاعل
الجن
النفرة
الكراهية
الخواطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that unwanted thoughts are entirely self-generated and under one's control.
Correction: The author argues that if a person has strong aversion and cannot repel the thoughts despite effort, then the thoughts cannot be from oneself; they must come from an external source.

Checkpoint
The second argument proves that the agent of the whispers cannot be the human himself because...
A) the human is too busy to generate them
B) the human often desires these thoughts
C) the human may be in extreme aversion yet cannot repel them
D) the human is unaware of the whispers

Key Takeaway
Internal whispers that a person cannot repel despite strong aversion must come from an external agent, which we call jinn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring negative thought that you have tried to push away but cannot. Does this lesson change how you view that thought? Will you now see it as coming from an external enemy rather than yourself? How will you respond differently starting today?
?
If the whispers are from jinn, then your struggle is not with your own soul but with a real adversary. Does that make you more determined to seek Allah's protection? What specific action will you take the next time such a thought comes?
?
The author says that the human 'may use many devices to repel them but is utterly unable.' Have you ever felt defeated by your own thoughts? Knowing now that they are not from you, will you stop blaming yourself and instead turn to the One who can repel them? What is the first step you will take?

The Logical Proof That Mental Images Are External Persons
Concludes proof that mental images are external existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that mental images and words cannot be mere non-existence or mental existents, but must be real external persons.

Core Idea
Mental images are not just in your head—they are real external persons.

The author concludes a dense logical proof about the nature of mental images. He systematically eliminates two possibilities—pure non-existence and mere mental existence—leaving only one conclusion: these images are real external beings that appear and speak to people. This lesson walks you through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about simple substance
Note the author's starting point: a form in a simple substance (like a soul) cannot have different aspects or parts. This sets the stage for the argument.
Simple substance here refers to a non-material substance like the soul.
2
Identify the three-fold division
The author says mental words and images must fall into one of three categories: pure non-existence, existents only in minds, or existents in external objects.
3
Grasp why the first two are invalid
The proof claims the first two categories are invalid. Though the details are condensed, the conclusion is that only the third category remains: external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنطبعة فيه ، يمتنع أن يحصل فيها الجوانب المختلفة ، والأجزاء المتباينة بحسب الأحياز والجهات . وإذا كان كذلك ، كانت الصورة الحالة في الجوهر المجرد يمتنع أن يكون لها شكل ، وجوانب متميزة بحسب الجهات . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكل ما كان موصوفاً بهذه الصفات، يمتنع حلوله في الجوهر المجرد فثبت بهذا البيان الذي لخصناه : أن هذه الألفاظ الذهنية ، وهذه الصور المتخيلة ، إما أن تكون عدماً محضاً ، ونفياً صرفاً . وإما أن تكون موجودات [ إلا أنه لا وجود لها إلا في الأذهان ] وإما أن تكون موجودات في الأعيان ، وثبت بطلان القسمين الأولين. فبقي القسم الثالث . وهو أنها أشخاص موجودة في الأعيان ، وأنها تتراءى للناس وتخاطبهم . ثم ههنا دقيقة أخرى : وهي أنه كلما كان اشتغال الإنسان بالمحسوسات أكثر ، كانت تلك الخيالات أضعف . وكلما كان انقطاعه عن المحسوسات أكثر ، كان بقاؤه في الفكر والخلوة أكثر ، فكان ظهور هذه الأحوال أكثر. فهذا يقوى في العقل وفي الخواطر : أنه لا يزال يتزايد ذلك الظهور، ويقوى ذلك التجلي ، حتى ينتهي إلى الانكشاف التام لتلك الأشخاص الروحانية . وهذا كلام قوي في هذا الباب
...impossible for it to have different aspects and diverse parts in terms of places and directions. And if so, the form subsisting in a simple substance cannot have shape or distinct aspects in terms of directions. And if that is the case, everything described with these attributes cannot inhere in a simple substance. So it is established by this summarized proof: that these mental words and imagined forms are either pure non-existence and absolute negation, or existents [but they have no existence except in minds], or existents in external objects, and the first two divisions are invalid. So the third division remains: that they are persons existing in external objects, and they appear to people and speak to them. Then here is another subtle point: that whenever a person's engagement with sensory things is greater, those imaginations are weaker; and whenever his detachment from sensory things is greater and his remaining in thought and solitude is greater, the manifestation of these states is greater. So this strengthens in the mind and thoughts that this manifestation continually increases and that unveiling strengthens until it reaches complete disclosure of those spiritual persons. And this is a strong statement in this chapter.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusion of a proof regarding mental images. It begins with a premise that a form in a simple substance cannot have different aspects and parts. Therefore, anything described with such attributes cannot inhere in a simple substance. Then it summarizes the proof: mental words and imagined forms are either pure non-existence, existents only in minds, or existents in external objects. The first two are invalid, leaving the third: they are real persons existing in the external world that appear to people and speak to them. Then a subtle point: the more one is occupied with sensory objects, the weaker these imaginations; but the more one withdraws from sensory things and engages in thought and seclusion, the stronger these states become, leading to complete unveiling of these spiritual persons.

Key Terms
جوهر مجرد
الألفاظ الذهنية
الصور المتخيلة
الأشخاص الروحانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental images are merely subjective illusions or hallucinations with no reality outside the mind.
Correction: The author argues that they are real external persons existing in the objective world.

Checkpoint

B) Three categories: non-existence, mental, and external

Key Takeaway
Mental words and images are real external persons, not mere mental constructs or non-existent.

The Spiritual Condition for Unveiling Spiritual Persons
Explains the spiritual condition for unveiling spiritual persons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how detachment from sensory engagement and solitude strengthens the manifestation of spiritual persons until complete unveiling.

Core Idea
Detachment from the senses amplifies visions of spiritual persons.

After concluding that mental images are real external persons, the author adds a subtle point about how these manifestations become stronger. The more you withdraw from sensory distractions and engage in thoughtful solitude, the more vividly these spiritual persons appear—eventually leading to full disclosure. This lesson explores that spiritual dynamic.

Guided Path
1
Note the inverse relationship
The author states that greater engagement with sensory objects weakens imaginations, while detachment strengthens them.
This is a key principle for spiritual practice.
2
Identify the cause of strengthening
Detachment and solitude in thought and seclusion lead to greater manifestation of these states.
3
Understand the culmination
This process continues until complete unveiling of those spiritual persons is achieved.
The author calls this a strong statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Second Proof: The Agent of Internal Whispers is Not the Human Mind
Presents second proof with two refutations of philosophers' objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof that internal whispers must have an agent distinct from the human, and how the philosophers' objection (thinking faculty) is refuted through logical divisions.

Core Idea
The whispers you hate cannot come from you; they must come from an independent agent.

The first proof established the existence of an agent for internal whispers. Here, the author provides a second proof that this agent cannot be the human mind or any human faculty. He then addresses the philosophers' claim that the 'thinking faculty' could be the agent, dissecting it with logical divisions. This lesson follows that argument step by step, from the initial proof to the final (incomplete) conclusion that the agent must be a self-subsistent substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the basic proof
Start by reading the opening argument: whispers must have an agent, and that agent cannot be the human because the human dislikes and tries to repel them. Notice how the author uses a simple conditional statement.
The proof relies on the observed fact that humans often hate these thoughts.
2
Consider the philosophers' objection
The philosophers propose that the thinking faculty could be the agent. Read their objection and the author's dismissive remark, then note that the author provides two formal refutations.
The author calls this objection 'absolutely false' and gives two reasons.
3
Follow the first refutation
The first refutation argues that the one who perceives and acts in the body is a single thing (the human), so thinking cannot be a separate agent. The human does not want these words, so he cannot be their agent.
This refutation relies on the unity of the human self.
4
Analyze the second refutation
The second refutation divides the thinking faculty into three possibilities: it is either the human, an attribute of the human, or an independent agent. The first two are invalid, so it must be an independent agent. Then the author argues this agent must be knowing and powerful, and it is either an accident or a substance. The passage cuts off, but the implication is that it is a self-subsistent substance.
Pay attention to the logical division (tripartite classification).
5
Reflect on the incomplete conclusion
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence while arguing that the agent cannot be an accident. The implied conclusion is that the agent is a self-subsistent substance (a jinn). Consider how this connects to the overall topic.
The missing conclusion is inferred from the argument's structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن هذه الوساوس التي تخطر بالبال والكلمات التي تقع في القلب ، لا بد لها من فاعل وفاعلها، إما ذلك الإنسان ، وإما غيره والأول باطل . لأن ذلك الإنسان قد يكون في غاية النفرة والكراهية لتلك الخواطر حتى إنه قد يحتال بالحيل الكثيرة في أن يدفعها عن نفسه فلا يقدر عليها البتة . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يكون فاعلها هو هذا الإنسان فوجب أن يكون المتكلم بتلك الخواطر ، والناطق بها : فاعل آخر غير الإنسان ، وأن ذلك الفاعل يقدر على إيصال نطقه إلى باطن الإنسان . ولا نعني بالجن إلا هذا الفاعل

قالت الفلاسفة : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون فاعل تلك الكلمات ، وتلك الألفاظ : هو القوة المفكرة ، المنتقلة من صفة إلى صفة ، ومن حالة إلى حالة ؟ ونقول : هذا الكلام طال عهد الإنسان بسماعه ولهذا السبب تمكن في قلوبهم ) .
وهو باطل قطعاً ويدل عليه وجهان :
الأول : إنا بينا في المقالات المتقدمة : أن الموصوف بجميع الإدراكات والمتصرف بجميع أنواع التصرفات في هذا البدن يجب أن يكون شيئاً واحداً . وهو الإنسان . وإذا كان كذلك ، امتنع أن يقال : الفكر عمل شيئاً آخر سوى الإنسان . لأنا قد دللنا على أن الإنسان لم يفعل هذه الألفاظ ، لأنه لا يريدها . بل يكرهها . فبطل هذا الكلام
الثاني : إن هذا الذي سميتموه بالقوة المفكرة . إما أن يكون هو الإنسان ، وإما أن يكون صفة من صفاته وحالة من أحواله ، وإما أن يكون فاعلاً مستقلاً بنفسه، قد تقدم على الأفعال باختيار نفسه . والأول باطل . لأنا بينا أن الإنسان لا يريد هذه الحالة. والثاني باطل . لأن الصفة القائمة بذات الإنسان ، لا يمكنها أن تقدم على فعل، على خلاف إرادة الإنسان . ولما بطل هذان القسمان، ثبت أن الفاعل لتلك الكلمات فاعلا آخر غير الإنسان، وغير مجموع صفاته . فنقول : ذلك الفاعل لا يمكنه أن يأتي بتلك الكلمات، إلا إذا كان عالماً قادراً [؟] نه إن لم يكن عالماً بأحوال تلك الكلمات، يمتنع منه القصد إلى تكوينها . لأن القصد إلى ما يكون معلوماً محال . فذلك الفاعل عالم بحقائق الأشياء، وقادر على تكوين تلك الكلمات. وهو شيء مغاير لذات الإنسان والمجموع صفاته. فذلك الشيء إما أن يكون عرضاً حالاً في جسم، وإما أن يكون جوهراً قائماً بذاته. والأول باطل . لأن العلم الضروري حاصل بان العرض القائم بالغير، لا يكون حياً عالماً قادراً فاعلاً للأفعال الشاقة . ولما بطل
The second proof: These whispers that occur to the mind and the words that occur in the heart must have an agent. And its agent is either that human or another. The first is invalid because that human may be in extreme aversion and hatred for those thoughts, so that he may try many tricks to repel them from himself but is utterly unable. And if so, it is impossible that its agent is this human. Therefore the speaker of those thoughts and the utterer must be an agent other than the human, and that agent is able to convey its speech to the interior of the human. And we mean by jinn nothing but this agent.

The philosophers said: 'Why is it not permissible that the agent of those words and those expressions is the thinking faculty, which moves from attribute to attribute and from state to state?' And we say: This statement has a long history of being heard by humans, and for this reason it has become entrenched in their hearts.
And it is absolutely false, and two points indicate this:
First: We have shown in previous treatises that the one described with all perceptions and who disposes all kinds of dispositions in this body must be one thing, namely the human. And if so, it is impossible to say that thinking is something else other than the human. For we have indicated that the human did not do these words because he does not want them; rather he dislikes them. So this statement is invalid.
Second: This thing you have called the thinking faculty: either it is the human, or it is an attribute of his attributes and a state of his states, or it is an independent agent that undertakes actions by its own choice. The first is invalid because we have shown that the human does not want this state. The second is invalid because an attribute subsisting in the human cannot undertake an action contrary to the human's will. And when these two divisions are invalid, it is established that the agent of those words is another agent, other than the human and other than the totality of his attributes. So we say: That agent cannot produce those words unless it is knowing and powerful. For if it were not knowing of the states of those words, intending to produce them would be impossible, because intending something unknown is impossible. So that agent is knowing of the realities of things and powerful to produce those words. And it is a thing different from the essence of the human and the totality of his attributes. Then that thing is either an accident subsisting in a body, or a self-subsistent substance. The first is invalid because necessary knowledge holds that an accident subsisting in another cannot be living, knowing, powerful, performing difficult actions. And since it is invalid...
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof concerning the agent of internal whispers. It argues that the whispers occurring in the mind and heart must have an agent. This agent cannot be the human himself, because the human often hates these thoughts and tries to repel them but cannot. Therefore, the agent must be another being, which we call jinn. The philosophers object that the agent could be the thinking faculty (quwwa mutafakkira). The author refutes this with two arguments. First, the one who perceives and acts in the body is a single thing, the human, so thinking cannot be a separate agent. Second, the thinking faculty is either the human (which is invalid because the human dislikes these thoughts), or a quality of the human (which cannot act against human will), or an independent agent. Since the first two are false, it must be an independent agent. This agent must be knowing and powerful, and it is either an accident inhering in a body or a self-subsistent substance. The accident option is invalid because accidents cannot perform strenuous acts. The argument cuts off here, implying the agent is a self-subsistent substance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The thinking faculty (human mind) itself produces unwanted internal whispers.
Correction: The human mind cannot produce whispers that the human hates and tries to repel; an independent agent (jinn) must be the source.

Checkpoint
In the second refutation, what are the three possibilities the author lists for the thinking faculty?
A) The human, an attribute of the human, or an independent agent
B) The human, a jinn, or an angel
C) An accident, a substance, or a quality
D) The soul, the mind, or the body

Key Takeaway
The second proof, through logical division, demonstrates that the agent of internal whispers must be an independent, self-subsistent substance (a jinn), not the human mind or any human faculty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever experienced a recurring whisper of sin or doubt that you desperately wanted to stop, but no matter what you did, it persisted? What does that powerlessness teach you about the source of that whisper? How will you turn to the One who controls the unseen to protect you?
?
If you can banish a thought from your mind, that thought is under your control. But if a thought lingers against your will, it must come from outside you. In light of this lesson, what will you do the next time a sinful thought that you hate stays in your heart?
?
You often say, 'It was just my mind playing tricks.' But this passage proves that your mind does not act against your will. Will you stop excusing your evil thoughts as your own? How will you now hold yourself accountable to seek refuge in Allah from the accursed whisperer?

Distinguishing Divine and Satanic Thoughts by Ultimate Intention
The author provides a criterion for distinguishing divine thoughts from satanic thoughts based on ultimate intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ultimate purpose of a thought or inclination determines whether it is divine or satanic, and why outward appearances can be deceptive.

Core Idea
The final intention, not the outward action, distinguishes divine from satanic thoughts.

This passage tackles a practical challenge for spiritual seekers: how to tell whether a thought or inclination comes from God or from Satan. The author acknowledges that mystical experts have disagreed on this, and then provides a clear criterion based on the ultimate purpose. He also warns against a common fallacy where outward piety may hide worldly ambition, and vice versa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a full sense of the author's argument.
Pay attention to the flow from the general criterion to the exception and examples.
2
Identify the basic criterion
Notice how the author says anything calling to spiritualities is divine, and anything calling to worldly pleasures is satanic. This is the starting point.
The criterion is rooted in the superiority of intellectual felicities.
3
Observe the fallacy (mughlaṭa)
The author warns that an act may appear divine but actually be satanic, or appear satanic but be divine. Mark the two examples he gives.
This shows the need to look deeper than the surface.
4
Understand the decisive rule
Focus on the sentence: 'if the ultimate purpose is directing to the unseen, it is divine; if it is for a worldly interest, it is satanic.' This is the key takeaway.
The rule is simple but requires honest self-reflection.
5
Reflect on the application
Consider how this criterion applies to your own thoughts and actions. What is your ultimate intention?
This lesson is meant to be practical, not just theoretical.
