والفرع الثاني: وهو أن أصحاب المكاشفات اضطربوا في ذكر الفرق المضبوط بين الخواطر الشيطانية، وبين الخواطر الرحمانية، وطالت كلماتهم فيه. وأنا أقول: إنا قد بينا: أن السعادات العقلية المتعلقة بعالم المفارقات أكمل وأفضل من السعادات المتعلقة بعالم الجسمانيات. فكل ما دعاك إلى شيء من الروحانيات، فهو الداعية الرحمانية، وكل ما دعاك إلى شيء من لذات هذا العالم وخيراته، فهو الداعية الشيطانية. إلا أن ههنا مغلطة يجب التنبه لها. فإنه ربما ظن في الفعل في أول الأمر أنه من الداعية الرحمانية، ولا يكون كذلك، بل يكون من الداعية الشيطانية. وربما كان بالضد منه. ومثاله: أن من واظب على العلوم الحقيقية والزهد الخالص في الدنيا، فهذا قد يظن به أنه داعية رحمانية، لكن قد لا يكون الأمر كذلك، وذلك إذا كان مقصوده من ذلك العلم المباهاة على الأقران، وطلب الرئاسة في عالم الجسمانيات، ومن واظب على ترك الالتفات إلى الأمور المعتبرة في العرف والعادة، فهذا قد يظن به أنه داعية شيطانية. لكن قد لا يكون كذلك. إذا كان مقصوده منه: فطم النفس عن الالتفات إلى هذه الدنيا وطيباتها. فيجب في كل فعل في القلب ميل إليه أن ينظر فيه، فإن كان المقصود الأخير منه: التوجه إلى عالم الغيب فهو الداعية الرحمانية، وإن كان المقصود منه رعاية مصلحة من مصالح هذا العالم الجسماني، فهو الداعية الشيطانية. وهذه أبحاث عميقة، ينتفع بها في الدين والدنيا. ونسأل الله التوفيق والخير والرحمة والكرم والنعمة.
And the second branch: It is that the people of unveilings (aṣḥāb al-mukāshafāt) have disagreed in mentioning the precise distinction between satanic thoughts (al-khawāṭir al-shayṭāniyya) and divine thoughts (al-khawāṭir al-raḥmāniyya), and their words have become lengthy in that. And I say: We have explained that the intellectual felicities related to the world of separate beings (ʿālam al-mufāraqāt) are more complete and better than the felicities related to the world of corporeals (ʿālam al-jismāniyyāt). So everything that calls you to something of spiritualities (al-rūḥāniyyāt) is the divine caller (al-dāʿiya al-raḥmāniyya), and everything that calls you to something of the pleasures of this world and its goods is the satanic caller (al-dāʿiya al-shayṭāniyya). Except that there is a fallacy (mughlaṭa) that one must be alert to. For perhaps one might think at first of an act that it is from the divine caller, but it is not so; rather it is from the satanic caller. And perhaps it is the opposite. Its example is: whoever perseveres in the true sciences and pure asceticism in this world, this might be thought to be a divine caller, but it may not be so, if his purpose from that knowledge is boasting over peers and seeking leadership in the world of corporeals. And whoever perseveres in neglecting attention to matters considered in custom and habit, this might be thought to be a satanic caller. But it may not be so, if his purpose from it is weaning the soul from attention to this world and its pleasures. So it is necessary in every act for which there is an inclination in the heart to look into it: if the ultimate purpose from it is directing oneself to the world of the unseen (ʿālam al-ghayb), then it is the divine caller; and if the purpose from it is taking care of some interest of this corporeal world, then it is the satanic caller. And these are deep investigations, beneficial in religion and this world. And we ask God for success, goodness, mercy, generosity, and grace.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is the second subsidiary branch (al-farʿ al-thānī). The author first notes that experts in mystical unveiling (mukāshafāt) have disagreed on a precise criterion to distinguish satanic thoughts (khawāṭir shayṭāniyya) from divine thoughts (khawāṭir raḥmāniyya). He then provides his own criterion. He reiterates that intellectual/spiritual felicities (saʿādāt ʿaqliyya) related to the world of separate beings (al-mufāraqāt) are superior to corporeal ones. On that basis, any thought that calls you to spiritual matters is divine, and any that calls you to worldly pleasures or goods is satanic. However, he warns of a common fallacy (mughlaṭa): the initial appearance of an act may be deceptive. For instance, one who constantly pursues true sciences and asceticism might seem to be following divine call, but if his intention is boasting or seeking worldly leadership, it is actually satanic. Conversely, someone who neglects social customs might appear satanic, but if his aim is to detach the soul from worldly attachments, it is divine. The ultimate test is the final intention (al-maqṣūd al-ākhir): if it is directed toward the unseen world (ʿālam al-ghayb), it is divine; if it is aimed at some worldly interest, it is satanic. The author concludes that these are profound inquiries beneficial in both religion and worldly life, and ends with a supplication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person can judge a thought as divine or satanic based solely on the outward action or apparent piety.
Correction: The final intention (al-maqṣūd al-ākhir) is the true determinant; an outwardly pious act may be satanic if its aim is worldly gain, and an outwardly negligent act may be divine if its aim is detachment from the world.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the ultimate criterion for distinguishing a divine thought from a satanic thought?
A) The apparent nature of the act
B) The amount of effort involved
C) The ultimate purpose of the act (directing to the unseen or to worldly interest)
D) The opinion of spiritual masters

Key Takeaway
The ultimate purpose (maqṣūd) of an inclination, not its external form, decides whether it is from divine or satanic source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an act you perform regularly that appears religious or spiritual. What is the ultimate intention behind it? Is it truly directed towards the unseen world, or is there a hidden desire for recognition or worldly benefit? Be honest with yourself and write down what you uncover.
?
The author warns that even asceticism and pursuit of knowledge can be satanic if the intention is boasting. When you engage in acts of worship or study, do you ever catch yourself seeking praise from others? How can you train yourself to purify your intention in those moments?
?
Consider a time when you neglected social customs or expectations for the sake of religion. Was your intention truly to wean your soul from this world, or was there a degree of pride or rebellion? What does this lesson teach you about examining your motives more deeply before acting?

Distinguishing Divine from Satanic Inspirations
The author states the criterion for distinguishing divine from satanic inspirations and warns against a common fallacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the criterion for distinguishing divine inspirations from satanic ones, and why the ultimate purpose of an inclination is the decisive factor rather than its outward appearance.

Core Idea
The determining factor between divine and satanic inspiration is the ultimate purpose: orientation to the unseen or to worldly gain.

In this passage, the author addresses a common confusion among spiritual seekers: how to tell whether an inner call is from God or from Satan. He provides a simple rule based on the object of the call, but then warns of a subtle fallacy that can mislead even experienced individuals. By examining the ultimate purpose behind an action, one can reliably identify its true nature.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial rule
The author begins by stating a simple principle: anything that calls you to spiritual things (al-rūḥāniyyāt) is divine, and anything that calls you to worldly pleasures is satanic. This is based on the superiority of intellectual happinesses.
This rule is derived from the earlier discussion on happiness.
2
Recognize the fallacy
The author warns of a fallacy (maghlaṭa): an action that appears to be a divine call may actually be satanic if the underlying intention is worldly, and vice versa.
Outward appearance alone is not sufficient to judge.
3
Study the examples
Two examples illustrate the fallacy: pursuing knowledge for boasting (seemingly divine, actually satanic) and abandoning social conventions for detachment (seemingly satanic, actually divine).
Focus on the intention behind each act.
4
Learn the refined criterion
The ultimate purpose (al-maqṣūd al-ākhir) is the decisive factor: if it is orientation toward the unseen world (ālam al-ghayb), it is divine; if it is to serve a worldly interest, it is satanic.
This criterion applies to every inclination in the heart.

ذكره ما فيه من اللذات والراحات بما يميل طبعه إليه . وإذا أراد الملك منعه عنه ذكره ما فيه من المكروهات التي ينفر طبعه عنها والفرع الثاني : وهو أن أصحاب المكاشفات اضطربوا في ذكر الفرق المضبوط بين الخواطر الشيطانية ، وبين الخواطر الرحمانية ، وطالت كلماتهم فيه . وأنا أقول : إنا قد بينا : أن السعادات العقلية المتعلقة بعالم المفارقات أكمل وأفضل من السعادات المتعلقة بعالم الجسمانيات . فكل ما دعاك إلى شيء من الروحانيات ، فهو الداعية الرحمانية، وكل ما دعاك إلى شيء من لذات هذا العالم وخيراته ، فهو الداعية الشيطانية . إلا أن ههنا مغلطة يجب التنبه لها . فإنه ربما ظن في الفعل في أول الأمر أنه من الداعية الرحمانية ، ولا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون من الداعية الشيطانية. وربما كان بالضد منه . ومثاله : أن من واظب على العلوم الحقيقية والزهد الخالص في الدنيا ، فهذا قد يظن به أنه داعية رحمانية ، لكن قد لا يكون الأمر كذلك ، وذلك إذا كان مقصوده من ذلك العلم المباهاة على الأقران ، وطلب الرئاسة في عالم الجسمانيات ، ومن واظب على ترك الالتفات إلى الأمور المعتبرة في العرف والعادة ، فهذا قد يظن به أنه داعية شيطانية . لكن قد لا يكون كذلك. إذا كان مقصوده منه : فطم النفس عن الالتفات إلى هذه الدنيا وطيباتها فيجب في كل فعل في القلب ميل إليه أن ينظر فيه فإن كان المقصود الأخير منه : التوجه إلى عالم الغيب فهو الداعية الرحمانية ، وإن كان المقصود منه رعاية مصلحة من مصالح هذا العالم الجسماني ، فهو الداعية الشيطانية . وهذه أبحاث عميقة ، ينتفع بها في الدين والدنيا . ونسأل الله التوفيق والخير والرحمة والكرم والنعمة .
He mentioned in it what is in it of pleasures and comforts that incline one's nature towards it. And when the angel wants to prevent him from it, he mentions in it what is in it of disliked things that his nature recoils from. The second branch: that the people of unveilings (ashāb al-mukāshafāt) have disagreed in mentioning the precise distinction between satanic inspirations (al-khawāṭir al-shayṭāniyya) and divine inspirations (al-khawāṭir al-raḥmāniyya), and their words have become lengthy concerning it. And I say: we have already explained that the intellectual happinesses (al-saʿādāt al-ʿaqliyya) pertaining to the world of separate entities (ʿālam al-mufāraqāt) are more complete and more excellent than the happinesses pertaining to the world of corporeal things (ʿālam al-jismāniyyāt). So everything that calls you to something of spiritual things (al-rūḥāniyyāt) – it is the divine call (al-dāʿiya al-raḥmāniyya); and everything that calls you to something of the pleasures and goods of this world – it is the satanic call (al-dāʿiya al-shayṭāniyya). Except that here there is a fallacy (maghlaṭa) that must be alerted to. For perhaps an act is thought at first to be from the divine call, but it is not so; rather it is from the satanic call. And perhaps it is the opposite. An example: one who perseveres in the true sciences and pure asceticism in this world – this might be thought to be a divine call, but it may not be so; that is when his purpose in that knowledge is boasting over peers and seeking leadership in the world of corporeal things. And one who perseveres in disregarding matters that are regarded in custom and habit – this might be thought to be a satanic call, but it may not be so; if his purpose in it is to wean the soul from paying attention to this world and its delights. So it is necessary in every act that has an inclination in the heart to examine it: if its ultimate purpose is orientation towards the unseen world (ālam al-ghayb), then it is the divine call; and if its purpose is to take care of some interest of this corporeal world, then it is the satanic call. And these are deep researches, beneficial in religion and in the world. And we ask God for success, goodness, mercy, generosity, and blessing.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is an argumentative clarification aimed at establishing a definitive criterion for distinguishing between divine inspirations (khawāṭir raḥmāniyya) and satanic inspirations (khawāṭir shayṭāniyya). The author first sets up the context: the people of mystical unveiling have disagreed and spoken at length on this matter. He then presents his own solution based on a previously established principle—that intellectual happinesses related to the world of separate immaterial entities (ʿālam al-mufāraqāt) are superior to those related to the corporeal world. From this he derives a simple rule: anything that calls you toward spiritual things is divine, and anything that calls you toward worldly pleasures is satanic. However, he immediately introduces a crucial caveat: there is a fallacy (maghlaṭa) that must be recognized—outward appearances can be deceptive. An action that initially seems to be a divine call might actually be satanic if the underlying intention is worldly gain, such as seeking prestige through knowledge. Conversely, an action that appears satanic, like abandoning social conventions, might actually be divine if the intention is to detach the soul from worldly attachments. He therefore refines the criterion: the determining factor is the ultimate purpose (al-maqṣūd al-ākhir) of the inclination in the heart. If it is oriented toward the unseen world (ālam al-ghayb), it is divine; if it is aimed at some worldly interest, it is satanic. The author concludes by emphasizing the profundity of these investigations and their practical benefit for both religion and worldly life, ending with a supplication for divine assistance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the outward form of an action (e.g., pursuing knowledge or asceticism) determines whether it is a divine inspiration.
Correction: The true determinant is the ultimate purpose: if it is for worldly gain (like boasting or seeking leadership), it is satanic even if it looks spiritual; if it is for detachment from the world, it is divine even if it looks antisocial.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the ultimate criterion for determining whether an inner inclination is a divine inspiration?
A) Whether it leads to intellectual happiness
B) Whether its ultimate purpose is oriented toward the unseen world (ālam al-ghayb) rather than a worldly interest
C) Whether it involves perseverance in true sciences and asceticism
D) Whether the majority of spiritual masters agree on it

Key Takeaway
The decisive factor in discerning inspirations is the ultimate purpose (al-maqṣūd al-ākhir): if it is oriented toward the unseen world, it is divine; if toward worldly interests, it is satanic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in religious learning or practice, what is your ultimate purpose? Is it truly to draw closer to the unseen world, or is there a hidden desire for recognition or social standing? Test yourself by asking: would you continue this act if no one else ever knew about it?
?
Think of a recent inclination in your heart that you assumed was spiritual. Was it truly oriented toward the unseen, or did it serve some worldly interest of yours? Be honest: what is the 'last purpose' (al-maqṣūd al-ākhir) of your most cherished religious practices?
?
Consider an action that appears outwardly worldly or socially unconventional that you have avoided because it seemed 'satanic.' Could it actually be a divine call to detach your soul? What is the intention behind your avoidance?

The Hadith Evidence for Celestial Life
The author uses a hadith to argue for celestial life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of the sun's prostration implies that the sun must be a living, knowing, and obedient being.

Core Idea
Prostration requires knowledge, so the sun must be alive and knowing.

The author begins by addressing the people of apparent meaning (ahl al-ẓāhir) who find the idea of celestial life far-fetched. He points out that they themselves transmit a hadith that logically requires the sun to have life, power, and knowledge. This lesson unpacks that hadith and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author builds his argument step by step.
The passage is from the beginning until 'the many verses of the Quran indicate that the matter is so.'
2
Identify the key claim
The people of apparent meaning find the claim far-fetched, but the author says their own hadith supports it. Find the hadith and the logical deduction.
3
Understand the deduction
The hadith says the sun prostrates. Prostration requires knowledge of the Lord. Knowledge requires life, power, and knowledge. Therefore, the sun must be a living obedient being.
This is a syllogistic argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the sun is a 'ḥayawān muṭīʿ' (an obedient animal). Ask yourself: what does this imply about the nature of celestial bodies?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أن أهل الظاهر إذا سمعوا هذا الكلام استبعدوه . وهذا الاستبعاد مستبعد منهم جداً . وذلك لأنهم يروون خبراً عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، أنه قال : « إن الشمس عند الغروب ، يذهب بها ، إلى ما تحت العرش ، وعند الطلوع تسجد الله تعالى سجدة ، ثم تطلع ، ومعلوم أن السجود لا يصح منها إلا إذا كانت عارفة بربها. وذلك يقتضي إثبات الحياة والقدرة والعلم ، فوجب بمقتضى هذا الخبر : كون الشمس حيواناً مطيعاً لله عز وجل . وسنذكر في فصل مفرد : أن الآيات الكثيرة من القرآن تدل على أن الأمر كذلك . إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : اختلف أهل البحث والنظر فيه : أما الفلاسفة : فقد أطبقوا على أن الأفلاك والكواكب : أحياء عاقلة (1) إن هذا الخبر تصوير لحالة خضوعها لله تعالى. ولا يدل على أن الأفلاك والكواكب : أحياء ناطقة . وإنما يدل على ما يدل عليه قوله تعالى : ( فقال لها وللأرض : اثنيا طوعاً أو كرهاً قالتا : أتينا طائعين ) [فصلت ١١ ] وقول الله تعالى لهما فيه وجهان . أحدهما : أنه قول تكلم . والثاني : أنها قدرة منه ظهرت لهما ، فقامت القدرة مقام الكلام في بلوغ المراد . وقالتا : أتينا طائعين ، فيه أيضاً وجهان . أحدهما : أنه ظهور الطاعة منهما حيث انقادا وأجابا ، فقام مقام قولهما . والثاني : بل خلق الله فيهما الكلام ، فتكلمنا كما أراد تعالى [ انظر تفسير القرطبي ] والصحيح : أن نقول : ليست الأفلاك والكواكب أحياء ناطقة ، لتنتفي شبه العابدين للقمر والكواكب . فإنهم يدعون أن للقمر والكواكب حياة كحياة البشر ، وإذا كانت الآيات تحتمل أكثر من معنى ، فالمعنى المناسب لعظمة الله أحق بالقبول من غيره .
Know that the people of apparent meaning (ahl al-ẓāhir), when they hear this statement, find it far-fetched. But this far-fetchedness is far-fetched from them greatly. That is because they narrate a report from the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, that he said: 'Indeed the sun at sunset is taken to what is beneath the Throne, and at sunrise it prostrates to God Almighty a prostration, then it rises.' And it is known that prostration is not valid from it unless it is knowing of its Lord. That requires affirming life, power, and knowledge. So by the implication of this report, it is necessary that the sun is an obedient animal to God Almighty. And we will mention in a separate chapter that the many verses of the Quran indicate that the matter is so. If you know this, we say: The people of research and reflection differed concerning it: As for the philosophers, they have unanimously agreed that the heavens and the planets are living rational beings. (1) This report is a depiction of its state of submission to God Almighty. It does not indicate that the heavens and planets are speaking living beings. Rather it indicates what is indicated by His statement: 'Then He said to it and to the earth: Come willingly or unwillingly. They said: We come obediently.' (Fussilat 11). And the saying of God Almighty to them has two aspects: one is that it is a speech spoken, and the second is that it is a power from Him that appeared to them, and the power took the place of speech in achieving the desired end. And they said: 'We come obediently' also has two aspects: one is that it is the manifestation of obedience from them when they complied and responded, so it took the place of their saying; the second is that God created speech in them, so they spoke as He willed, may He be exalted. [See Tafsir al-Qurtubi.] And the correct view is that we say: The heavens and planets are not speaking living beings, so as to eliminate the doubt of the worshippers of the moon and planets. For they claim that the moon and planets have life like the life of humans. And if the verses admit of more than one meaning, then the meaning appropriate to God's majesty is more deserving of acceptance than others.
Source pages p. 336

Commentary
This passage presents an argument and its refutation concerning whether celestial bodies are living rational beings. The author begins by noting that the people of apparent meaning (ahl al-ẓāhir) find the claim far-fetched, but he considers their reaction itself far-fetched because they themselves transmit a hadith in which the sun is described as prostrating to God. The author argues that valid prostration requires knowledge of the Lord, which in turn requires life, power, and knowledge. Thus, the sun must be an obedient animal (ḥayawān muṭīʿ). He then promises a separate chapter on Quranic verses supporting this. Next, he states that people differed: the philosophers unanimously hold that the heavens and planets are living rational beings. The passage then includes a lengthy footnote (likely the author's own or a commentator's) that refutes this interpretation. The footnote argues that the hadith only depicts submission, not literal speech or rationality. It interprets Quran 41:11 similarly, offering two possible meanings: either God spoke literally, or His power took the place of speech. Similarly, the response of the heavens and earth can be understood as manifest obedience or as God creating speech in them. The footnote concludes that the correct view is to deny that celestial bodies are speaking living beings (aḥyāʾ nāṭiqa) in order to undermine the claims of moon and planet worshippers who attribute human-like life to them. When verses are ambiguous, the meaning befitting God's majesty is to be preferred. Thus, the passage both presents the evidence for celestial life and provides a theological refutation to avoid polytheistic implications.

Key Terms
أهل الظاهر
سجود
حيوان مطيع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of the sun's prostration is merely metaphorical and does not imply actual life for the sun.
Correction: The author argues that literal prostration requires knowledge, so the sun must be alive and knowing.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is required for the sun’s prostration to be valid?
A) That the sun is a celestial body
B) That the sun is knowing of its Lord
C) That the sun is made of fire
D) That the sun is at a specific angle

Key Takeaway
The hadith of the sun's prostration logically implies that the sun is a living, knowing, obedient being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a teaching of the Prophet (ﷺ) because it seemed far-fetched to your rational mind? How does this hadith challenge you to submit to divine revelation even when it contradicts your default assumption? What specific teaching are you avoiding today?
?
If the sun, a massive creation, prostrates to Allah in humble obedience with full knowledge, how does your own prayer and prostration measure up? Is your sujūd performed with the same awareness and love the sun has? What one change will you make in your next prayer to recapture that consciousness?
?
This lesson shows that from the Prophet's words, we can deduce profound attributes of unseen beings. Are you actively seeking to understand the deeper implications of the Sunnah, or do you read hadith superficially? What hadith will you study this week with the intention of deriving its deeper lessons?

Refutation: Celestial Bodies Are Not Speaking Living Beings
The author refutes the philosophical view of celestial bodies as speaking beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the claim that celestial bodies are speaking rational beings, and why the correct view denies this to avoid polytheism.

Core Idea
The hadith and Quranic verses only indicate submission, not literal speech or rationality.

The author now turns to the philosophical view that celestial bodies are living rational beings. He presents the unanimous opinion of the philosophers and then offers a detailed refutation through a footnote. The refutation interprets the hadith and Quranic verse (41:11) as merely depicting submission, not literal speech. This lesson explores that refutation and its theological motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
The philosophers unanimously hold that the heavens and planets are living rational beings. Note this as the view to be refuted.
2
Analyze the refutation of the hadith
The footnote argues that the hadith only depicts submission, not speech. Compare this with the earlier literal interpretation.
The key is the word 'submission' (khudūʿ).
3
Examine the interpretation of Quran 41:11
The verse has two aspects regarding God's command and the response. Understand both possibilities and why one is preferred.
The two aspects are: actual speech vs. power taking the place of speech.
4
Understand the theological motive
The correct view denies that celestial bodies are speaking living beings to avoid supporting moon and planet worshippers.
Polytheism is the ultimate concern.
5
Apply the principle of interpretation
When verses have multiple meanings, the one befitting God's majesty is preferred. See how this principle is used.
This is a key hermeneutical rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Universe as One Living Being: First Discussion on the Elements
Philosophers' claim, objection, and analogical answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the entire universe is a single living being, and how the presence of non-living elements does not negate that, using analogies from the human body.

Core Idea
The whole universe is one living being — just as a body has non-living parts yet lives.

This passage presents the first of two discussions in proving that the heavens and stars are alive. The philosophers argue that the entire cosmos is a single living being obedient to God. An objection arises: How can the whole be alive when it contains non-living elements? The answer uses powerful analogies from the human body to show that life can encompass non-living parts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: the claim, the objection, and the answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find where the opponent objects that the elements inside the heavens are not alive, so how can the whole be alive?
3
Understand the analogies
The answer uses two analogies: a small clod in a man's stomach, and the non-living parts (bones, humors) in a human body. See how each supports the conclusion.
The analogy of the clod shows that a tiny non-living part does not negate the life of the whole; the body analogy shows that even in a living being, many parts are not alive.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the absence of life in the elements, which are negligible compared to the heavens, does not prevent calling the world a single living being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليكن ههنا آخر كلامنا في إثبات أن السموات والكواكب : أحياء ويتفرع عليها بحثين (۱) : البحث الأول : إن الحكماء لما أقاموا الدلالة على إثبات هذا المطلوب ، قالوا : ثبت أن أكثر العالم بكليته حيوان واحد مطيع الله سبحانه وتعالى . قالوا : وليس لأحد أن يقول بأن هذه العناصر موجودة في داخل السموات ، مع أنها ليست موصوفة بالحياة . فكيف يقال : العالم كله حيوان واحد ؟ وأجابوا عنه : بأن نسبة جملة العناصر إلى تخن السموات ، أقل من نسبة المدرة الصغيرة إلى كل الأرض . ومن نسبة القطرة إلى كل البحر . ثم إنا لو قدرنا أن إنساناً ابتلع مدرة صغيرة . فوجود تلك المدرة الصغيرة في جوفه لا يمنع من القول بأن هذا الإنسان حيوان ، فكذا ههنا · بل نقول : أكثر أجزاء هذا البدن المحسوس خالي عن الحس والحركة والإدراك . فإن العظام والرباطات خالية عن هذه الحالة . والأخلاط والرطوبات خالية عن هذه الحالة . وإذا كان خلو هذه الأجرام الكثيرة ، لا يمنع من القول بأن هذه الجثة (٢) حيوان . فخلو جملة العناصر ، مع أنها بالنسبة إلى جملة الأفلاك كالعدم، بأن لا يمنع مه القول بأن جملة العالم حيوان واحد : أولى
And let this be the end of our speech in proving that the heavens and stars are living beings. And it branches into two discussions (1). The first discussion: When the philosophers established the proof for this sought proposition, they said: It is established that most of the entire world is one living being obedient to God, glorified and exalted. They said: None has the right to say that these elements exist inside the heavens while they are not described with life. So how can it be said that the whole world is one living being? And they answered: The proportion of the entirety of the elements to the thickness of the heavens is less than the proportion of a small clod to the whole earth, and less than the proportion of a drop to the whole sea. Then if we suppose that a man swallowed a small clod, the existence of that small clod in his stomach does not prevent saying that this man is a living being. Likewise here. Rather, we say: Most parts of this sensible body are devoid of sensation, movement, and perception. Indeed, bones and ligaments are devoid of this state, and humors and fluids are devoid of this state. If the absence of these many bodies does not prevent saying that this body is a living being, then the absence of the entirety of the elements, while they are relative to the entirety of the spheres like non-existence, is even more appropriate not to prevent saying that the whole world is one living being.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is the first subsidiary discussion in proving that the heavens and stars are living beings. The philosophers argue that the entire universe is a single living being obedient to God. An objection is raised: The elements (earth, water, air, fire) exist inside the heavens but are not alive, so how can the whole be called a living being? The answer: The elements are very small compared to the heavens, like a clod of dirt to the earth or a drop to the sea. If a man swallows a clod, he is still a living being. Moreover, even in a human body, many parts (bones, etc.) are devoid of sensation and movement, yet we still call it a living being. So the absence of life in the elements, which are negligible relative to the heavens, does not prevent calling the whole universe a single living being.

Key Terms
أحياء
العالم
حيوان واحد
العناصر
المدرة
البدن المحسوس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: For a whole to be alive, every part must itself be alive.
Correction: The author shows that even a human body has non-living parts (bones, humors) yet is considered alive. Therefore, the universe can be alive despite non-living elements.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to show that non-living parts do not negate the life of the whole?
A) A desert and an oasis.
B) A man who swallowed a clod of dirt.
C) A tree with dead leaves.
D) A house with empty rooms.

Key Takeaway
The universe is one living being because the non-living elements within it are like non-living parts of a human body — they do not negate the life of the whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the universe is a living being obedient to God, what does that mean for your own obedience? Are there parts of your life that are like the 'elements'—seemingly non-living spiritually—that you have ignored, thinking they don't affect your overall faith? How will you change that starting today?
?
Consider the analogy: your body is alive despite non-living parts. What 'non-living' habits, thoughts, or actions have you allowed in your life that you now realize must be brought into submission to God? What one step will you take this week to transform them?
?
The philosophers argue that the elements are virtually non-existent compared to the heavens. What spiritual 'elements' in your life—small sins, neglected prayers, worldly attachments—do you treat as insignificant? How does this lesson challenge you to see them as part of a whole that must be alive with obedience?

The Philosophers' Proof That the World Is a Single Living Being
Philosophers argue the cosmos is a single living being and answer an objection about elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the cosmos is a single living being and how they respond to the objection about inanimate elements.

Core Idea
The universe is a single living being; inanimate elements are negligible like a swallowed clod.

The philosophers argue that most of the cosmos is a single living animal. They anticipate the objection that elements (earth, water, etc.) are not alive, and they answer using analogies from the human body and a clod of earth. This lesson unpacks their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the flow: claim, objection, answer.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Identify the claim and objection
Locate the philosophers' claim that the world is a single animal, and the objection about elements lacking life.
3
Examine the two analogies
The answer uses two analogies: a swallowed clod of earth and the human body's inanimate parts. Understand how each supports the conclusion.
The second analogy is presented as even stronger ('first' / أولى).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the analogy of the human body gives additional weight to the argument.
The philosophers conclude that the whole world is a single living being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليكن ههنا آخر كلامنا في إثبات أن السموات والكواكب أحياء ويتفرع عليها بحثين البحث الأول إن الحكماء لما أقاموا الدلالة على إثبات هذا المطلوب قالوا ثبت أن أكثر العالم بكليته حيوان واحد مطيع الله سبحانه وتعالى قالوا وليس لأحد أن يقول بأن هذه العناصر موجودة في داخل السموات مع أنها ليست موصوفة بالحياة فكيف يقال العالم كله حيوان واحد وأجابوا عنه بأن نسبة جملة العناصر إلى تخن السموات أقل من نسبة المدرة الصغيرة إلى كل الأرض ومن نسبة القطرة إلى كل البحر ثم إنا لو قدرنا أن إنساناً ابتلع مدرة صغيرة فوجود تلك المدرة الصغيرة في جوفه لا يمنع من القول بأن هذا الإنسان حيوان فكذا ههنا بل نقول أكثر أجزاء هذا البدن المحسوس خالي عن الحس والحركة والإدراك فإن العظام والرباطات خالية عن هذه الحالة والأخلاط والرطوبات خالية عن هذه الحالة وإذا كان خلو هذه الأجرام الكثيرة لا يمنع من القول بأن هذه الجثة حيوان فخلو جملة العناصر مع أنها بالنسبة إلى جملة الأفلاك كالعدم بأن لا يمنع من القول بأن جملة العالم حيوان واحد أولى
Let this be the end of our speech in proving that the heavens and the planets are living. And it branches into two discussions. The first discussion: When the philosophers established the proof for this desired conclusion, they said: It is established that most of the entire world is a single animal obedient to God the Exalted. They said: It is not for anyone to say that these elements exist inside the heavens while they are not described with life; so how can it be said that the entire world is a single animal? And they answered it: Because the proportion of the totality of the elements to the body of the heavens is less than the proportion of a small clod of earth to the whole earth, and less than the proportion of a drop to the entire sea. Then, if we suppose that a person swallowed a small clod, the existence of that small clod in his insides does not prevent saying that this human is an animal, so likewise here. Rather we say: Most parts of this sensible body are devoid of sensation, movement, and perception. For bones and ligaments are devoid of this state, and humors and fluids are devoid of this state. And if the absence of these many bodies does not prevent saying that this body is an animal, then the absence of the totality of the elements—given that they are relative to the totality of the celestial spheres as if nonexistent—does not prevent saying that the entirety of the world is a single animal. That is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is the first of two discussions supporting the thesis that the heavens and planets are alive. The philosophers present a proof that most of the entire cosmos is a single living animal obedient to God. They anticipate an objection: the elements (earth, water, air, fire) exist inside the heavens yet are not described with life, so how can the whole world be a single animal? Their answer uses two analogies. First, the proportion of the elements to the celestial spheres is like that of a tiny clod of earth to the entire earth, or a drop to the entire sea. If a person swallows a tiny clod, its presence in his stomach does not prevent calling him a living being. Second, even in the human body, many parts—bones, ligaments, humors, fluids—lack sensation and motion, yet the body is still called a living animal. Therefore, the absence of life in the elements, which are negligible relative to the heavens, does not prevent calling the entire world a single living being. The argument progresses from analogy to a stronger claim (أولى).

Key Terms
أحياء
حيوان
عناصر
أفلاك
مدرة
جثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The presence of inanimate elements (earth, water, air, fire) inside the heavens disproves the life of the cosmos because they are not alive.
Correction: The philosophers argue that the elements are negligible in proportion to the celestial spheres, and that many parts of a living body (like bones) are inanimate, so this does not contradict the cosmos being a single living being.

Checkpoint
What analogy do the philosophers use to respond to the objection that elements inside the heavens lack life?
A) The analogy of a seed growing into a tree
B) The analogy of a person swallowing a small clod of earth
C) The analogy of water and fire mixing
D) The analogy of a machine operating without sensation

Key Takeaway
The philosophers defend the unity and life of the cosmos by analogy with the human body, showing that inanimate parts do not negate the life of the whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly see the universe as alive and obedient to God, or do you treat it as a dead machine? What would change in your daily attitude if you believed the stars themselves are living beings submitting to God? Commit today to look at the night sky with reverence and gratitude.
?
Your own body contains countless parts that have no awareness—bones, fluids, tissues. Yet you are alive. How does this fact point to the reality that life is a gift from God that cannot be reduced to mere mechanism? What specific act of gratitude can you perform today to acknowledge that gift?
?
The philosophers argue that the elements are negligible relative to the heavens. How does this perspective challenge your preoccupation with material concerns? Are you more focused on the 'clods of earth' in your life than on the celestial, spiritual realities? What one worldly attachment can you release today to realign your focus toward the eternal?

Refutation of Potentiality Theory and Psychic Perfections
Al-Razi refutes the potentiality theory and introduces the three psychic perfections as the purpose of celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi rejects the potentiality theory for celestial motion and how he introduces psychic perfections (life, perception, intellect) as the true purpose.

Core Idea
Celestial motion aims at acquiring psychic perfections, not extracting forms from potentiality.

Al-Razi presents a critical examination of a classical Aristotelian explanation for celestial motion. He finds the potentiality theory far-fetched and instead proposes that the celestial spheres move to attain psychic perfections. This lesson explores his refutation and the threefold classification of those perfections.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic and translation to understand al-Razi's dismissal of the potentiality theory and his alternative.
Notice the strong language: 'very far-fetched'.
2
Identify the two divisions
Al-Razi mentions two divisions; the first is refuted, the second is his chosen view. Understand the contrast.
3
List the three psychic perfections
From the passage, identify life, perception, and intellect, and consider how each might relate to celestial motion.
These are the candidates for the purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك محال . فلهذا السبب بقيت الأيون والأوضاع فيه بالقوة ، ولم تخرج بكليتها إلى الفعل [ فهو يتحرك لأجل استخراجها من القوة إلى الفعل ] هذا هو القول المختار عند هذين الرجلين الفاضلين ، ونقلاء عن الحكيم أرسطاطاليس ، وهو عندي بعيد جداً ، ولا يليق بالرجل العاقل الفاضل أن يميل عقله إليه . والقسم الثاني من القسمين المذكورين في التقسيم : أن يقال : إن مقصود الفلك من تلك الحركات [ استفادة ] كمالات نفسانية . واعلم : أن الكمالات النفسانية أحد أمور ثلاثة : إما الحياة ، وإما الإدراك ، وإما العقل، وكل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة محتمل ههنا .
And that is impossible. For this reason, the forms (ayun) and positions (awda') remained in it in potentiality, and did not entirely emerge into actuality [so it moves in order to extract them from potentiality to actuality]. This is the chosen opinion according to these two virtuous men, and it is transmitted from the sage Aristotle, but in my view it is very far-fetched, and it is not fitting for an intelligent virtuous man to incline his mind to it. The second of the two mentioned divisions in the classification is to say: the purpose of the celestial sphere in those movements is [to acquire] psychic perfections. And know: that psychic perfections are one of three things: either life, or perception, or intellect, and each one of these three divisions is possible here.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage begins with al-Razi's refutation of a specific theory regarding celestial motion. He dismisses the idea that the celestial sphere moves to bring 'forms' (ayun) and 'positions' (awda') from potentiality to actuality, calling it far-fetched and unworthy of an intelligent person. He then introduces the second of two main divisions: the purpose of celestial motion is the acquisition of psychic perfections (kalamāt nafsāniyya). He enumerates these perfections as three: life, perception, and intellect. Each of these is a possible candidate for the purpose of celestial motion, and the subsequent lessons will explore each in turn.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial motion's purpose is to bring forms and positions from potentiality to actuality.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that this is far-fetched; the true purpose is to acquire psychic perfections such as life, perception, or intellect.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi rejects the potentiality theory and argues that celestial motion aims at acquiring one of three psychic perfections: life, perception, or intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Al-Razi proposes that celestial motion is for acquiring psychic perfections. Do you live as if your actions have a purpose beyond the physical? What is the ultimate perfection you seek, and how does your daily pursuit reflect that?

Celestial Motion as the Preserver of Life
Al-Razi argues for life as the purpose of celestial motion, using induction and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's inductive argument that celestial motion preserves life, and the limits of his claim.

Core Idea
Celestial motion is tied to life, but the exact causal link remains unknown.

Al-Razi explores the purpose behind the celestial spheres' perpetual motion. He proposes that this motion serves to sustain life, drawing on observable correlations between motion, subtlety, and vitality. Through analogy with the heart and contrast with the earth, he builds a compelling but qualified argument.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to grasp the main claim and supporting reasoning.
Focus on the structure: claim, evidence, analogy, contrast, qualification.
2
Identify the inductive correlation
Notice al-Razi's claim that subtlety, transparency, luminosity, motion, and life are always or usually concomitant. Identify the opposite set: coarseness, stillness, death, ignorance.
This is based on empirical observation (induction).
3
Examine the heart-earth analogy
Al-Razi uses the heart as a model: it is the source of life and in perpetual motion. He analogizes the celestial sphere to the heart, and the earth to death/stillness. See how he extends this to describe celestial motion as circular and orderly.
The analogy reinforces the necessity of motion for life.
4
Note the uncertainty
At the end, al-Razi admits we do not know whether motion causes life, life causes motion, or both are effects of a separate cause. This is a key limitation.
It shows honesty about the limits of rational inquiry.
5
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this argument uses observation and analogy to support a teleological view of celestial motion, while remaining cautious about causal claims.
What does this argument achieve, and where does it fall short?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الاحتمال الأول : وهو أن يقال : المقصود من هذه الحركة إبقاء قوة الحياة . فتقريره : أن يقال : الاستقراء دل على أن اللطافة والشفافية والنورانية والحركة وقوة الحياة والعقل : أمور متلازمة . إما دائماً وإما في الأكثر . وأما الكلمة والسكون [ والموت ] والجهل . فهي أمور متلازمة . إما دائماً ، وإما في الأكثر . ألا ترى أن القلب لما كان ينبوع الحياة ، لا جرم كان دائم الحركة . فكذا ههنا الفلك لما كان ينبوع الحياة ، لا جرم كان دائم الحركة ، ولما كان حياة الفلك أكمل وأشرف من هذه الأجسام ، لا جرم كانت حركاته أشرف الحركات . وهي المستديرة الخالية عن التفاوت والاختلاف ، الموصوفة بكل ترتيب ونظام . وأما جرم الأرض . فلما كان معدن الظلمة والكثافة ، لا جرم كان معدن السكون والجمود . فالموت والسكون متلازمان ، والحياة والحركة متلازمتان. ولما كانت الحياة في عالم الأفلاك ، أبقى وأشرف ، كانت الحركات هناك أكمل وأشرف . وأما البحث عن أن الحركة الموجبة للحياة ، أو الحياة هي الموجبة للحركة ، أو هما معلولا علة منفصلة فذلك غير معلوم .
As for the first possibility: which is to say: the purpose of this motion is to preserve the power of life. Its exposition: it is said: induction indicates that subtlety, transparency, luminosity, motion, the power of life, and intellect are concomitant matters, either always or for the most part. As for coarseness, stillness, death, and ignorance, they are concomitant matters, either always or for the most part. Do you not see that the heart, because it is the source of life, is necessarily in perpetual motion? Likewise here, the celestial sphere, because it is the source of life, is necessarily in perpetual motion; and since the life of the celestial sphere is more perfect and nobler than these bodies, its motions are necessarily the noblest of motions: they are circular, free from disparity and variation, described with all order and system. As for the body of the earth, since it is the mine of darkness and density, it is necessarily the mine of stillness and stasis. Thus death and stillness are concomitant, and life and motion are concomitant. And since life in the celestial world is more enduring and noble, the motions there are more perfect and noble. As for the inquiry into whether motion necessitates life, or life necessitates motion, or both are effects of a separate cause, that is unknown.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents al-Razi's first possible explanation for celestial motion: the preservation of life. The argument is based on induction: al-Razi claims that subtle, transparent, luminous, and moving things are consistently associated with life and intellect, while coarse, still, dark things are associated with death and ignorance. He provides the analogy of the heart, which is the source of life in the body and is in constant motion, and contrasts it with the earth, which is dense and dark and remains motionless. Since the celestial sphere is more noble and enduring in life, its motion is more perfect and noble—circular and orderly. However, al-Razi acknowledges a limitation: whether motion causes life, life causes motion, or both have a separate cause is unknown. This uncertainty does not undermine the correlation but shows the limits of human knowledge regarding the precise causal relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi claims to know exactly why the celestial spheres move.
Correction: He explicitly says the causal direction is unknown; only the correlation is established.

Checkpoint

B) The earth's stillness as a source of death
C) Both A and B
D) Astronomical observations of circular motion

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that celestial motion is correlated with life, using induction and analogy, but he admits the exact causal relationship is unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Do you acknowledge that your very life is sustained by forces beyond your control, and does this realization humble your sense of self-sufficiency? What one thing will you do today to remember your dependence on Allah?
?
Al-Razi admits he does not know whether motion causes life, life causes motion, or both come from a separate cause. Is there a belief about your own life or faith that you hold onto as certain, even though you have not examined its foundations? Are you willing to admit ignorance where the sources are silent, or do you fill gaps with assumptions?
?
Al-Razi describes the earth as the 'mine of stillness and stasis' and links it to darkness and density. In your spiritual journey, are there areas where you have become 'dense' and still—refusing to move, grow, or change? Identify one area and describe a small motion you can take toward life and subtlety starting today.

Celestial Motion as the Pursuit of Infinite Knowledge
Al-Razi argues for celestial motion as knowledge acquisition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that the celestial spheres move in order to acquire infinite sciences and knowledges, analogous to human thought.

Core Idea
Celestial motion is an infinite quest for knowledge, not for extracting physical forms.

Al-Razi presents a second possible explanation for the perpetual motion of the celestial spheres: they are engaged in an endless pursuit of knowledge. Drawing an analogy to human thought, he argues that just as humans use mental movements to acquire knowledge, celestial souls use their motion to attain infinite sciences. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of al-Razi's argument. Notice how he builds from human experience to celestial activity.
2
Identify the analogy
The core of the argument is an analogy between human thought (using cerebral spirits) and celestial motion. Find the key elements that are compared.
Human thought involves moving cerebral spirits; celestial motion involves moving celestial spheres.
3
Understand the infinite regress
Al-Razi asserts that the ranks of sciences are infinite, so the pleasure and motion of acquisition must also be infinite. Why is this important?
This justifies the endless nature of celestial motion.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Razi claims this possibility is 'closer to intellects' than the alternative. What does that imply about his assessment?
He finds this explanation more plausible than the theory of extracting forms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتمال الثاني : وهو أن يقال : المقصود للأفلاك من تلك الحركات : اكتساب العلوم والمعارف، والمبالغة في تكميلها . فهذا أيضاً محتمل. وتقريره : إنا نعلم أن الإنسان حال ما يحاول تحصيل المعارف الكسبية ، فإنه يستعين بالفكر والتأمل ، وذلك لا يمكن إلا بتحريك الأرواح الدماغية على وجوه مخصوصة . وإذا كان ذلك كذلك ، لم يمتنع أيضاً أن يقال : إن مراتب العلوم غير متناهية . واللذة الحاصلة من تحصيل العلوم والمعارف : أقوى اللذات وأكمل السعادات . فهذه النفوس الفلكية مستغرقة بتحصيل هذا النوع من السعادات ، وأنه لا يمكنها تحصيل هذا المطلوب إلا بواسطة هذه الحركات المخصوصة ، فكانت مواظبة الأفلاك على هذه الحركات ، لأجل أن يتوصل بها إلى استفادة المعارف والعلوم. ولما كان لا نهاية للعلوم والمعارف ، فلا جرم لا نهاية الحركاتها ، لأجل أنه تحصل الوصلة بها ، إلى تحصيل تلك المطالب. فهذا احتمال ممكن ، وهو أقرب إلى العقول من استخراج الأيون والأوضاع .
As for the second possibility: which is to say: the purpose for the celestial spheres in those movements is the acquisition of sciences and knowledges, and the intensification in perfecting them. This is also possible. Its exposition: we know that the human being, when he attempts to acquire acquired knowledges, he relies on thought and reflection, and that is only possible through the movement of the cerebral spirits in specific ways. And if that is so, it is not impossible also to say that the ranks of sciences are infinite. And the pleasure resulting from the acquisition of sciences and knowledges is the strongest of pleasures and the most complete of happinesses. Thus these celestial souls are absorbed in acquiring this kind of happiness, and it is not possible for them to achieve this objective except by means of these specific movements. Therefore, the celestial spheres persist in these movements in order to attain thereby the acquisition of knowledges and sciences. And since there is no end to sciences and knowledges, there is necessarily no end to their movements, because the connection to achieving those objectives is obtained through them. This is a possible possibility, and it is closer to intellects than the extraction of forms (ayun) and positions (awda').
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the second possible explanation for celestial motion: the acquisition of sciences and knowledges. Al-Razi draws an analogy with human learning: when a person seeks to acquire knowledge, they use thought and reflection, which require specific movements of the cerebral spirits. Similarly, the celestial souls (nufūs falakiyya) engage in motions to acquire knowledge. Since the ranks of sciences are infinite, the pleasure and happiness derived from knowledge are the greatest, and the celestial souls are absorbed in this pursuit. Consequently, their motions are infinite because the knowledge they seek is infinite. Al-Razi concludes that this possibility is more reasonable than the earlier rejected theory of extracting forms and positions (ayun and awda').

Misconception: Celestial motion is only for extracting physical forms and positions.
Correction: Al-Razi argues it could be for acquiring infinite knowledge, which is more plausible.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi proposes that the infinite motion of the celestial spheres is best explained as an endless pursuit of knowledge, which yields the greatest pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the purpose of your existence were defined by the same principle—endless acquisition of knowledge—how would your life change? Would you reorder your priorities, your time, and your efforts to align with that purpose starting today?

The Astrological Proof for Intellectual Perfection
Al-Razi presents the astrological proof for the third possibility, citing planetary properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the astrological foundation Al-Razi presents for the third possibility of celestial motion seeking intellectual perfection.

Core Idea
Astrological properties of planets suggest they act with purpose, aiming for intellectual perfection.

Al-Razi has proposed three possibilities for the purpose of celestial motion. The third is that celestial bodies move to attain perfection in their intellect. To support this, he turns to astrological claims about planetary properties and interactions. This lesson examines the first aspect of that proof, which draws on the detailed divisions of astrological judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that Al-Razi explicitly states this possibility is 'possible' before proceeding.
The passage ends abruptly; the full argument is not complete here.
2
Identify the astrological divisions
List the specific astrological categories mentioned: exaltation (sharaf), fall (hubut), house (bayt), trine (muthallatha), bounds (hudud), faces (wujuh), lots (faraj), and the concepts of friendship and enmity among planets.
These categories are from the science of astrological judgments (ilm al-ahkam).
3
Analyze the purpose of these divisions
Consider how these intricate divisions imply a designed order, which could indicate that celestial motions are directed toward a goal, such as intellectual perfection.
4
Recognize the incomplete nature of the passage
Note that Al-Razi mentions 'its exposition is through [several] aspects' but only presents the first. The argument is cut off at the page boundary.
This is a fragment; later pages would continue with further aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتمال الثالث : وهو أن تكون حركاتها لطلب الكمال في القوة العقلية . فهذا الاحتمال ممكن . وتقريره من وجوه : الأول : وهو قول أصحاب الأحكام. وهو أنه ثبت أن للكواكب في هذه المدارات خصوصيات مختلفة. فبعض تلك البروج لها شرف ، وبعضها هبوط . وكذا القول في البيت والمثلثة والحدود والوجوه [ والفرج ] وسـائـر الأقسام المذكورة في علم الأحكام. وأيضاً : فإن الأحكاميون ذكروا أن بين تلك الكواكب صداقة وعداوة ، وأن كل واحد منها يحاول إعانة غيره على فعله تارة
As for the third possibility: which is that their movements are for seeking perfection in the intellectual faculty. This possibility is possible. Its exposition is through [several] aspects: The first: which is the opinion of the astrologers (as-hab al-ahkam). It is that it is established that for the planets in these orbits there are different particularities. Some of those zodiacal signs have exaltation (sharaf), and some have fall (hubut). Likewise for the house (bayt), the trine (muthallatha), the bounds (hudud), the faces (wujuh), and the lots [and the openings?] (al-faraj), and the rest of the divisions mentioned in the science of astrological judgments. Also: the astrologers mentioned that there exists friendship and enmity among those planets, and that each one of them attempts to assist another in its action at times...
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage introduces the third possible purpose of celestial motion: seeking perfection in the intellectual faculty. Al-Razi states that this possibility is possible and will be exposited through several aspects. Only the first aspect is presented in this passage: the opinion of the astrologers (as-hab al-ahkam). According to astrologers, celestial bodies have specific properties in their orbits: some zodiacal signs have exaltation (sharaf), some have fall (hubut), and similar distinctions exist for houses, trines, bounds, faces, lots, etc. These divisions are part of the science of astrological judgments. Additionally, astrologers claim that planets have friendship and enmity with each other, each attempting to assist or oppose others. The passage ends abruptly at the bottom of the page, leaving the argument incomplete. Al-Razi would likely continue with further proofs for this third possibility, but they are not included in the available pages.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi uses astrological categories of planetary properties and relationships as the first evidence that celestial motion is directed toward intellectual perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the celestial bodies, in their constant motion, are striving for intellectual perfection, what does that say about your own pursuit of knowledge? Are you as consistent and purposeful as the planets, or do you let distractions pull you away from seeking truth? What will you change today to align your efforts with that cosmic drive?
?
The astrologers claim that planets assist each other in their actions. In your life, who do you actively assist in their pursuit of understanding? Are there people you have neglected to support? Take a moment to commit to helping one person this week in their quest for knowledge.
?
Al-Razi presents the astrological view as a possibility, not a certainty. Do you dismiss signs of purpose in the universe as mere coincidence, or do you let them provoke you to reflect on your own purpose? What evidence of design in your life have you ignored, and what will you do about it?

Historical Views on the Number of Intellects
Survey of opinions on the number of intellects and souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various historical opinions on the number of celestial intellects and souls, from the famous ten to the claims of later scholars and Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
The number of intellects is not fixed; philosophers debated it extensively.

This lesson surveys the different views held by philosophers and astronomers regarding how many intellects and souls exist. Starting from the well-known system of ten intellects, we will see how additional arguments from sub-spheres, planets, and even Babylonian reports led thinkers to propose larger numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the different opinions presented.
Pay attention to the structure: each view is introduced with 'ومنهم من يقول' or similar.
2
Identify the famous view and the eleven-intellect view
Note that the famous view is ten intellects, but some say eleven including the Active Intellect.
3
Understand later scholarly arguments
Later scholars argue for additional intellects due to sub-spheres, planets, and Ibn Sina's uncertainties.
Ibn Sina suggests possibilities beyond the fixed stars and the Supreme Firmament.
4
Recognize the conclusion
The author concludes that restricting the number to ten or fifty is unbefitting of rigorous investigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في البحث عن فرعين من شروع القول بهذه العقول والنفوس الفرع الأول : المشهور أن عدد العقول : عشرة : العقل الأول والعقول التسعة بعدد الأفلاك . ومنهم من يقول : إنها أحد عشر . لأنه أثبت لمجموع العالم العنصري : عقلاً آخر وهو المدبر لأحوال هذا العالم ، وسماه بالعقل الفعال . وأما المتأخرون فقالوا : إنه وإن كان عدد الكرات الكلية تسعة ، إلا أن كل واحد منها ينقسم إلى عدد من الكرات، وهي الحوامل والتدويرات . ويجب أن يكون لكل واحد منها : نفس على حدة ، وعقل على حدة بالاستدارة . ومنهم من زاد فقال : الدليل الذي يدل على أن هذه الكرات الفلكية متحركة بالاستدارة، هو أيضاً يدل على أن الكواكب متحركة بالاستدارة . فوجب أن يثبت لكل واحد منها أيضاً عقل ونفس . وأيضاً : قال الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي بن سينا لم يظهر لي أن فلك الثوابت كرة واحدة ، أو كرات منطوي بعضها على بعض . فإن كان الحق : أنه كرات بحسب عدد الثوابت أو أكثر ، أو أقل ، وجب أن يثبت لكل واحد منها نفس وعقل على حدة . وأيضاً : زعم الشيخ الرئيس : أنه لا يبعد أن يقال : إنه حصل فوق فلك الثوابت ، وتحت الفلك الأعظم ، كرة أخرى . وبسبب حركاتها تتفاوت أحوال الميل فإن ثبت القول بوجود تلك الكرة ، وجب أن يثبت لها نفس وعقل على حدة . وحكى أبو الوفاء البوزجاني في مجسطيه : أن قدماء البابليين زعموا : أن القطبين يحصل لهما ارتفاع وانخفاض في كل كذا وكذا من السنين . فإن ثبت ذلك ، وجب أن يثبت لتلك الكرة أيضاً : نفس وعقل على حدة . وإذا عرفت هذا ، فقد ظهر لك أن حصر عدد العقول والنفوس في العشرة أو في الخمسين : قول لا يليق بأهل التحقيق بل نقول : الاحتمالات بعد ما ذكرناه باقية من وجوه : الأول : إن الحال فيما وراء الفلك الأعظم غير معلوم . فلعله أحاط به كرات كثيرة ، بعضها محيط بالبعض . وأحوالها غير معلومة لنا . وبهذا التقدير ففي العقول والنفوس كثرة . والثاني : إنا قد ذكرنا أنه لا يمتنع في العقل وجود فلك عظيم الثخن ، ويكون هذا الفلك الأعظم الذي نعرفه نحن ، موجوداً في ثخن تلك الكرة . ويحصل أيضاً في ثخنه ألف ألف كرة ، مثل هذا الفلك الأعظم . وبهذا التقدير ففي العقول والنفوس كثرة . الثالث : إنا ذكرنا : أنه حصل خارج العالم خلاء لا نهاية له ، ولم يدل دليل خيالي فضلاً عن دليل يقيني ، على أن العالم واحد . وبتقدير أن تكون
Chapter Six. On Investigating Two Branches from the Beginning of the Statement Concerning These Intellects and Souls. The First Branch: The famous [view] is that the number of intellects is ten: the First Intellect and the nine intellects according to the number of the spheres. And among them is one who says: they are eleven, because he affirmed for the totality of the elemental world an [additional] intellect, which is the manager of the states of this world, and he called it the Active Intellect. And as for the later scholars, they said: even though the number of total spheres is nine, each one of them divides into a number of spheres, which are the carriers and the epicycles. And it must be that for each one of them there is a separate soul and a separate intellect by rotation. And among them is one who added, saying: the proof which proves that these celestial spheres are moving by rotation also proves that the planets are moving by rotation. So it is necessary to affirm for each one of them also an intellect and a soul. And also: the Chief Master Abu Ali ibn Sina said: it has not become clear to me whether the sphere of the fixed stars is one sphere, or spheres wrapped one within another. If the truth is that it is spheres according to the number of the fixed stars, or more, or less, then it is necessary to affirm for each one of them a separate soul and intellect. And also: the Chief Master claimed: it is not unlikely that it be said: there is, above the sphere of the fixed stars and beneath the Supreme Firmament, another sphere. And due to its movements the states of inclination vary. So if the statement of the existence of that sphere is established, then it is necessary to affirm for it a soul and an intellect separately. And Abu al-Wafa' al-Buzjani reported in his Al-Majisti: that the ancient Babylonians claimed: that the poles undergo elevation and depression every so many years. If that is established, then it is necessary to affirm for that sphere also a soul and an intellect separately. And if you know this, then it has become clear to you that restricting the number of intellects and souls to ten or to fifty is a statement unbefitting of people of verification. Rather we say: the probabilities after what we have mentioned remain in several respects: First: The situation beyond the Supreme Firmament is unknown. Perhaps many spheres encompass it, some surrounding others. And their states are unknown to us. And on this estimation, the intellects and souls are numerous. Second: We have mentioned that it is not impossible in intellect for there to exist a sphere of great thickness, and this Supreme Firmament that we know is located in the thickness of that sphere. And also within its thickness occur a thousand thousand spheres like this Supreme Firmament. And on this estimation, the intellects and souls are numerous. Third: We have mentioned that outside the world there exists infinite void, and no proof, fanciful much less certain, has indicated that the world is one. And on the estimation that...
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage surveys various opinions on the number of celestial intellects (عقول) and souls (نفوس) in the context of Islamic Neoplatonic cosmology. It begins by stating the famous view that there are ten intellects: the First Intellect plus nine corresponding to the nine spheres. Then it mentions an alternative view that adds an eleventh intellect for the elemental world, called the Active Intellect (العقل الفعال). Next, it presents the position of later scholars who argue that since each of the nine total spheres contains multiple sub-spheres (carriers and epicycles), each of these sub-spheres requires its own soul and intellect. The author then extends this reasoning: the proof that the spheres rotate also applies to the planets, so each planet must have its own intellect and soul. Citing Ibn Sina, the passage notes uncertainty about whether the sphere of fixed stars is one or many; if many, each requires a separate intellect and soul. Ibn Sina also suggests a possible sphere above the fixed stars and below the Supreme Firmament, whose movements cause variations in inclination, again requiring a distinct intellect and soul. Finally, the ancient Babylonian claim about the oscillation of the poles implies yet another sphere with its own intellect and soul. After presenting all these arguments, the author concludes that restricting the number to ten or fifty is unsuitable for rigorous investigators. The passage then offers three additional theoretical possibilities that further multiply the number: (1) unknown spheres beyond the Supreme Firmament, (2) a thick sphere containing countless smaller spheres, and (3) the existence of infinite void and possibly multiple worlds, each requiring its own intellects and souls. The third possibility is cut off at the end of the page. Throughout, the author demonstrates that the number of intellects and souls cannot be definitively limited.

Key Terms
العقول
النفوس
العقل الأول
العقل الفعال
الكرة
الفلك
الاستدارة
فلك الثوابت
الحوامل والتدويرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The number of intellects in Islamic philosophy is fixed at ten.
Correction: This passage shows diverse opinions and open-ended possibilities, with many arguments for more than ten.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT given as a reason for increasing the number of intellects and souls beyond ten?
A) The need for each sub-sphere (carriers and epicycles) to have its own intellect and soul.
B) The planets' rotation requires separate intellects for each planet.
C) The spheres of the fixed stars are definitely multiple and each requires a separate intellect.
D) The ancient Babylonian report about the poles' movement implies an additional sphere.

Key Takeaway
The number of intellects is not definitively limited; various arguments suggest more than ten.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina admitted uncertainty about the number of spheres. When was the last time you admitted uncertainty in your religious knowledge? Could humility open you to greater truth, and what might you be missing by insisting on certainty?
?
The ancient Babylonians observed pole movements and concluded new spheres. Are you attentive to signs in creation that challenge your assumptions? How might ignoring them limit your understanding of Allah's power and wisdom?
?
Do you restrict your knowledge of Allah's creation to what is famous or widely accepted, or do you actively investigate deeper possibilities? What difference does it make for your worship and awe of the Creator?

Theoretical Possibilities for Unlimited Intellects
Presents three theoretical arguments for multiplying intellects and souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three theoretical arguments that suggest an indefinite number of intellects and souls beyond the known spheres.

Core Idea
Even beyond empirical observations, theoretical reasoning implies countless intellects.

After surveying historical opinions, the author presents three theoretical possibilities that radically expand the number of intellects and souls. These arguments draw on the unknown beyond the Supreme Firmament, the possibility of nested spheres, and the existence of infinite void and multiple worlds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the three possibilities.
The passage is enumerative; each possibility is introduced with 'الأول' etc.
2
Understand the first possibility
The first possibility: unknown spheres beyond the Supreme Firmament may exist, each requiring its own intellect and soul.
3
Understand the second possibility
The second possibility: a thick sphere could contain countless smaller spheres within it, each needing its own intellect and soul.
4
Understand the third possibility
The third possibility: infinite void exists outside the world, and the world may not be unique; multiple worlds would require multiple sets of intellects and souls.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the idea is clear.

Talisman Spirits and Cosmic Hierarchy
Razi enumerates the talisman spirit hierarchy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the talisman practitioners' belief in a complex hierarchy of spirits governing degrees, days, hours, regions, and creatures, and their justification using prophetic traditions.

Core Idea
Talisman practitioners saw spirits behind every natural phenomenon and urged worship of them.

In this passage, al-Razi details the elaborate spirit hierarchy proposed by talisman practitioners. They assigned a spirit to each of the 360 degrees of the zodiac, to each day and hour, and to every division of the earth—seas, mountains, deserts, inhabited areas—as well as to cities, plants, birds, insects, and animals. They even cited the names of angels from Islamic tradition to support their worldview. Let's explore their intricate system.

Guided Path
1
Read the talisman passage
Read the Arabic passage listing the spirits carefully. Notice how it enumerates different categories of spirits and their domains.
The passage uses a repetitive structure for each domain.
2
Identify the spirit hierarchy
List the categories: spirits of degrees, days, hours, four divisions of earth, each sea/mountain/desert/city, plants, birds, insects, beasts, and animals.
This is a hierarchical worldview where everything has a spirit manager.
3
Note the use of prophetic evidence
See how they invoke the Prophet's mention of angels (Jibril, Mika'il, 'Azra'il) to argue that their system is consistent with religion.
They co-opt Islamic angelology to legitimize their beliefs.
4
Understand their call to worship
They claim it is obligatory to build temples and worship these spirits. Note Razi's observation that most people of India follow this.
